PROLOGUE: PRELUDE

Sparks of War

He felt searingly hot air scorch his lungs, and he coughed violently from the pain and suffocation.

“Hkk!”

He looked one way, then another, panting, coughing, seeking fresh air somewhere, anywhere. But all around him the air had been scorched by flames. He pressed his sleeve to his mouth, squinting against the smell of the burning, and moved forward with all the strength he could muster.

The place looked nothing like it had before.

The cityscape, which had teemed with life-people and goods everywhere, as befitted one of the most prominent trading posts in the kingdom-was now in shambles, the work of all its people painted over with destruction.

The roads were blackened, the trees that ran along them wreathed in flames. There were collapsed buildings and people screaming…

He gritted his teeth, shutting his eyes against the stinging smoke, and pressed forward. Onward, through the battlefield.

Yes, a battlefield. One he knew, a hell he had passed through before and was now driving toward again.

He’d walked this path before and likewise trodden among the flames, blood spattered and alone.

He’d known nothing of the ways of the battlefield then, nor had he any means of surviving. He had been only a fresh young recruit, struggling to live -pathetic, helpless, weak, and frail.

He had survived his first battle, but not thanks to any hidden power within him, or some survival instinct that kicked in at the moment of greatest need, or because anyone had saved him.

He’d simply been lucky. More fortunate than the people who had died that day.

What about this time, then? Would it be the same? Would he survive thanks to his luck again?

“Hnngh!”

No. He could say with certainty: no.

This time was not like the last, not like his first battle. Luck would not be the deciding factor in whether he made it out of this nightmare alive.

Besides, his entire mindset was different this time. Back then, he’d had no reason to fight. He’d gone to the battlefield out of a shallow desire for heroism and an inability to resist group pressure.

He’d been a fool-not someone who wanted to live, but simply someone who didn’t want to die.

If he was no different now than he was then, then his feet should have been carrying him away from the city. If surviving, enduring, was his only goal, that was the direction he should have gone. And yet, he didn’t.

Instead, he headed toward the city center. That was where he would find the front lines.

A great howl resounded against the bruised and burning sky around the city. It drowned out the people’s screams, swallowed up the sound of the buildings that crumbled one after another, and, as if still unsatisfied, sought to carve itself into the very fabric of the world. It was the terrible triple roar of a black dragon.

The evil creature’s bellow was enough to freeze the hearts of all who heard it. Soldiers stopped in their tracks, fell to their knees, and dropped their swords. He pressed on regardless. He forced himself onward. Forward, always forward, clutching his shield.

And why? Because within reach of that awful dragon’s fangs and claws was his brother-in-arms.

“…Il…elm…!”

His old wounds ached. His mutilated throat could not produce his friend’s name, nor that of the woman he held dear.

Nonetheless, he called out to his brother-in-arms. Shouted for him.

His heart raged. His friend was there, in that place… In that place, the Sword Devil would be found.

The Sword Devil, Wilhelm-his brother-in-arms-his dear friend, was fighting.

“…Ait…f’… me!”

I’m coming. I’m coming now. I’ll reach you, I swear.

I won’t let you fight alone. Not now, not ever.

Bearing with him that choice, and that resolve, the man-Grimm Fauzen -proceeded through the crimson-soaked city.

He pushed himself toward the front line of the battle where the evil dragon Bargren, the Three-Headed, pitted its life against that of Wilhelm, the Sword Devil.

He headed into the deepest depths of the hell fired by Death Wish, who had stoked the flames of war that mocked the feeble struggles of one mortal man.

CHAPTER 1: ACT I

First of Fruits

1

It was a day of glorious sunlight that dappled through the trees.

The season of ice and skin-freezing cold was ending, and the time of year when the sun in the air gradually warmed had arrived. The trees and flowers would soon be flush with color, bursting forth in all their variety to make the world a riotous place.

He’d always been oblivious to such changes of the season before.

If it was hot, he would sweat. If it was cold, his fingers would go numb. He did remember resenting both of those things, for both made it harder to hold his sword. But now, it was different.

Now, he felt some pathos at the season that was departing, and anticipation of the one that was to come.

Admittedly, the reason was less than pure. For…

“Wilhelm!”

There was a woman he cherished, one who would speak his name no matter the season. And he wanted to see the look on her face as she greeted this new time of the year.

“Wilhelm!”

His name issued from her pink lips, like cherry blossom petals, and rang sweetly in his ears. Strangely, the sound of it stirred something that felt new in him no matter how many times he heard it.

It was the very thing he had felt the first time they had spoken alone together by that field of yellow flowers, and ever since…

He had been foolish then; he’d put a lid on those feelings, refused to so much as look at them. Hence, he had taken a roundabout route before he could embrace her-had taken it too many times.

He let his strength flow into his arms, as if to make up for the time he had so foolishly wasted.

Whereupon…

“Wilhelm! Are you listening to me?”

At the forceful summons, Wilhelm blinked his almond-shaped eyes.

He looked down, and there she was in his arms, her beautiful face like a flower, her head cocked to one side as she looked up at him, her eyes-blue as if they had swallowed the sky-glistening. They were wide and round and full of protest; she was kind, and this was a sign she was as angry as could be.

The question was, what had angered her-Theresia van Astrea-so much?

“What’s wrong?” Wilhelm asked.

“What a question! I knew you weren’t listening! How can we be so close, and you still don’t- What were you thinking about?!”

“What do you mean, what? You, obviously! Seriously, what’s the matter with you?” Wilhelm knitted his brow.

“Me?! Er… Oh. Oh. Of course. In that case… Well, hm…” Theresia blushed, looked at the ground, and fidgeted. But then, she looked back up at him, still flushed and deflated, and exclaimed, “That’s not what I meant! It’s wonderful you’re thinking about me, naturally. You’re my husband after all, so it only makes sense, right?”

“Sure it does. Is that all you wanted to talk about?”

“You think all I wanted to do was make sure you still love me? Well…not that that’s such a bad thing. But no… Here, let go of my arm.”

Theresia, red up to her ears, tapped Wilhelm’s powerful arm, which was wrapped around her slim waist.

His wife’s self-conscious request made him draw a breath. “Do you know your position?” he asked.

“You mean, other than Wilhelm’s wife?”

“That’s the most important one, but you have other statuses to think of.

You ought to know I’m doing this because of them. So don’t be selfish.”

“Selfish? Me? AmIselfish?!”

“Who else could I be talking to?”

“You actually said it! Now listen, Wilhelm, I know you’re really worried about me. But… But…!”

“But what? Say it with your words.”

In a vanishingly small voice, and with her eyes brimming and glistening ever brighter, Theresia said, “…At least let me go to the bathroom by myself!”

At that, Wilhelm’s eyes widened slightly. “Even I wouldn’t barge in on you in the bathroom. That’s just common sense.”

“But you’ve followed me right to the bathroom door! We’re standing in front of it!”

“I’m here just in case anything happens.”

“And when I think about all the times whennothinghappened, I think I might keel over and die on the spot!” Theresia managed to wriggle her arm free of Wilhelm’s grip, then confronted him head on, pointing a finger in his face. “Now, you listen. I’m very happy you’re so concerned for my wellbeing, but there are limits. You can’t be with me when I’m changing, and you can’t be with me when I’m in the bathroom!”

“Oh, spare me. We’ve even bathed together. What do you have left to hide?”

“That makes you sound like my dad, Wilhelm!”

“Y-your dad?! Come on, there are some things you just don’t say!” Wilhelm’s eyes narrowed just like Theresia’s. She might have said it in the heat of the moment, but he couldn’t let it go.

Theresia, however, only raised an eyebrow as if to saySee?and replied, “There are certain nonnegotiables, even between husband and wife. Now, I’m sorry…but give up on this one, Wilhelm!”

“Hrm… No, I don’t care if you say I’m like your dad. I still won’t!”

In spite of Theresia’s brilliant oratory skills, Wilhelm was prepared to endure any humiliation. Theresia was clearly moved-maybe his resolve had communicated itself to her-and the slightest bit of joy crept into her eyes, for she saw Wilhelm was prepared to put her first, even if it meant being embarrassed himself.

When he saw her reaction, Wilhelm’s warrior instinct-honed through many battles-told him this was the moment of truth. He pressed closer to her once again, moving to wrap his arms around her…

“How dare you tie Lady Theresia in knots with your foolish quibbles, you insolent cur!”

The bellowing voice struck Wilhelm and Theresia almost like a physical blow. They turned in that direction and saw two people standing in the doorway of the room in which they had been arguing. One was a goldenhaired woman, her arms crossed, glaring daggers at Wilhelm. The other, standing beside her, was a man with purple hair and a defeated smile on his face. He gave a much softer impression.

Theresia blinked at the sight of them, then let out a little “Oh!” She was looking at something in the smiling man’s hands-a notepad he used to communicate, on which there was a single line, a message that imparted a terrible realization:

“We can hear you all the way outside.”

“Wilhelm! This is allyourfault!” Theresia exclaimed, but for the life of him, Wilhelm couldn’t figure out why she shouldn’t share the blame.

2

“Wilhelm, you vile, stinking…! Just how long were you planning to jerk around Lady Theresia?”

“C-Carol, calm down. I’m okay, I’m fine! We do this every day!”

“You put my lady through thisevery day?”

This was the very definition of pouring oil on a fire. Theresia’s attempt to cool things down only fanned the flames.

Frankly, Wilhelm’s impression of the woman, Carol Remendes, was that she was always angry. She’d been shouting at him like this since the first time they met.

The place was, as usual, the Astreas’ private home in the noble quarter of the royal capital, the mansion where Wilhelm and Theresia now lived. As Wilhelm endured the hostile scowls of one of the two intruders who had broken in on what he and Theresia had thought was a private discussion between husband and wife, he shrugged toward the second intruder. “Yeesh. I can’t believe you can stand this woman.”

“Look in a mirror and say that.”

“Hmph! Grimm, what is that supposed to mean, may I ask? Do you have some kind of problem with my dear Carol?” Theresia demanded.

“It just means that when you’re in love, no amount of trouble is too much trouble.”

“I see… Yes, I can’t find anything wrong with that answer. All right, I won’t take Carol from you.”

“Thank you very much.”

The man communicating these prewritten words of gratitude to Theresia for her composed reaction was Grimm Fauzen, the lover of the lady in question. The way he looked and acted could give the sense he was soft or retiring, but in fact, he had his fair share of guts. Otherwise, he could never have been Carol’s lover-er, rather, he could never have been the vicecaptain of the kingdom’s picked squadron of knights, Zergev Squadron.

He was also a brother-in-arms of long standing to Zergev Squadron’s captain himself, Wilhelm.

Nonetheless…

“The point is, you heard what they said,” Theresia said to Wilhelm. “We need to have a serious talk before the neighborhood overhears anymore about our lives as newlyweds.”

“I don’t give a damn about the neighborhood. You’re what matters to me. If you want things to change, you’d better just hope that they hurry up and catch that shady son of a… I mean, that Stride guy.”

Theresia’s expression tightened at the mention of that name. Wilhelm gave a cluck of his tongue, upset both with the very man he had mentioned and with himself for having put that look on Theresia’s face.

It had been just the other day that Wilhelm and the others had encountered the vile man named Stride. Wilhelm and Theresia had been right in the middle of their honeymoon.

Stride, looking for any excuse for a fight, and “Eight-Arms” Kurgan, his collaborator, had given Theresia’s father, Veltol, an incurable wound in an attempt to lure Theresia into a duel. But Wilhelm had taken up the sword in her place.

The duel in the merchant city of Pictat came to be called the Silver Flower Dance, and formally speaking, Wilhelm had been the victor. But his opponents had withdrawn before the score had actually been settled, and neither he himself, nor presumably Stride and Kurgan, considered it a true victory.

“And we still don’t know where they are,” he said. “You could hardly miss those guys, but they somehow slipped through our search cordon. We can’t be too cautious.”

“They might already be back in the Empire. Since Eight-Arms was there, I think we can assume the Empire is involved, right?” said Theresia.

“I agree they must have come from Volakia. But there’s no way they ran back home. You saw his face, Theresia. You must know what I mean.”

Theresia, who had spoken more out of hope than optimism, cast her eyes to the ground at Wilhelm’s words.

Grimm and Carol looked tense as well, but it wasn’t the same. They would never understand the sheer sense of danger and caution that gripped Wilhelm unless they stood face-to-face with the evil man and looked him in the eye, as Wilhelm had done. The aura of catastrophe that emanated from him was just that great.

Maybe we should call him the walking disaster.

“He said he could still ‘play.’ Can you believe it?”

They were the final words Stride had spoken as he interceded in the duel between Wilhelm and Kurgan: that their “game” would have no end.

“He’s after the Sword Saint. He was so desperate to fight you, Theresia, that he even dragged your dad into it. Because you’re the nation’s sword.”

“Only for public-relations purposes,” she said, “I belong to you now.” She took Wilhelm’s arm; she had moved on from being the Sword Saint. She sounded less playful than like she was trying to convince herself of that fact.

It was not, in fact, public knowledge that she had abdicated her status as Sword Saint.

After all, the blessing of the Sword Saint could be obtained only by inheritance; it could be neither taken up nor set aside of the bearer’s own volition. The truth was, the blessing of the Sword Saint remained with her even now, and the Sword Saint she would be until she passed it on to the next generation.

Thus, Theresia’s name was left as a legacy not only within the kingdom itself, but in other lands as well, for all that she had done during the Demihuman War.

Grimm had come to the same conclusion as Wilhelm. “That’s why the

Empire wants Theresia.”

“Well, I won’t let them have her,” Wilhelm growled, pulling Theresia closer and placing an arm around her shoulder in his clumsy way, a bit too tightly. “I won’t let them have their way. You’re not the Sword Saint, you’re my woman.”

“Yes, I know. Oh, Wilhelm, I know that.”

His declaration was rather too impetuous to be a proper vow and rather too brusque to be a reassurance. Theresia demonstrated that she shared her husband’s love by giving him a gentle hug in return.

“Y’r ad’r’ble,” Grimm rasped at the sight. His throat had been crushed, so he now communicated chiefly in writing. For him to speak aloud was something of a sign of friendship; he knew that the others would make the effort to understand what he was trying to say and that what he wanted to convey would reach them.

“Yes…Disgustinglyadorable,” Carol said. Grimm smiled and nodded when Carol grasped his gesture of affection.

This gave Wilhelm his opening. “So you see now? As long as Stride and Eight-Arms are running loose, we can’t let down our guard. Anyone disagree?”

“I…I see. No, I suppose there’s nothing I can say to that. So I guess… you’re right?”

“Lady Theresia, please calm down,” Carol said. “This is just a ploy on Wilhelm’s part. And you! How dare you take advantage of Lady Theresia’s

disquiet and your place as her husband to get what you want!”

“What do you mean, my place as her husband? What about you? What place areyouspeaking from?”

“Please, don’t fight.”

Wilhelm and Carol glared at each other, with Theresia caught in the middle, where her love for her husband had put her. Grimm broke in with a sigh, then turned his notepad toward Wilhelm.

“I understand how you feel, but there’s a more immediate problem. The knights have work to do.”

“Work? You think that’s more important than Theresia?!”

“You’ll just wind up out of a job.”

“Grr…!”

Wilhelm crumbled when confronted with Grimm’s words, written so quickly and yet with the precision of a blade aimed at his heart. He had in fact taken temporary leave from his position to marry Theresia, but he had serious psychological resistance to the thought of actually losing his job for good.

“You sure have a way with words, nowadays,” he grumbled. Grimm dashed off: “As the vice-captain, I’d better.” Wilhelm tried to think of a comeback, but he had nothing.

“It looks likethisduel has been decided. Give it up, Wilhelm.” Carol stepped forward and stood beside Grimm, looking as triumphant as if she had won the argument herself. He desperately wished he could snap back at her, but she went on, “No one wishes for Lady Theresia’s safety as fervently as I do. Do not imagine you have to take the entire burden upon yourself.”

“Bah…” Wilhelm clucked again, feeling awkward as Carol abruptly sounded very serious. To her, too, he had no response. Grimm and Carol were truly a formidable pair.

“I gather you don’t much like it, but you’ll have to live with it. You, Grimm, and I can share the job of protecting Lady Theresia. We’ll take shifts.”

“You make it sound like I’m some sort of princess…or is that not very polite to say?” Theresia, the object of Carol’s suggestion, stuck out her tongue playfully.

There was no one there who would reprove her and tell her she wasn’t taking this seriously enough. All of them understood that this was Theresia’s way of trying to dispel the tension, an act of courtesy.

In the face of this small act of heroism, Wilhelm found he couldn’t maintain his severe demeanor.

“I never thought I’d miss Bordeaux this much…in more ways than one,” he mumbled. He noticed Grimm give a wry smile.

Bordeaux had been the captain of Zergev Squadron before Wilhelm; he now served the kingdom as an advisor, far from military duty. He had given his place to Wilhelm, who found himself far busier than he had been before- and now, those demands rankled him.

As for the other reason he missed Bordeaux so much… “Wilhelm?” Theresia asked.

“It’s nothing. Don’t worry about it… Okay, fine. I agree to standing guard in shifts, but whenever I’m at home, I’m going to stick with Theresia. As for the two of you, just stay out of the newlyweds’ hair.”

“I’ll be going to the bathroom on my own, thank you very much!” Theresia yelped. Wilhelm was less than enthused, not quite ready to concede the point yet. Grimm and Carol looked at each other again and sighed at the young couple.

3

The walking disaster named Stride, and Eight-Arms Kurgan who accompanied him: Their attempt to attack the Sword Saint Theresia van Astrea amounted to more than just an interruption of her honeymoon. This was an event so seismic that the Kingdom of Lugunica could not stand idly by.

As it happened, Lugunica had just emerged from the Demi-human War, which had lasted nearly a decade. It had looked like they were finally on the cusp of beginning to rebuild the nation in earnest when this incident occurred.

Considering Stride and Kurgan were connected to the Volakian Empire, which had butted heads with Lugunica since the founding of both nations, their intervention could easily have sent new sparks flying between the two countries. Naturally, the Empire was not eager to be drawn into a war because of the rash actions of a single individual. And so, the Kingdom had sought from the Empire official information on this man, Stride. However…

“The Empire told us they have no citizen named Stride, and as for EightArms, his whereabouts are currently unknown. What kind of fools do they take us for?!” Bordeaux Zergev bellowed, incensed at the glib response to his kingdom’s inquiries.

His fury crackled through the air of the office. Wilhelm and Grimm, who had met him upon his return to the capital, took it head-on, and their faces went stiff-not from Bordeaux’s rage, but from the answer he brought back.

“Eight-Arms is the Empire’s most powerful warrior, isn’t he? And they just…lost track of him?”

“I don’t think they’re going to cooperate. Hell, who can even say if they actually investigated Stride?” Bordeaux snarled. “Does the Empire want to start a goddamn war?!”

“All right, Bordeaux, calm down. You’re letting it run away with you.”

Just those few words from Wilhelm were enough to pull Bordeaux back from the brink. “Grrr,” he grumbled and fell silent. The giant man took several deep breaths, then dropped back into the chair he had abruptly leaped from earlier. “Sorry, I did kind of lose it there.”

“Don’t worry about it. If I’d been the envoy instead of you, I would’ve tried to cut them all down on the spot.”

“If that’s a joke, it’s not very funny.”

Wilhelm hadn’t particularly been kidding, so he could only shrug at Grimm’s reproach.

Be that as it may, this was the Empire’s answer-the one they had given to Bordeaux, who had gone to the border as an official ambassador, ready to listen to the other party’s explanation, only to be met with what amounted to mockery.

“Look at me, being talked down by Wilhelm! I’ll never be able to face Pivot again.”

“Damn right, you won’t. If he heard about this, he’d jump out of his grave in shock.”

“If that’s a joke, it’s not very funny.”

Grimm showed the same words on his notepad, but this time, Wilhelm snorted.

Pivot had been Bordeaux’s former aide-de-camp, but he had died during the civil war. He’d been Bordeaux’s personal attendant for a very long time and had also served as vice-captain in Bordeaux’s Zergev Squadron. He looked somewhat indifferent, but he knew how to take care of people; he had been a guiding light to Bordeaux and the entire unit right up until the moment of his death.

Bordeaux had proven fairly docile since Pivot’s passing, but at times, the personality that had earned him the nickname “Mad Dog” would rear its head. Indeed, at this moment of frothing rage, he looked very much like a fearsome hound.

And the troubling implication of the Empire’s answer was impossible to laugh off.

“If war is really what the Empire wants, then going after Theresia would solve two problems at once for them,” Wilhelm said.

“Making our hostility clear and taking out the Empire’s most powerful fighter, you mean? Sure, but…”

“Lugunica has the Holy Dragon,” Grimm concluded, at which Wilhelm and Bordeaux both nodded.

The Holy Dragon Volcanica was the name of the great dragon who had defeated the Witch of Jealousy and stopped her from destroying the world. When the fighting subsided, the Holy Dragon had made a pact of friendship with the Kingdom of Lugunica, vowing to ensure its prosperity and to stand against any external foes who would threaten it.

After that, Lugunica became known as the Dragonfriend Kingdom and experienced a glory no other nation could or would thwart. So long as that pact endured, the Empire’s threats could not harm the Kingdom-or so it should have been.

“But no one knows if the pact will really still be in force when the chips are down,” Wilhelm said. “I mean, look how long the Demi-human War

dragged on, and no one heard a peep from the Dragon.”

“Yeah, but that was a civil war. The Dragon only promised to dispose of enemies fromoutsideour borders. At least…that’s the kingdom’s official conclusion on the nature of the Demi-human War.”

“And what’s your personal take? Do you think the Dragon will keep its promise?”

“…”

Bordeaux’s only answer to the question was silence, but that was answer enough.

As a practical matter, hardly anyone in the kingdom believed the Dragon would miraculously show up to save the land if it was threatened by existential peril. No one could be sure whether this was due to a change of the times, the Dragon’s shifting whims, or the possibility that the Holy Dragon had died. Whatever the case, however, everyone knew the Dragon was not coming. If it were ever going to appear and save them, surely it would have done so during that grinding, tragic civil conflict.

“You think the Empire is acting so high and mighty because they believe the same thing?”

“The Demi-human War put us through the wringer, and our precious Dragon never showed up. Who can blame them for not thinking much of us? And all the time we’ve been fighting among ourselves, the Empire has been building up its strength,” Bordeaux said.

Wilhelm added, “Meanwhile, our kingdom has been babied by its pact with the Holy Dragon. Until that civil war, we had been free of conflict for centuries. Now that we’ve experienced it, a simple lack of national strength is going to be the chink in our armor. You know how they talk about being ‘boxed in’? This is what it means.”

It was a perfect storm, the ideal moment for the Empire to make its move. It was such a series of bad breaks for the kingdom that all you could do was smile.

“We’re trying to think of everything, so that we won’t be,” Bordeaux said. “I think the first step is to pound some courtesy into the Empire-teach them how to be polite and listen!”

“Say that again while you’re raising hell with your halberd in their capital city. They might actually listen to you.”

“If that’s a joke, it’s not very funny.”

With this third repetition of the same sentence, Grimm broke through the increasingly dark conversation.

Gallows humor was all well and good, but the unfortunate fact was they hadn’t obtained the information they had wanted. They had, however, discovered the hard way they were dealing with someone without a convenient soft underbelly.

“Sorry, guys. I wanted to come back with good news. I really did,” Bordeaux said.

“At least now, we’ll all be on the same page. Those Imperial slugs are no good. That’s a hundred times better than nothing.”

“You’ve gotta be careful with Lady Theresia. Grimm, help them out however you can.”

“Yethir!” Grimm exclaimed, answering Bordeaux’s show of consideration not with his notepad but with his own voice.

Wilhelm almost smiled grimly at the exchange but put a hand to his mouth to suppress it.

The threat was not diminished in the least. He had no time for silly displays under the circumstances.

“Bordeaux looked pretty down, huh?” Grimm wrote as they left

Bordeaux’s office and headed out of the castle.

“Yeah,” Wilhelm agreed, picturing how the man had looked moments earlier. “He looked so put out, I started to feel likeIhad done something wrong. And is it just me, or has that big body of his started to get a bit soft now that he doesn’t have so much time for training?”

“He’s still training, I’m pretty sure. He was just looking wilted because he was so sorry not to have anything for you.”

“Hey… It’s not his fault, either.”

Easy enough to say-but it wouldn’t have made Bordeaux feel any better. It was the way of Zergev Squadron never to begrudge the opponent’s cruelty or craft so much as they lamented their own lack of strength. Wilhelm, Grimm, and the other squad members were no exceptions to the rule.

While they might not have gotten the answers they had hoped for from Bordeaux, they weren’t going home empty-handed.

“Grimm, I have to stop by somewhere, just like I told you. You-”

“I’ll go swap with Carol. I’d like to let her get some rest.”

“Don’t go even crazier about this than me and Theresia. I mean, it is a big help, I admit…”

Those last words were a frank expression of gratitude, ones he would never have been able to say to Carol’s face. If she had to hear about them from someone other than Wilhem, well, so be it.

Grimm understood that perfectly well, so he said, “Yeh, yeh,” and nodded.

Once they were outside the castle, they parted ways. Wilhelm went not back toward his house, but into town.

It wasn’t that he was off duty today, but he had handed off most of the paperwork to his vice-captain Grimm, or Grimm’s assistant Conwood-and anyway, there was the situation with Theresia. The squad understood that and was more than helpful. Wilhelm decided to accept their decency with gratitude.

With the time thus freed up, he would do what he could…

“Here, I think this is the place,” he said. He was deep along a road tucked between the commercial and commoner-residential districts of the royal capital, a place full of shops that looked mysteriously and vaguely unsettling. He found one with the right name on the sign, a lonely looking tavern.

When he pushed open the saloon-style doors and entered, he found the interior dim and gloomy in spite of the midday sun. A man stood behind the bar, wiping a glass and looking much more pleasant than the general atmosphere.

The bartender stared at him in greeting. Wilhelm pulled in his chin in a nod, then swept the establishment with his gaze.

“Hello, hellooooo, I’m over here,” called a voice from a four-person table near the back, its owner waving at him. “I’m thriiilled to see you here even earlier than promised.”

Wilhelm’s brow knitted at the mischievous summons, but he went over obediently to the owner of the wave and sat down across from her.

The other person put her chin in her hands, giving him full view of her striking eyes, the left and right different colors.

“May I take that as a sign of how eager you are to see me? Ooh, you nauuughty man.”

“I’m newly married, and I’ll thank you not to say things that might harm my reputation.”

“My, so cold. But then, that’s part of your…flavor.” Wilhelm’s conversation partner chuckled, covering her mouth with a hand, and it was as if the voice went directly to his spine, sending a tingle down it. He crossed his arms, deflecting the tease with his stoic posture.

He wanted to be clear he was not here to flirt. The point of this exercise was to get information.

Sitting across from him, smirking as if she understood perfectly well what he thought he was communicating, was one Roswaal J. Mathers. She was a woman as alluring as a succubus, and just as dangerous, her long, indigo hair cascading down her back.

“I only wish I could deepen my relations with you…in the best pooossible sense.”

Wilhelm scowled at Roswaal, another comrade-in-arms. He’d never quite known how to handle her.

4

“I doooo wonder, if your dear wife found out we were meeting in secret like this, away from prying eyes, whatever would she think?”

“She wouldn’t think anything. Stick to the point.”

Roswaal held up her long hair and let her eyes sweep Wilhelm in a flirtatious glance, her lips curving upward. She looked at the empty glasses on the table-maybe she was already drunk-and cocked her head. “Want a drink?” she asked, calling to the bartender. The bartender was there with a glass for Wilhelm before he could say no, the vessel brimming with alcohol.

“Let’s have a toast,” Roswaal suggested. “To our first reunion in so long.

Oh, and don’t worry. This one’s on me.”

“It’s the middle of the day.”

“You mean, the time when alcohol tastes its best.”

Without so much as a hint of guilt, Roswaal lifted her glass, and Wilhelm could only sigh. It was obvious there would be no discussion if he didn’t join her in this toast, so, resigned, he clinked his glass against hers. Then-also resigned-he took the smallest sip of the drink, only to the point where he wetted his lips. Even that was enough to tell him this was no cheap booze.

“I’m lucky my family’s side business has been going so well. I just can’t get enough of these wooonderful drinks.”

“That’s not what I’m asking about. Tell me about what we came here to discuss.”

“My, my, you are iiiiimpatient. Or perhaps I could say, you’re a bit too single-minded about your wife?” Roswaal winked, leaving her blue eye open to watch Wilhelm. He was aware this was not the most laudable way to behave when seeing someone again for the first time in so long, but he was willing to put off the chitchat about their personal situations for another time.

There was a reason he was delaying his return to Theresia to speak to Roswaal. And that reason was…

“About this maaan, Stride, who you asked me about. Even with my considerable connections, I wasn’t able to get concrete details on exactly who he is,” she said.

“All that build-up, and you’ve got nothing? Surely, you didn’t call me all the way out here just to apologize for coming up empty.”

“The mingled notes of venom and disappointment in your voice-may I take that as evidence that my dear Bordeaux was no more successful than I was?”

Wilhelm went silent. She was absolutely right, and he realized he had shown his despondency without meaning to.

While Bordeaux had been off asking the Empire of Volakia about Stride’s identity as an official envoy, Wilhelm had tasked Roswaal with working the case from another angle.

Truthfully, although he hadn’tknownBordeaux would fail to get any information, he’d had an unpleasant premonition. He’d gone to Roswaal, with her inexplicably wide network of connections, as a sort of insurance policy, but now…

“Did you think if the alcohol was expensive enough, I would forget you disappointed me?” he asked.

“Ha-ha-ha! If you insist on thinking so, there’s certainly nothing I can saaaay. However, and while it’s bad enough not to be able to help you when you ask, I’m not the kind of person who enjoys being made a fool of, either.

So I’ve made a play.”

“A play?”

“I should say so. Clind?” Roswaal snapped her fingers, and the bartender cleared the glasses off the table and replaced them with some kind of package. An awfully heavy one, from the sound of it.

“What’s this? A weapon? It doesn’t look like any ordinary dagger, I’d say,” Wilhelm remarked.

“You’d say correctly. It’s akunai, a weapon suitable for throwing. It’s an implement much loved by the shinobi spies of Kararagi, to the west.”

“A shinobi tool? What’s it doing here? Hold on… You said you made some sort of play. Don’t tell me you…”

“Don’t tell you I what?”

“Used yourself as bait to provoke an enemy attack?”

Wilhelm saw Roswaal’s smile deepen and realized that what had been merely a ridiculous passing thought for him was, in fact, the truth. He wanted to put his head in his hands.

Yes, he had known Roswaal’s means of gathering information would be less than legal. But this?

“You let them come after you- What would I have said to Theresia if anything had happened?” Wilhelm demanded.

“Oooohh, my, and here I thought you were worried about me-but it was about your wife. Weeeell, newlyweds should be single-minded like that. It

hurts, but I think you’ll find me a veeeery useful woman.”

“Shut up,” Wilhelm snapped, but after a moment he asked cautiously, “Casualties?”

“Heh-heh! None at all. They weren’t very good assassins, as far as I could tell. Aaaalthough it wasn’t I who dealt with them, but Clind over there.”

Roswaal smiled and gestured at the brisk, efficient bartender. No-no, he probably wasn’t a member of the staff at all. He must be something or someone closer to Roswaal.

Noticing Wilhelm’s gaze, the man placed a hand to his chest and gave a bow. “Sir, this tavern belongs to my mistress, so you’re not wrong to imagine I am involved in this. Properly: I am Clind, butler to the House of Mathers. A pleasure.”

“A butler, huh? The butlers I know look after whatever chores their house needs done. Most of them would consider it outside their job description to fight assassins coming after their master.”

“My family haaaas many enemies, you see,” Roswaal put in. “Any butler of ours is expected to be capable of at least that much. But I assure you, search the whole nation over, aaaand you’ll find few butlers quite as capable as Clind.”

“It is my greatest pleasure to earn your praise,” Clind said without so much as the raise of an eyebrow. His every gesture was refined-he was a man who left no openings in any sense. It was easy to imagine he could just as easily outwit assassins as tend bar.

“I get it. You’re as good as she says. So, did you learn anything from the killers?”

“Sadly, they recognized they had been bested and promptly offed themselves. Poison. Nor did their personal effects give any indication as to

their identities. It was like grasping at shadows. Hazy, indistinct.”

“You both put your lives on the line, and you still came up with nothing?” Wilhelm asked.

“Oh, now, don’t be hasty. True, we didn’t get them to squeal. But there’s information to be gleaned even from the way they moved and the few

essential items they carried-even from the corpses themselves.”

Roswaal gave a wave of her hand and tapped thekunaion the table. Her fingernail made a loud scratch against the metal. Wilhelm registered it but stayed silent, implicitly encouraging her to continue.

“First was the other party’s reaction. As you suggested, I used myself as bait-by deliberately making it obvious I was sniffing around. That provoked a response. Specifically, the sending of shinobi to kill me. But there was something…a little odd.”

“Like what?”

“For people who must know that you, Master Wilhelm, as well as the kingdom’s best and brightest are on alert for them, their methods were somewhat…sloppy. Uncouth. My supposition,” Clind said.

Wilhelm considered. “You mean you don’t think Stride was behind it? Or, wait… You don’t think they were serious.”

“We know Stride’s, mmm, temperament. So one suspects the latter,” Roswaal said.

Clind nodded in agreement. Wilhelm put his hand to his chin and thought.

The fact someone had sent assassins made it seem like they weren’t pleased Roswaal was snooping around. But the assassins were sloppy, not very careful. Meaning…

“Either Stride isn’t trying to hide his tracks, or there’s someone else at work here.”

“I don’t know if this precisely supports that explanation, but there are certain things we learned from the dead shinobi themselves,” Roswaal said. “As I told you, the shinobi originate in Kararagi, where they’re known as ‘people of the shadows.’ They specialize in dirty jobs-missions that can’t be made public, matters that need to be dealt with in secret, that sort of thing.”

“You think the shinobi prove someone from Kararagi is involved?”

“A hasty conclusion, perhaps. The shinobi meeeerely originated in Kararagi. Rumor has it there’s a shinobi village in Volakia. When we removed their masks, they turned out to be of several different races, which might suggest the Empire, if anything. We were, however, able to be sure of one thing.”

Roswaal raised a single finger. After all this talk of suppositions and educated guesses, Wilhelm was taken aback to hear something was certain. His face tightened at the force of the word, and he waited to hear what she would say next.

Roswaal winked, and looking at Wilhelm with her golden eye, she said, “These people… Perhaps we could call them the Stride faction? This group has never left Lugunica. They rest concealed within the kingdom even now, working in the darkness until they can make their next move.”

Wilhelm gasped. Roswaal’s pronouncement was that much more powerful because she had insisted it was true. Itfelttrue-true enough to make him catch his breath. To make him believe. And it lined up with something Wilhelm had known somewhere in his heart.

Something he had known ever since Stride had told him with that twisted grin they weren’t done playing.

“No one would just go back home in the middle of a game,” Wilhelm reflected. “Except this guy is way worse than any bully.”

“Ah, yes. A fine addendum to our estimation of Stride. I think I’d like to keep nibbling away at this case for a while…”

“I’m the one who came to you asking. It is not my place to tell you to stop or to go on. Just be careful,” Wilhelm said.

“Naturally. And I’ll continue to leeeeave my family’s protection to Clind… Just as I did during the civil war.” Roswaal shrugged.

Wilhelm raised an eyebrow. “You know, that reminds me. I didn’t see this butler with you during the Demi-human War.”

He and Roswaal had known each other since the outbreak of the civil war, some five years past or more now. In that time, he had never seen Clind in her company. In fact, during the whole war, it had always been Carol who had been at Roswaal’s side in battle.

Carol, who had been fighting on behalf of the Sword Saint Theresia, who could not wage war.

“From what I can see, he’s at least as good as Carol, if not better,” Wilhelm said.

“I’ll refrain from responding to that for the sake of young Carol’s honor. But during the war, I was a walking state secret, trying to ferret out the plans of the Demi-human Alliance. This, of course, meant it was possible that not only I but my family might be in danger. I entrusted Clind with their protection.”

“Thankfully, one could count on one hand the number of attempts upon the estate during my mistress’s absence. Thanks to this good fortune, our time passed largely without incident, and I was able to pour my energy into caring for her son. My duty.”

“Yeah? Good, that’s good,” Wilhelm said, but then he stopped. “Hold on a second.” He squinted at Clind. He’d been about to let the man’s remarks go in one ear and out the other, but the butler had said something that caught his attention. “Just now-was that a bad joke? A little imitation of your master?”

“Hmm? I’m afraid I’m not sure what could have caused you to think that I was joking. I’m bewildered.”

“You mentioned her son. That almost makes it sound like Roswaal actually has children.”

Wilhelm was as good as announcing that the way Clind had put this was confusing. Clind, however, only cocked his head, innocent as anything. Without straightening up, he looked at Roswaal. “Accuse me of inept humor if you will, but it remains a fact that I was entrusted with the care of my mistress’s son. An act of loyalty.”

“Your master really has gotten to you…”

“Oh, come now, it’s not good to be so suspicious ofeverything,” Roswaal interrupted. “I’m sorry to tell you Clind is no different than he was the day I met him, and it’s true what he says about my son being in his charge. This time, however, I waaaas able to bring Clind along, as the boy is no longer of an age where he needs watching every hour of the day.”

Roswaal’s glass was still at her lips, and she was clearly enjoying this. Wilhelm looked at Clind, who shook his head very slowly.

Wilhelm took in both of their reactions, then tapped the scabbard at his hip. “So you’ve been chatting me up and giving me all this grief whenyouwere already married?”

“Ah, now, that’s a misunderstanding. I’m not married. I do have a child whom I bore through the pains of my own labor, but I have no husband to speak of. Hence why I’m raising the boy aloooone.”

“You’re nobility, aren’t you? Does House Mathers really accept that?”

Wilhelm was of noble stock himself, even if he had cut all ties with them long ago.

Succession in the Kingdom of Lugunica was typically hereditary, and the nobility in particular paid special attention to bloodlines. The Mathers family had been viewed as a minor house, inconsequential, before the war, but after the demi-human conflict had made everyone reconsider the importance of magic, they were no longer treated with the neglect they had endured before. And the head of the family had given birth to a child out of wedlock? How openly did she mean to flout convention?

“If aaaanything, my family welcomes this new member of our house, insofar as it means I already have a successor.”

“No one makes an issue of it? Does that mean everyone in House Mathers is like you?”

“Now, there’s a terrifying thought. You needn’t worry-it’s simply that there is no one in a position to object. My parents and siblings have already

shuffled off this mortal coil, so I can do what I like.”

Though Roswaal showed no embarrassment and indeed almost seemed proud of the proclamation, Wilhelm couldn’t hide his amazement. He’d never been aware she had a child-he’d never been aware of any of this. He had to reevaluate everything he thought he knew about her.

“Well, weeeell. Are you hurt? Sad to discover I’ve already borne another man’s child?”

“Not funny. You’ve only become even more confounding than before. You had a partner you loved, even had a child with them-so don’t go flirting with a married man.”

“You almost sound like you think it’s wrong for a widow to find another partner… Oh, maybe that’s not a nice way to put it. I know you’re just

offering a word of advice to a friend. I’ll try to bear it in mind.”

Wilhelm found himself thrown off slightly by Roswaal’s uncharacteristically serious response.

Recovering from his initial shock, he found himself beset by curiosity, feeling that he’d like to see Roswaal’s son just once-this boy Roswaal had borne and whom Clind was now raising.

“I would most like to furnish an opportunity for you to meet Master Karl,”

Clind said at that moment. “A suggestion.”

“Dammit, Roswaal, your butler’s intuition is way too sharp.”

“That’s what makes him such a treasure.”

Roswaal smiled as if Clind had suggested exactly the thing she had in mind, and Wilhelm could only sigh. He drained the rest of the alcohol in his glass in one swig, then stood up. He had every suspicion she had told him everything she was going to tell him about Stride.

“On your way back to the wife you love so much?” Roswaal asked.

“Maybe you should take a page from my book. Don’t leave your son to be looked after by your butler all the time. Hold him in your own arms once in a while,” Wilhelm shot back. Then he added, “If anything happens, I want you to let me know immediately.”

“There’s such passion in those words, they almost scorch!” Roswaal said, without taking her chin off her hands.

“I don’t quite trust your master. I’m leaving the same instruction with you,” Wilhelm said to Clind.

“Understood, sir. Understood indeed.”

The fact Wilhelm placed as much trust in a comrade-in-arms of five years’ acquaintance as he did in the butler of another family whom he had just met that day was a perfect illustration of Roswaal’s problems as a person.

“Whatever else happens, we all survived that awful civil war. Don’t do anything rash. None of us should,” Roswaal said.

“I would agree with you for once, if I thought you would take your own advice,” Wilhelm responded, and it was the truth.

In Lugunica, they had an expression describing how opaque the future was for the average person: “The Dragon only knows.”

5

Wilhelm had sought information on Stride from Bordeaux at the castle and then Roswaal at the bar, but there was no denying he had little to show for it. Nevertheless, Bordeaux had been unusually despondent, and Roswaal had been as tenacious as ever; it was clear that neither of them was about to give up the chase, and Wilhelm was glad to count them among his allies.

At the same time, however, he felt a hard and heavy lump growing in his chest. There was the Empire, which seemed so eager for war, and the walking disaster himself, who evidently had not left the kingdom’s borders even though he knew he was being pursued. And at the center of his foul plans was the wife Wilhelm adored.

Whether he liked it or not, the Sword Devil-Wilhelm van Astrea-and his sword were in a whirlpool.

“I hate always being one step behind,” he growled, ruminating angrily on how he seemed forced to be on the defensive.

Swinging his sword around had always been the only thing Wilhelm knew how to do. He might do it in different places, and he might gain some subordinates while he did so, but the fundamentals were never dulled or hidden.

He kept that knowledge close to his heart as he returned to the mansion where Theresia waited for him. Grimm had probably already told her about Bordeaux’s fruitless mission, and he wasn’t looking forward to having to add that Roswaal’s efforts had been in vain as well. Theresia wouldn’t be openly disappointed, of course-but he would know she had been hoping for more.

Wilhelm’s anxiety turned out to be for nothing, but in the worst possible way.

When he got home, he didn’t hear the expected greeting. At that, he put his hand on his sword and proceeded through the familiar hallways as

cautiously as if they were a battlefield. And then…

“So you’ve returned. What a most valorous fool you are, to make one such as I wait.”

There, where Wilhelm and his beloved wife spent happy hours together every morning and evening, he was met by someone he had not expected- but perhaps by someone he had imagined. A greeting most foul.

It was a man like the embodiment of catastrophe, standing in the open as if mocking Wilhelm’s efforts to track him down-efforts he had so far eluded.

“Stride,” Wilhelm said as he glared at him with such open hostility that it seemed his very gaze could make Stride bleed. The other man appeared unmoved by all this.

“As I said, you have made me to wait, Wilhelm van Astrea. That is your name, is it not?”

Stride was lounging on the couch there in the Astrea mansion, the very house where Wilhelm and Theresia lived, and wearing perhaps the most hideous smile ever seen on a man’s face. “I have heard that you sent your minions crawling after me but that none of you have been able to so much as step on my shadow. But I am no heartless beast. Hence, I have deigned to show myself here. Might I expect a bit of hospitality in return?”

Stride smiled, narrowing his eyes, which-despite his pronouncement- showed not a trace of compassion, and gazed at Wilhelm, who stood speechless.

He almost seemed to be celebrating the opening of a new act, the next battle between the Sword Devil and the Death Wish.

CHAPTER 2: ACT II

Harbinger of Disaster

1

That room, which should have been full of warm, fond memories, was instead suffused with the tension of a battlefield.

Wilhelm stood silently in the doorway, his blue eyes flashing with all the sharpness of a blade. But his actual sword, the one on which his hand rested, remained in its sheath for the time being.

It would have been so simple to let the bubbling hostility carry him away and simply draw his blade, but that would have been the height of foolishness. Wilhelm’s most dangerous enemy had appeared in his own home without warning, and the family members who should have been there instead were nowhere to be seen; giving in to his anger was out of the question.

First, he had to ask: “What have you done with Theresia?”

Stride sat relaxed on the couch where husband and wife would often sit side by side. He leaned against the arm rest, his chin on his hand. He looked totally at ease, totally in control, and he replied to Wilhelm’s question only with a “Hah!”

It was a laugh, but it mocked Wilhelm entirely, his frustration and his despair.

“Is that the first thing you say when you see me? Ask whether your wife is safe? You took the blade of the Sword Saint, the kingdom’s protector, on her behalf. Are you not supposed to be the knight who guards this land? You certainly don’t act like it.”

“I see you’ve learned a little something about our kingdom-unlike last time. You can enjoy the fruits of your labors in prison. Don’t make me repeat myself. Where’s Theresia?”

“Hmph! Giving me orders? The height of arrogance. Are you pushed to desperation because it was oh so hard to gain this woman for yourself? Now you can’t let her go? You suppress the desire to strike me down-a desire that burns so hot it scorches you-and ask trivial questions instead.”

“I think you’re misunderstanding something. Very badly.”

Stride made no effort to hide the contempt and mockery in his voice.

Instead, he raised an eyebrow. The walking disaster waited with real interest for what Wilhelm would say next. Wilhelm looked at him face on, took a breath, then drew the knight’s sword at his hip and pointed it at his enemy as he said, “You showed yourself here without that big lug of yours. Theresia is the one andonlyreason your head is still attached to your shoulders. Your life depends on her safety. She holds it in her hands. Don’t you forget it.”

For the first time, Stride was silent, as the wave of brazen hostility and obvious desire to kill washed over him. He was by no means cowed by Wilhelm’s display, but his silence certainly implied he felt less in control of the situation than he had before. Although it was impossible to tell at a glance, he seemed to be surprised.

Stride had, against all expectations, been caught off guard. It was the first time Wilhelm had seen him do anything quite so human.

Of course, that show of humanity lasted only an instant. Then the expression on the walking disaster’s face shifted, incongruously, to a smile. “Amusing… Most amusing, you yapping cur! Heh! Heh! So it’s your woman who holdsmylife in her hands? Only a complete fool or a true hero could say such a thing at this moment. I wonder…which are you?”

“I don’t give two shits which I am. My sword will be just as fast either way. If you don’t want to find out just how fast with your own neck…”Then let’s hurry it up, Wilhelm was obviously saying, allowing his swordsman’s aura to grow even stronger.

Which was precisely the moment when Stride said, “You and I occupy different positions, but it seems we have a similar talent for spotting opportunity.”

Wilhelm’s breath caught; Stride’s declaration brimmed with hauteur, but Wilhelm could make no response-for he had to occupy himself first and foremost with batting away the attacks that came from left and right. There was a series of screeches of metal on metal, and several objects lodged themselves in the room’s floor and walls.

Weapons of wrought black metal-kunai, of all things, just like he’d seen in the tavern. That proved he was dealing with…

“Shinobi!”

“Most perceptive.”

As Wilhelm parried another weapon, the response to his exclamation came from a dark shadow that lunged from one side of the room to the other.

The shadow hardly seemed to draw a distinction between the ground and the air, perching first on the walls and then the ceiling. It created fighting ground totally removed from that of someone like Wilhelm, who had discipled himself to the sword. This was a shinobi in their element. “Rrrruuuuaahhhh!”

Wilhelm was at risk of being led around by the nose by opponents he could hardly see, so he cut the Gordian knot, literally.

As he stepped in, the wind from his sword sent furnishings flying around the room-and sliced through the bouncing shadows’ former resting place, swept it away, and uprooted it so they would have nowhere to run. Now he had fashioned the battlefield to his advantage. Of course, his opponents had not remained still, but had alternated between blocking with and throwing thekunai. However…

“Impudent!”

“This gentleman is getting out of hand!”

If his opponents were moving to counterattack, well, that was fine by him. He met this with blows of his own. If the shinobis’ techniques could rend flesh and shatter bone, then it was simply a matter of snatching victory from them, greedily, by cleaving their innards with his own blade and cutting the souls from their bodies.

Wilhelm’s sword was like a flash of light; his target ducked the stroke, so Wilhelm launched a front kick at them. The shinobi took the blow on crossed arms and slammed back-first into the wall. Wilhelm followed up with a stab that the shinobi narrowly avoided with a twist of the hips-but the shinobi’s long scarf was too slow to escape being pinned to the wall, and suddenly, the dark shadow wasn’t so nimble.

The agile-one might almost say cheeky-shadow stopped, and finally, the true face of Wilhelm’s opponent came into view.

It was a young man with short white hair and a lean, wiry body that was the product of much training. He was clad in black down to his toes, his mouth covered with a mask. Wilhelm couldn’t help noticing that the boy was younger than he might have expected-but that would not slow his sword.

He took his blade, still lodged in the wall, and dragged it sideways, hoping to cleave his opponent in half-but just as he planted his foot firmly on the floor, he found it caught by something.

“Hngh?”

He sucked in a breath, and at that moment, he felt the air around him warp as the shinobi’s fist slammed into him. He pulled back his sword and tried to block with his scabbard, but the impact ignored his defenses, making his ribs and internal organs cry out. The tremendous force of the blow sent Wilhelm flying backwards into the opposite wall.

The punch, the product of the inhuman training that shinobi underwent, was so piercing that it not only injured Wilhelm’s body, but also disrupted the very flow of mana within him.

“Gnngh… Hngh…!”

For most warriors, mana flowed subconsciously through their bodies. To have it disrupted was akin to forgetting how to move one’s arms and legs or losing sight of how to hold one’s sword.

On the battlefield, where an instant of inattention could be deadly, the slowdown was life-threatening.

“Is that all the fight in you? I must say, I am more than a little disappointed,” the shinobi said.

As Wilhelm struggled to make his hands and feet fight for him, he was shaken by that mocking smirk. In his mind’s eye, he saw Stride grin coldly- but then, the image was replaced by Therese’s gentle smile. A fire lit within him.

“Ngghaaaaa!”

Wilhelm shoved his shoulders forward, veritably peeling himself off the wall, and met the shinobi who charged at him like a wild animal.

Thekunaicame at him as straight as an arrow, accompanied by another flying, whirling blade. Wilhelm dispatched both of them with quick movements, the most efficient possible slashes of his sword.

Even with those threats removed, however, the true weapon-the shinobi himself-was still coming on with a roar.

Wilhelm planted his feet on the floor hard enough that it seemed the floorboards might crack, ready to stop the onrushing opponent. Then he felt it again: the same sensation as before, this time wrapping itself around his ankles…

“Hrk?!”

He jerked his foot up as fast as he could, and with it came the second shinobi, who had latched onto his ankle. Wilhelm drew them out of the shadow like a fish out of the water. He didn’t know whether it was magic or sheer craft, but somehow, the shinobi had been hiding within the shadow itself. Wilhelm dragged them onto the field of battle proper, kicking them toward the onrushing first shinobi.

“Rrrruuuuaahhh!”

The first shinobi, charging ahead, and the second one, kicking backwards, collided in midair. Wilhelm launched a single, all-or-nothing strike, intending to cut down both of them at once. The force of the blow made his own arms shiver, and the two shinobi went sprawling backwards.

However…

“Pfah. Persistent bastards, aren’t you?” he growled. “I say, you are a most formidable warrior.”

“Indeed. We did not expect you to fight like this.”

Before Wilhelm stood the two shinobi. Each was bleeding profusely from an arm, one from the left, the other from the right. Still, they had managed to work together to avoid being slaughtered.

The two shinobi looked very similar. In fact, their builds and their faces were almost identical. He suspected they were twin brothers. Their coordination in battle was almost impossible to believe-unless they had been trained for it since the moment they were born.

At that point, Stride intervened.

“I see,” he said. “Among all my pawns, even you Generals are able to manage only this much.”

The deadly struggle had lasted only a matter of ten seconds or so, hardly enough to blink.

Stride had not so much as shifted from the way he was sitting when Wilhelm had walked into the room, draped across the couch as if watching a spectacle put on for his amusement. It was hard to say whether that was a display of absolute nerve or evidence his capacity to feel fear and tension had died within him…

“It is clear to me now that if I wish to check you, I will have to do it with Eight-Arms. Much as I hate to say it, you’ve done well to train yourself so far when you are not even an agent of the observers. I give you my praise.”

“Praise? You son of a… What are you even thinking?”

The table had been shattered, and the floor was littered with tableware that had tumbled from the battered shelves. And Stride gave hardly a thought to the man who had fought amid so much destruction, instead offering idle praise. Wilhelm felt something more like contempt than rage.

He’d gotten his chance to confront Stride, to speak with him-if not in the way he might’ve wished-and now, he found he could hardly make sense of their conversation.

It would have been better had he been speaking to a beast who could not understand his words. But Stride was no beast. The lack ofresponsethat Wilhelm sensed from this man filled him with a different kind of disgust altogether.

That impression was only strengthened by the dead look in Stride’s eyes.

“Your question is inescapably trivial and idiotic,” Stride said. “I have already answered it. You should know by now. Everything is for my amusement.”

“You’re seriously telling me you did all this just for some game, on a whim?”

“Don’t be silly. Why ever should amusement need be obtained by a whim? These diversions only make the heart truly dance when they are carefully devised, thoughtfully executed, and prepared with appropriate care.”

Wilhelm didn’t speak. Whatever Stride said to him, the logic was as if on the far side of a great waterfall-more than enough to convince the Sword Devil that even attempting to understand him was a fruitless task.

The shinobi, who had simply basked in the aura of battle in silence, now unexpectedly spoke up with advice for their master.

“Lord, we humbly request you not antagonize this gentleman any further.” “The injuries my elder brother and I have sustained are far from trivial.

We cannot stay long.”

In spite of their report, their wounds, which should have been lifethreatening at the very least, had already stopped bleeding. Did they have some sort of medicine they had used, or did they simply exercise that much control over their own bodies? Whatever the reason…

“Don’t think for a second any of you are going home alive,” Wilhelm said.

He could talk all he wished; Stride was not soft enough to give away whether Theresia was safe. And it would be dangerous to let the shinobi recover any more of their strength. This, Wilhelm decided, was the moment to attack.

However…

“Do you happen to remember my rings?” the walking disaster asked, even as Wilhelm began to move. The awful thing was that even if Wilhelm had felt it was the right choice to ignore him and simply cut him down, this topic would have stopped him.

Wilhelm gritted his teeth, while Stride raised his hands demonstratively. There were rings on each of his fingers-no, not quite. The little finger on his right hand was bare.

The ring that had once decorated that finger had been called “The Scarlet Pinky,” and it had bestowed the curse that had threatened the life of Veltol, Theresia’s father and Wilhelm’s father-in-law. That curse had been broken, the ring destroyed as proof of Wilhelm’s victory in the Silver Flower Dance -but Stride possessed nine other rings like it, and Wilhelm gasped at the sight of them once again.

Stride chuckled at this, and the next instant, the rings on the middle and pointer fingers of his right hand gleamed.

“The ‘Jade Pointer’ and the ‘Amber Middle,’” he said. “You may consider them kindred of the Scarlet Pinky.”

“What? You think you’re going to curse me this time with those rings?”

“If I could inflict hexes left and right, well, that might be amusing in its own way. But there is a proper order even to the Ten Proud Commandments. I have no plans to put such a curse on the likes of you. I am merely laying down the foundations for the day to come.”

“What are you-”

Talking about, Wilhelm was going to say, but he never got the chance. The two shinobi, who had remained silent, moved before he could finish his sentence, once again attacking Wilhelm in unison.

“Shit!” he howled, turning toward the shinobi, who came at him withkunaiin hand. He deflected their initial, mirrored attacks as forcefully as he could-and then, he noticed… One of the shinobi had jade-colored eyes, and the other amber, shining with the same colors as Stride’s rings.

With the Scarlet Pinky, Stride had put a curse on Veltol that placed a limit on his very life. He must be controlling these shinobi with similar spells from his rings to make them do what he wished.

“They aren’t actually your comrades?!” Wilhelm cried.

“Have you never played shatranj? I am not so perverse as to think of my pawns on the board as ‘comrades.’ And I might add…”

“What?!”

“The woman I found in this mansion is taking a little nap upstairs.”

The moment he heard that, everything went white for Wilhelm. The strokes of his sword pushed back Stride’s enthralled shinobi. Wilhelm dealt with his enemy’s “pawns”-who fought in spite of their wounds, and possibly against their will-in a single blow, and then the Sword Devil charged at Stride, his blade gleaming as it sliced through the air, toward Stride’s vile face…

An instance before the silver streak sliced through the walking disaster, Wilhelm’s eyes went wide at an unexpected disruption. It took the form of a pocket mirror that had suddenly appeared in Stride’s palm. The man opened the lid and turned it toward Wilhelm-who saw in it the face of a woman he didn’t recognize.

“Do it,” Stride commanded.

“Yes, my lord,” answered the woman in the mirror. She had hair the color of ash, and her eyes were gently closed; at Stride’s word, they slowly opened. Wilhelm met her eyes-in which there was a twisted pattern, like a spider’s web.

Wilhelm began to feel an all-encompassing chill, as if he were caught in a web just like the pattern…

“My lord!”

Wilhelm fought through the terror, lashed out with his sword, which smashed the mirror with the woman. It failed, however, to reach the walking disaster beyond and take his life-Stride was saved by one of his shinobi, who grabbed him by the collar and dragged him backward. The mirror dropped and shattered at his feet.

“Your jest goes too far, sire. Let this be at an end.”

At these words of advice from the shinobi who had saved him from death by a hair’s breadth, the walking disaster merely sniffed. Then he fixed his eyes on Wilhelm, who had just lost the chance of a lifetime. “My plans are in motion. You may wait with anticipation for the next opportunity.”

“W-”

Wait, Wilhelm was about to shout, but before he could get the word out, there was an explosion of white smoke that filled the room and kept him from seeing anything at all.

The other shinobi, the one who had not saved Stride’s life, had flung something against the floor to create a smoke screen. Amid the haze, Wilhelm immediately whipped up a wind with his weapon, but he was never going to catch his prey that way.

They had bested him, completely and utterly. Worse still, if what Stride had said was true, then before Wilhelm had even gotten home, Stride had already laid hands on Theresia…

“W-what’s all this?! What in the world is going on? Wilhelm! Wilhelm, are you there?!” “Theresia?!”

Having just been grinding his teeth with fear and rage, Wilhelm was taken aback by the voice he heard on the other side of the smoke. He rushed toward it, making his way out of the room and toward a figure near the mansion’s entranceway-two figures, in fact. They turned out to be Theresia and Grimm, both of them wide-eyed.

“Theresia!” The moment he saw her standing there, Wilhelm ran to her and enfolded her in his arms. Nothing else mattered to him. She yelped at his sudden embrace but hesitantly hugged him back.

“I-I’m okay, Wilhelm, so just try to calm down. Um… What happened here?”

“That’s what I’d like to know. Why are you coming in from outside?”

“What do you mean, why? Grimm and I went shopping. But then, we walked through the door and saw smoke everywhere…”

Despite her evident confusion, Theresia answered Wilhelm’s question in full. Wilhelm glanced at Grimm, who nodded firmly as if to say he had nothing to add.

It turned out Stride and his minions had not turned their proverbial fangs on Theresia after all. The knowledge brought Wilhelm a deep relief.

“But… Then, wait,” he said. “If he wasn’t talking about you, then who was that woman he mentioned?”

Maybe it had simply been a bluff to throw Wilhelm off his guard. That would be the best they could hope for. But that seemed a naively optimistic interpretation when it came to that walking disaster. Wilhelm went pale and shouted, “The second floor!” “Wha…?” asked a startled Theresia.

“!”

It was Grimm who sensed the tension and responded. Like the vicecaptain that he was, he answered his captain immediately, sprinting for the stairs to the second floor like a shot. Wilhelm was close behind him, dragging Theresia along by the arm.

There, in Wilhelm and Theresia’s bedchamber, directly above the living room, they found…

“Argh!”

“Carol?!”

Their exclamations were directed at the center of the room, where a woman was seated in a chair-Carol. She, not Theresia, was the woman who had been left here at the house.

She sat upright, her eyes closed as if sleeping. Her arms and legs appeared free, not bound, and she had no obvious external injuries.

Knowing Carol, it was impossible to believe that an encounter with Stride and his shinobi had left her untouched.

“Aaaool! Aaaolll!” Grimm howled, shaking her slim shoulders in an effort to wake her up. Theresia pulled him back and took Carol’s hand, her face pale.

“No, Grimm, don’t make this any worse! We have to start by getting her to the bed…”

Wilhelm and Grimm looked at each other, then prepared to lift Carol and carry her-but at that moment, her long eyelashes fluttered, and from her slim lips emerged the words, “Lady…Theresia?”

Theresia’s own eyes went wide. Carol’s green eyes drifted open, and she blinked several times before really registering that Theresia was there.

“Carol? Are you all right? Do you recognize me?” Theresia asked.

Carol blinked a few more times. “I… What did I…? I remember seeing you and Grimm off…” She sounded like she was just waking up from a deep sleep. As her memories came back to her, her eyes snapped open and she said, “That’s right! I saw the shadows flicker-someone got into the house. They were…”

“He’s gone now,” Wilhelm said. “I chased him off… Or, I guess I should say, he ran away.”

“Wilhelm… I see. If you and Lady Theresia are all right, then that’s enough for me.” Carol was clearly agonized by what she remembered from the moments before she lost consciousness, but she tried to put on a brave front.

Distressed though she might have been, at least her answers were coherent. It was strange the three intruders had just left her here, but if she was safe, then that was what mattered. Or so they thought.

“Aaoll,” said Grimm, clearly relieved to see Carol safely conscious again. He’d gone around behind her to help lift her into the bed, so only now did she realize he was there. She turned and opened her mouth to speak her lover’s name, but-

“…Ah…kaah…” Her eyes went wide abruptly, and only a rasping, scratching breath escaped her lips.

Grimm paled and cried, “Aooll?! Aaaaool!”

“Wha? Carol?!” Theresia exclaimed. “What’s wrong? Fight it!”

“The hell? Her throat-”

“No, it’s not her throat,” Grimm said.

“She can’t breathe?!” Theresia cried.

Wilhelm shoved his wife aside and grabbed Carol’s arms as she scrabbled at her neck. It was terrifying-she looked like she was drowning.

The explanation soon appeared on her pale throat, clearly visible.

“A bruise…from a black hand?”

Carol’s neck was beginning to bleed from her scratching at it, but even more prominent was a dark bruise in the shape of a hand. There was, of course, no actual appendage there. Yet even at that very moment, in the present progressive, Carol was being strangled by the hand that was producing that bruise. She struggled to breathe.

The image of Stride’s mocking grin flitted through Wilhelm’s mind, almost driving him mad with rage.

Grimm, however, said, “Wilhelm!” bringing him back to himself.

Wilhelm hauled Carol out of her chair-it would be faster for him to carry her than for Grimm or Theresia to do it. “We’ve got to get her to a healer or… no! Roswaal must still be at the tavern!”

If this was part of Stride’s awful plans, then the hospital would not be able to help them, but Roswaal might. They would have to gamble, trusting that she could do something.

“Theresia! Grimm!” he shouted to his two frozen companions. “You tell the castle what’s going on! I’ll find Roswaal and-”

“Haa…gh…!Cough!”

“Wha?” Wilhelm slowed his pace just as he was about to fly from the bedroom. Carol, who had been in such desperate straits in his arms the previous instant, coughed and began sucking in air. The pressure from the invisible hand had released her neck, permitting her to breathe.

“Huff… Haah!C-cough!”

“Carol! Can you breathe again?!” Wilhelm said.

“Y-yes… Somehow, I…I can. F-for the moment…” She was still gasping, but she was free of the immediate danger of suffocation.

“C-Carol! Are you sure you’re all right?”

“Yes, Lady Theresia. I’m sorry to worry you… And you, Grimm.” She stood on her own now, though she was still using Wilhelm’s arm for support. Her eyes brimmed from the pain, but she mustered an apology; the other two shook their heads. Then Grimm reached out to touch her once more.

“Wilhelm…”

“Hold it, Grimm. Don’t touch her.” Wilhelm clapped a hand on Grimm’s wrist. He had noticed something strange.

“Wilhelm? What now?” Theresia asked, nervous.

“Her neck,” was all Wilhelm said. Theresia looked at Carol and gasped softly.

The dark impression of a hand was beginning to appear once again on Carol’s neck.

The moment Wilhelm pulled back Grimm’s arm, it began to lighten. But when Grimm took a step closer, it grew dark and threatening…

“We’re going to see Roswaal,” Wilhelm said. He already had a pretty good idea what was going on.

The four of them left the house, under a shared cloud of anxiety and fear, seeking any ray of hope. They were careful to keep as much distance as possible between Grimm and Carol.

“It’s all right, Carol. I’m sure it will be fine,” said Theresia, who was holding Carol’s hand on Grimm’s behalf and trying to reassure the servant whom she cherished like a sister. She was trying to help them all cling to hope as they ran along.

Yet they all knew that hope was feeble at best.

2

“There’s no doubt now that Stride is targeting you and your wife,” Bordeaux said, having concluded his inspection of the mansion’s upturned living room. Wilhelm felt nothing but anger at that: Why was Bordeaux wasting his time telling him things he already knew?

“Don’t glare at the young man so. You know as well as he does.”

“Don’t give me that look. I don’t need you to tell me how badly I’ve screwed this up.”

Wilhelm must have been glaring daggers at Bordeaux. At the reproof from Bordeaux-and another voice-Wilhelm massaged his eyebrows and muttered, “Sorry.” He’d only been lashing out. He felt the same anger at himself that Bordeaux did.

The fact the Astrea mansion had been attacked in the royal capital itself, in broad daylight, was a profound affront to the kingdom’s reputation.

“Unless my eyes tricked me, those shinobi with Stride moved in and out of the shadows. If they can take other people with them when they do that, then worst-case scenario, they could easily sneak into the castle.”

“We’ll take every precaution,” Bordeaux said. “But there are a lot of secrets surrounding the shinobi and their abilities. And the kingdom knows little about these secrets, the same way we were clueless about magical countermeasures at the outset of the Demi-human War.”

“We let ourselves trust the Dragon for everything, never honing our own abilities, and now the bill is coming due,” Wilhelm said.

“You’ve got us there. At the moment, a single kidnapping could shake the foundation of the entire kingdom.” Bordeaux put a large hand to his large forehead, heaved a great sigh, and looked up at the ceiling. Wilhelm looked, too, his mind traveling up toward the roof-no, toward the second floor.

In the bedchamber up there, Roswaal was examining Carol. Theresia and Grimm were with her. For the moment, Carol’s bizarre symptoms seemed to have subsided.

“How’s our young Carol doing?” Bordeaux asked.

“She’s not what you would call asleep, but she’s letting Roswaal look at her. We were just lucky this happened while she was still in the capital,”

Wilhelm said, and then he kicked the ground, infuriated by his own answer. “Dammit!”

Wilhelm hardly felt he was in any position to talk about good luck. He had seen the dark hand grasping Carol’s neck with his own eyes. Thinking of the pain Theresia and Grimm must be going through only made it worse. The two of them had gone out shopping, leaving Carol behind in the house-that was why she was suffering like this. The two of them had avoided calamity through sheer coincidence.

Wait… Was it really coincidence?

“Once you start thinking, there’s no end to it. If you come to believe you’re dealing with a Witch, with someone or something you can’t handle, then you play right into your enemy’s hands,” a familiar voice said in Wilhelm’s head.

“Believe me, I know that. That bastard is just a damned madman.”

Bordeaux placed a hand on Wilhelm’s shoulder. “Calm down, Wilhelm. Grimm’s already lost his head. We can’t have you going the same way. That would be playing into the enemy’s hands.”

“Hands this, hands that! Dammit!” He shoved Bordeaux away and spat out a breath.

It was a mistake to think that every last thing was the product of Stride’s plans and calculations. It was undeniable the walking disaster had not foreseen Theresia being out of the house at the moment of his attack. That was Grimm’s good work. Only he had predicted Stride’s assault.

Even for that supposition, however, there was no proof of it…

“Grimm’s intuition has saved our skins more than once,” Bordeaux said.

“He’s always so humble, but he actually seems aware he’s got a good gut instinct. Although it’s got to be killing him to know that it led him to go with

Theresia and leave Carol at the house.”

“Does he feel it as keenly as his pride in serving his unit?”

That brought Wilhelm up short. Grimm was an essential member of Zergev Squadron, up to and including his intuition. On some level, he could be said to stand shoulder to shoulder with Wilhelm and Bordeaux for sheer bravery, or perhaps even above them. Incapacitating Grimm, whether deliberate or not, was one of the best moves Stride could have made for himself.
