The familiar voice was right. Wilhelm didn’t want to be more afraid of Stride than was warranted, as if the man was a Witch or something. Yet the thought that even chance was on Stride’s side was not a pleasant one.

They could use a tailwind of their own to help them along.

“If y’all will pardon me for interrupting you, I’d like to drop in.”

Wilhelm turned at the unaccountably lighthearted voice from the doorway to the living room. No one was allowed in the house except members of the knight unit. It was inconceivable an outsider had gotten in.

The man who stood grinning in the doorway, though, certainly didn’t look like a knight.

“Ah, you’re here. It’s all right, let him through. I called him,” Bordeaux said.

“That’s right, our dear Bordeaux invited me, sodolet me through.” The man grinned, his spirits obviously undampened by the swords the knights had pulled on him. He walked past them as if he belonged here. Wilhelm moved to meet him but scowled mightily in doubt and confusion.

The man gave a great shrug. “C’mon, now,” he said. “Don’t tell me you’ve forgotten me? You’d have to be the least grateful guy in the world.

You got your wife back on account of my advice, right? Surely you remember ol’ Orfeu Six-Tongue.”

“I haven’t forgotten you,” Wilhelm said slowly. “Which is exactly why I’m making this face. What are you here for, you swindler?” The man acted altogether too familiar.

“Oof!” he exclaimed and smacked himself on the head. The theatrical gesture seemed very appropriate for him. He was tall and slim, with a face and voice that could be attractive to anyone, regardless of gender. He put them to good use making quite a name for himself in the kingdom: He was known as Orfeu Six-Tongue. His relationship to Wilhelm was what one might call jail buddies, as they’d spent a short stint in a prison tower together.

“Look around. This room’s nothing but men,” Wilhelm said. “There are three ladies upstairs, but they don’t have time to waste with you.”

“Ah, so cold, my friend, so cold. I guess you don’t know a woman’s heart and purse strings are never more open than when her back is against the wall.”

“Seriously, what did you come here for? They might have let you out of prison, but I see you’re still bent on fleecing women.”

“Oh, yeah, you’ve got that right. But this time, I’m doing it for the good of the state, eh?” Orfeu winked, committed to looking flippant as ever.

Wilhelm-annoyed by Orfeu’s pointless jabbering-reached out, ready to chase the man out, but Bordeaux stopped him. “Hold it,” he said. “Like I said, I called him here. Hell, you’re the one who told me he might be useful. And you were right-he’s a fast talker and thinks on his feet. He’s been a valuable asset.”

“Him? Are you insane?”

“I know you’re all in a tizzy, and you feel like you’re cornered, but that’s no way for the very guy who recommended me to talk. I may not look like it, but I’m really grateful to you and your buddy Bordeaux.”

“Something else he may not look like, but turns out to be, is a surprisingly loyal worker.”

“Ever ambiguous as to whether he’s praising someone or not. An appraisal very much to be expected of the young man.”

Orfeu didn’t look particularly gratified by Bordeaux’s description, but as for Wilhelm, he gave a sigh, then tried to let go of the contact he had had with Orfeu prior to this and face the moment at hand-to confrontwhyBordeaux had called such a quick thinker to this particular place.

“You’re not going to try to tell me you’re some kind of specialist in magic or spells, are you?” Wilhelm said. “So whatcanyou do?”

“I’ve only ever been good at one thing: talking. This tongue gets me places in the royal city-heck, the whole kingdom-that you’d never expect. Stuff people would never tell a man with an official title and a stick up his ass, they’ll spill quicker than you’d ever think if you just make friends with them.”

“Living up to your name, are you, Six-Tongue?” Wilhelm said. “And do you get anything out of all that work?”

“Oh, plenty.”

Wilhelm’s eyes went a little wider at Orfeu’s prompt and clear answer.

“But you might not like it very much,” he added. “I heard about how this guy’s little servants moved through the shadows. Which means this is no ordinary game of hide-and-seek, but I figured, they can’tlivein those shadows, can they?”

“I…suppose not?” Wilhelm said, his hand on his chin. “They can’t bethatabnormal.”

He thought back on one particular moment that stood out to him. In that final exchange, Stride had shown him a mirror-probably one of those “conversation mirrors” or whatever that allowed you to see someone with a similar mirror who was far away. Wilhelm hadn’t recognized the woman he saw there, but it revealed information that could bolster Orfeu’s inference.

“The woman wasn’t in any shadow, but was in some sort of building,” the familiar voice offered.

“If she was someone he couldn’t bring with him to the fight, he would have left her somewhere safe. If she could just stay in the shadows forever, she would have done that. Meaning, she couldn’t.”

“I hear you, friend, and I agree,” Orfeu said. “Which means that bunch must have some little hidey-hole somewhere-outside the shadows. Somewhere right here in the kingdom of Lugunica. To cut to the chase…”

“There’s someone here in the kingdom who is helping Stride and his minions,” Bordeaux concluded.

“Took the words right out of my mouth,” Orfeu said, snapping his fingers.

His warning had been on-target. This was the kind of information that could send them into a downward spiral. To know that there was a traitor somewhere in the kingdom who was actively collaborating with the walking disaster and his shinobi.

“You have to figure they’re giving him information and a base of operations, and probably food and shelter. If they’re actually helping him get in and out of the kingdom itself, then the situation might really be out of hand,” Orfeu said.

“And he has ways of making people help him even if they don’t want to,”

Wilhelm mused. He’d seen how the rings on Stride’s fingers-the Ten Proud Commandments or whatever he’d called them-had forced the two shinobi to obey him. Then there was the curse on Veltol’s life, and the dark bruise afflicting Carol, which could also probably be attributed to the power of Stride’s rings. All his powers were hideous, and none made it obvious what the others might be.

What was he capable of? The question applied both to his humanity and his abilities, and both answers were deeply troubling.

“I’ll keep sniffing around, my good man,” Orfeu said. “But there’s one thing I want your authorization for.” He raised a single finger. Bordeaux told him to continue with only a look, and Orfeu got a very serious expression on his face as he said, “I want the right to make contact with the Demi-human Alliance’s cells around the Kingdom. And to interrogate them if necessary.”

3

Bordeaux had escorted Orfeu back to the castle, saying that his request would need some arranging and discussion. He’d been strident in leaving orders to alert him if Carol’s condition changed in the slightest. Even after rising so far in the ranks, he still seemed like the captain of Zergev Squadron.

They were all irreplaceable comrades-in-arms to Bordeaux-Wilhelm and Grimm, needless to say, but also Carol. Those three had fought alongside him throughout the civil conflict.

“It seems to me that as you are now, you have the same sense of camaraderie as the young man there.”

“Don’t give me that. You don’t have to needle me about every little thing,” Wilhelm replied.

“Do pardon me. I just find myself so eager to comment on the youth. I was always like that, even in life.”Wilhelm was silent.

“Hm? I was trying to make a joke there.”

“It wasn’t funny.”

Wilhelm’s conversation partner tilted his head innocently, but Wilhelm’s lips curled in annoyance.

Once Bordeaux and his men had left, Wilhelm had ordered the knights to stand guard outside, which got them out of the room. Now he finally had time to focus on the problem in front of him-the one he could literally turn and face. Namely…

“Pivot.”

“It does feel more than a little odd to hear you call me that. A strange thing, a chance for the living to exchange words with one already dead.”

The speaker looked at Wilhelm through a monocle on his right eye. He had brown hair and an intelligent look to him. He was pleasantly handsome but could give an impression of cold distance at a glance.

It would be a mistaken impression, however. Every member of Zergev Squadron, Wilhelm most of all, knew within this man’s breast dwelled the spirit of true knighthood.

The man’s name was Pivot Anansi. He had been Zergev Squadron’s vicecaptain during Bordeaux’s time as its leader and had died in the civil war- Wilhelm had seen it himself.

In short, this was an impossible meeting.

Pivot clasped his hands behind his back and gave Wilhelm a gentle look.“I recall during the civil war, a Witch used a secret technique to animate corpses. Might that have some connection to my presence here now?”

“She didn’t bring the dead back to life, she just controlled them like puppets. You don’t look like any Witch’s puppet to me. So you…” Wilhelm stopped mid-sentence and looked down. It wasn’t that he had to collect his thoughts-much the opposite. He hesitated because it was all too clear.

After a long moment, Pivot asked,“So I what?”

“You’re…dead, no question. And the dead don’t come back to life. You’re just a counterfeit.”

“Very much how I would expect you to put it. If my heart were less stout, those sharp words might have claimed my very life. Indeed, as it turned out, it

was not words, but an actual blade that cut me down.”Wilhelm had no response to that.

“You don’t even go pale at that? Ah, now that’s Wilhelm Trias.”Pivot nodded-just a tug of his chin-two or three times, satisfied. Wilhelm ground his teeth even harder than usual.

Pivot’s eyes, his voice, his attitude showed no hostility to or estrangement from Wilhelm. But so what?

He had begun to be able to see Pivot, and to hear his voice, immediately after chasing out Stride and the others. That meant this was some attack, left with him by that walking disaster. Continuing to speak with it would only make Stride happier.

“You’ve come back just to snipe at me at this late date?”

And yet, Wilhelm’s lips, his emotions, desperately wished to talk with Pivot.

“Hardly,” Pivot replied, shrugging at Wilhelm’s hesitation and confusion. “As you say, it would be too late to vent my spleen now. Even if I were to become a hollow and appear before you, well, it would just be too little, too late.”

“So what’s the point? Why are you showing yourself like this?”

“A fair question. Whatismy purpose here, Wilhelm?”

“Like I’d know.” Wilhelm paused. “Did you used to be like that?” he asked, frowning at the sense that he was being teased. He glared at Pivot.

To reiterate, Pivot might look calm and restrained, but he was man enough to keep a leash on the Mad Dog, Bordeaux. Even Wilhelm, who had fought on many a battlefield at Bordeaux’s orders (and on behalf of Bordeaux, who certainly wished he could have been there)-even Wilhelm, who was now captain of Zergev Squadron himself, still felt he had things to learn from Pivot.

But excepting their stations and the question of their capabilities therein…

“Were we really close enough, you and I, to speak of the impression we leave on each other?”

Wilhelm said nothing.

“We served two years in the same unit…but ultimately, we were only colleagues. I don’t think we were ever at ease enough to call each other friends.”

“Yeah. I’d say the same thing. Which is what makes it so weird that I’m seeing you.”

Pivot hadn’t been a friend. And if what he said was to be believed, he didn’t resent Wilhelm for anything.

There was no doubt Wilhelm should not be seeing the phantom in front of him. If the appearance of this illusion was some plot of Stride’s, then Wilhelm should cut through the smoke and mirrors and-

“Do not…let Wilhelm die!”

No sooner had the thought crossed his mind than he heard a shout follow it.

It was the voice of Pivot, in the middle of the Demi-human War, taking the fatal blow from the enemy to protect Wilhelm.

Wilhelm’s insides were now roiled by the rage and astonishment of that moment’s loss. His hand was on his sword, yet in the face of this fearsome illusion, he couldn’t draw it.

Wilhelm stood, grinding his teeth and unable to move, when he heard a small sigh.

“Ahh, yes. You’re right. I always did draw the short straw.” Pivot adjusted his monocle and shook his head softly. It was just how he’d acted back when he was trying to wrangle the wild Zergev Squadron.

After he spoke, Pivot let his eyes drift shut. He was looking for some way to make the situation better. Many were the times that his suggestions had helped lead Zergev Squadron to victory.

So-

“Hngh!”

“You drew, I see.”

There was a clang of metal on metal, accompanied by Pivot’s voice, all but disinterested. The sword Wilhelm had so hesitated to draw was in his hand almost of its own volition-to block Pivot’s sword, which had been aimed at his neck.

“What…what do you think you’re doing?!”

“I lack either art or craft, but let this be my answer-now, what is my purpose?”

“Hrk…!”

Pivot winked, speaking as calmly as ever-only the flash of his sword came fast and sharp.

It was an illusion. A phantasm. Not a hollow or even a zombie soldier, Pivot had no physical form here. If so, however, then what was the blade that had collided with Wilhelm’s sword? What was the substance of this battle?

What was he fighting with and why? What did he hope to gain by standing here?

Pivot could not hope to best Wilhelm in a straight fight. He simply wasn’t good enough. He had average skills for a soldier, but nothing like those possessed by a true swordsman. Needless to say, he did not measure up to the Sword Saint-Theresia-and so Wilhelm, who had bested her, could have cut him down in a matter of five seconds.

And yet he didn’t. Couldn’t. Wilhelm found himself forced onto the defensive, until-

“What a shame. It seems this is all there is.”

After several ringing exchanges, Pivot had brought them blade to blade, their swords locked against each other. His announcement was entirely onesided.

For a moment, Wilhelm was taken aback-and then, he felt a great swell of rage within him. Pivot thought he could just show up whenever he wanted, lead Wilhelm around by the nose, and then decide when it was all over? That was too much. Too much!

The thoughts were clear in Wilhelm’s eyes as the two of them stood with their swords pressed against one another, each reflected in the other’s blade. Pivot, however, only laughed. It wasn’t a cold chuckle or mockery. It was hard to say what kind of laugh it was.

“Wilhelm Trias. It will be your turn next.”

Wilhelm’s breath caught, and Pivot vanished before he could demand what that was supposed to mean.

With his sparring partner abruptly gone, Wilhelm clucked his tongue at the weakness of his own sword as it sliced through the air. He sheathed it once more, then looked around the now empty room one more time before turning to the entrance.

“Wilhelm?”

Theresia was there, her eyes glistening.

Wilhelm swallowed hard. He realized why Pivot had vanished so suddenly

-either because the situation was less favorable to him or perhaps to spare Wilhelm the indignity of being seen talking to thin air. Whichever it was, it was obvious that Pivot’s phantom did not intend to talk to Theresia.

“What’s Carol’s prognosis?” Wilhelm asked.

“I was just about to go find out. Will you come with me?”

“Of course I will, dummy. Of all the times to hold back with me…”

Wilhelm went over and took Theresia by the shoulders. He stuck close to her all the way to the second floor, making sure that what his hands felt was real.

He’d held his sword only moments ago-but in his palms, there was no trace of the furious battle with Pivot.

4

In the room where they had sat Carol down, Roswaal delivered her diagnosis: “She loses the ability to breathe when she is touched by the one she loves.

That, I think we can say, is the yoke put on Carol by Stride’s spell.”

It was more or less what they had imagined to be the worst case scenario, and hearing it out loud only made the caster seem all the more evil. Forbidden to touch the one she loved-what could anyone see in that except malice?

Carol managed, “When he put the spell on me, I heard him say ‘Burgundy Thumb.’”

She was talking about what Stride had done to her body. It hurt more than anything to hear how she forced herself to remain detached as she said it.

“Burgundy Thumb? Does that mean…this is a spell similar to the Scarlet Pinky that Stride used on my father?” Theresia suggested, her expression dark.

“I think we can safely assume that,” Roswaal replied. “Wilhelm reported hearing something about the Ten Proud Commandments.”

Wilhelm, for his part, was leaning against the wall with his arms crossed. “Yeah,” he nodded.

Each of Stride’s fingers seemed to correspond to a spell he could cast: the pinky for Veltol, the pointer and middle fingers for the two shinobi he’d had with him, and the thumb for Carol. The logic of it was starting to come into focus, as far as it went.

“Our enemy has ten fingers, each of which has a spell that can control the will of others. We know of four of them, but there could be as many as six more,” Roswaal said.

“It might make sense if Eight-Arms is working with him under the influence of one of those rings,” Wilhelm said. “They looked pretty cozy, but we just don’t know.”

“Ah, I see. Even if you’re right, that means there could be another five we don’t know about. Even this ‘Ten Proud Commandments’ business could simply be misdirection-whoooo knows if his toes are in on the act, too?”

It was a chilling thought, but Stride was cruel enough that no one could say it was impossible.

“There’s something more important,” Theresia cut in. She had sat down next to Carol on the bed and placed her arms gently around the girl’s shoulders. “Is there nothing you can do for her? This is more than we can bear. I’ll never forgive him…!”

“I understand how you feel,” Roswaal said, her multi-colored eyes downcast. “And I’m sorry. This is beyond my power.”

Theresia swallowed hard, regretting she had made Roswaal feel as if she had to apologize. In point of fact, Roswaal bore no guilt in this. Everyone here, except perhaps Roswaal herself, knew that very well.

And so…

“Raise your head, Lady Mathers.”

“Carol…”

“It is my own failure I let the enemy get the drop on me. Under most circumstances, if I were too weak to defeat a foe, I would pay with my life. The reason I didn’t this time was not good fortune but the enemy’s hubris. Iwillmake him pay.”

Roswaal’s words seemed to have inspired Carol to marshal her own strength of heart. Truth be told, it was so powerful that even Wilhelm was almost overcome by it. Needless to say, then, Roswaal was shocked. Her eyes widened slightly, and she drew a breath.

“The same goes for you, Lady Theresia. I ask you not to be upset. Your Carol can still fight. Unlike with Lord Veltol, as long as I’m careful, this need not impact my ability to live my life.”

“Careful! What a word!”

“So long as I turn a blind eye to my emotions, I could go so far as to say the enemy made a grave miscalculation.”

This time, it was Theresia who was left speechless by Carol’s pronouncement.

They all knew, of course, she was only pretending to feel so strong. But Carol would not be moved. She would continue to insist none of them had been at fault, no matter how many times they pressed the subject.

It was the right thing to do. For Stride alone was evil incarnate.

“…aol…”

When she heard her name, Carol’s face tensed, though she had only just reassured first Roswaal and Theresia. The one who had called to her was Grimm, who had remained silent the entire time-and not because of the old war wound on his throat.

He was the one she loved, the person cut off from her by the spell.

“Grimm…”

Unable to touch each other, the two lovers could only exchange a look, and Carol, who had been so strong until that moment, hesitated when she saw him standing there with grim resolve.

No sooner had she spoken Grimm’s name, however, than Carol did something that shocked everyone around her-she reached out to him.

If Wilhelm and the others were amazed, Grimm’s eyes went wider than any of theirs.

Carol gave a sad smile to see her lover’s reaction, then grimaced. “Please tell me, Grimm… Tell me you don’t blame yourself.”

“Wehh, I…”

“You and I both did our duty. If this had happened to Lady Theresia or even Wilhelm, I couldn’t have endured it.”

“What I must do has also been decided. I have to destroy the Burgundy Thumb and take revenge. With my own hands…with all of us together, if I can.”

Once again, Carol reached toward Grimm. He looked from Carol’s hand and into her eyes, which were locked on him, and held his breath.

If they touched, the curse would activate, and the dark bruise would begin to strangle Carol.

And yet, even still…

Grimm mustered his resolve, and after a few seconds’ hesitation, he took Carol’s hand.

The curse, of course, took effect immediately, the dark mark appearing on her pale neck. Yet it could not stop the two in their shared determination to sense each other’s love.

“Hngh… Haah! Haah! How…do you like that? It’s…nothing,” Carol gasped.

“As if,” Roswaal said. “Heavens, you and Grimm must both be mad. To make yourselves suffer so…”

“But…it wasn’t…meaningless. Was it?” Carol asked with a grin at Roswaal, who had already begun to examine her neck. Carol was not normally so eager for battle. Roswaal gave her a wry smile, already admitting defeat.

Grimm and Carol had decided they needed to make this gesture, to endure this otherwise unnecessary pain. Roswaal knew that, as did Wilhelm and Theresia; it was why none of them chided the lovers for foolishness.

It had touched off something more-and not just for Wilhelm.

“Wilhelm, I need to talk to you.”

Theresia, from her place near Carol, looked at him gravely. At the light of resolution in her blue eyes, Wilhelm winked. He had a pretty good idea what she was going to say. What came next only underlined it.

“I know how much our promise means to you. If I were to break it… would you hate me?”

“You’re not the Sword Saint anymore,” Wilhelm said. He knew without asking which promise she meant.

Theresia had been targeted, and Carol had been hurt because of it. With her deep compassion and strong sense of responsibility, and above all, her profound love for Carol, Theresia was never going to be able to silently let this pass.

“Lady Theresia? No! You can’t do that! I don’t care what happens to me, you mustn’t-!”

“Iiielm,” Grimm said. He and Carol each had their own reactions to the exchange between Wilhelm and Theresia. Carol tried to pull Theresia back, as if to stop her from going to do whatever she was going to do, while Grimm turned a serious expression on Wilhelm. They both seemed to have forgotten they had just performed a mad ritual themselves.

All the same, no one could stop Theresia-Theresia van Astrea, the Sword

Saint. She stood at the apex of swordsmanship; only the Sword Devil Wilhelm Trias had been able to take her blade from her, and she had been relieved of her position only through the personal allowances of the king. Now she sought to take the sword up again on behalf of her dear friend, her family member, Carol.

Roswaal clapped her hands. “You must forgiiiive me, interrupting when you’re all feeling so passionate.” All eyes turned to her.

“Roswaal?” Theresia said.

“As a fellow woman, I wish I could endorse your heartfelt resolve-but because I’m a fellow woman, I have to be the one to rain oooon your parade.”

That was quite a way to start off. Theresia knitted her eyebrows. “As a fellow woman? What do you mean? I have a very bad feeling about what you’re going to say…”

“A bad feeling? True, I’m not quite sure how to express what I have to tell you. But letting you return as the Sword Saint now would not be the moral thing to do.”

“Enough ominous bullshit. What are you getting at?” Wilhelm snapped. Roswaal always took a roundabout route to get to her point, but at the moment, this tendency of hers was annoying him more than usual. Wilhelm leaned forward and encouraged her to be brief. While trying to understand the spell that had been put on Carol, Roswaal had also examined Theresia, Grimm, and Wilhelm. If she had found some sort of problem with Theresia…

“Don’t tell me that goddamned Stride-”

“Oh, no. In short-congratulations.”

“Buh?” Wilhelm froze, brought up short. He didn’t know what to make of this word. What was she saying to him? Congratulations? What did that mean?

“R-Roswaal, um… Ahem… Are you saying…?”

“Pleeeease, don’t take it as if you’ve come down with some strange disease. To put it properly: You’re with child. Pregnant. You are a woman

and a wife. But now, you shall also be a mother.”

Roswaal was calm but businesslike, yet Wilhelm still couldn’t get his head around what she was saying.

He wasn’t the only one. Theresia obviously felt the same. Her mouth worked open and closed. She stood up unsteadily, and then her eyes met Wilhelm’s where he stood frozen.

She was with child. She was withchild? Who? Theresia, with child?

“We can’t send a woman who’s about to become a mother to the battlefield. Soooo as much as I respect your decision, I’m going to have to stop you.”

“Y-yes, uh, right. You might be, um, right. Uh…Isshe right?” Theresia blathered.

Her confusion left Wilhelm feeling almost as flummoxed as she was. “Don’t ask me. It’syourbelly-yikes?!” He stepped back, caught off-guard by a wave of murderousness.

Something brushed just past his nose: a pillow from the bed, which should have been very soft. Imbued with that malice, however, it became a weapon that would have done a fair amount of damage if it had hit him. And the one who had thrown it was a woman who glared at him, her eyes trembling with rage-Carol. She fixed her green eyes on Wilhelm from her seat on the bed.

“Trias, you son of a…! Getting Lady Theresia pregnant? What have you done?!”

“Don’t call me by my old family name! You sound and look totally serious!”

“I am serious! Or are you trying to say that you’renotserious when it comes to Lady Theresia?”

“There’s no sense even talking to you! You’ll never listen to me…”

Wilhelm was less than pleased to have Carol coming after him, but this menace and passion were-and had been-the possessions of Carol Remendes since the day she was born.

While Carol was busy expressing her full fury, Wilhelm heard a quivering voice say, “Wilhelm…” He turned and saw Theresia, her hands on her belly. Somewhere under her palms, a new life was blooming.

To his own surprise, Wilhelm felt his entire body shake and only belatedly did he gasp in shock.

He and Theresia had fallen in love and become man and wife. Naturally, they had assumed their family would grow in time, but somehow, he had thought it would be a long way off.

“Wilhelm,” Theresia spoke his name again and touched his face. It was only when her pale fingers brushed his cheek that Wilhelm realized her hand was trembling.

He wasn’t the only one to be startled, confused, and set back on his heels

by this revelation. Theresia was facing the same question he was and trying to decide how to confront it.

And there was only one person in the entire world with whom she could share that question and figure it out together.

“Roswaal is right,” Wilhelm said finally. “I can’t let you take up the sword

-I vow it anew here.”

“But…when things are this dire…”

“I just have to get strong enough-no,bestrong enough-that there’s no need for you to do that.”

Strong enough that he could not be beaten by Stride, or by the shinobi who worked with that walking disaster, or by Eight-Arms Kurgan.

Wilhelm knew he had the strength and the friends required to accomplish that goal.

“Lady Theresia…”

Theresia started. “Carol… I’m sorry. You…you’re in the most awful situation, and I can’t…”

She began to apologize, but Carol shook her head. “No,” she said. “I’m the one who should apologize for losing my head. That was shameful. Truthfully, I should be the first out of everyone to say…”

“Carol…?”

“Congratulations on your pregnancy. I’m profoundly happy for you.” Carol smiled bravely, and Theresia’s breath caught.

The sense of guilt didn’t go away. Surely, the doubts would come again. But for just one moment, as the woman she loved like a sister congratulated her…

“Thank you, Carol. Thank you.”

Just for now, she could surely be permitted a heartfelt smile.

5

“Now then, one must be prepared if one is going to be pregnant. On that note, as I’m also a midwife, I would like to give some lessons to the ladies in the room. If I could ask the meeeen to politely withdraw?”

Thus chased out by Roswaal, Wilhelm and Grimm retreated from the bedroom. Roswaal might have sounded flippant, but having been acquainted with the woman for five years, the two men were more than willing to trust her relationship with Carol, along with the knowledge Roswaal would treat no life poorly.

Hence, they were not concerned in the slightest about what would happen inside the bedchamber-if anything, their worries were on the outside. “Sorry,” Wilhelm said to Grimm. “That was bad timing.” Finally alone with Grimm in the hallway, he could say what he had been unable to say in the room.

Grimm greeted his pronouncement with wide eyes, then frowned doubtfully. He wasn’t playing dumb; he really didn’t seem to understand what Wilhelm meant.

Wilhelm looked at the ground. “I meant what I said. There’s no way I’m ever going to let Theresia hold a sword again…but right when you and Carol are in a crisis, we find out this… I can’t exactly celebrate right-hrk!”

“Iiihem!”

Grimm grabbed Wilhelm around the chest and shoved him against the wall. Grimm’s anguished exclamation was accompanied by great anger in his eyes. He wasn’t frustrated Wilhelm had dared to pity his and Carol’s situation. No, he was condemning Wilhelm’s attitude-his behavior as a father.

Grimm was incensed to hear Wilhelm say he couldn’t fully celebrate his wife’s pregnancy, the blooming of a new life.

After all this time, Wilhelm was still putting others first? This was why Grimm was constantly telling him that being too kindhearted would only come back to bite him.

“That’swhat you’re angry about?”

“Iiihem… You!”

“It just doesn’t seem real yet. And I still need to really brace myself for it.

It’s just…”

“Juh wha?”

Only when Wilhelm’s flood of excuses-his voice almost cracking the whole time-finally ceased did Grimm let go of him.

Wilhelm had told Grimm something he should not have heard, placed a burden on him he should never have borne. He knew that, and yet no word of apology to Grimm came from his lips.

It wasn’t an abundance of pride that was preventing him from speaking. So what was?

“You were always vulnerable when a situation couldn’t be resolved with your sword. It’s your weak point. Am I right, Wilhelm?”

Wilhelm still didn’t speak. There, over Grimm’s shoulder, Wilhelm saw him: Pivot, leaning against the wall with his arms crossed. It felt like the phantom’s words were mocking precisely what Wilhelm felt deep inside.

Could the Sword Devil be a father, feeling likethis? Wilhelm ground his teeth so hard he tasted blood.

CHAPTER 3: ACT III

Hiding of Flowers

1

“Theresia, how are you doing? You’re not too cold, are you? You’re sure you’re doing all right? Wait… You’re not too hot, are you? If there’s anything you need, anything at all, you can just tell your dear old dad. Are you feeling thirsty? Hungry? I know! You had a stuffed animal you used to cherish. You would always stop crying the moment I gave it to you. I can go fetch it for you right now!”

“Father,that is enough!” Theresia said, enunciating each word.

The man she was speaking to was pacing restlessly about the room, looking far less in control than his imposing height and facial hair might have suggested. Theresia was pretty sure she’d sounded suitably angry, but he almost seemed to dance. “Ah! What’s the matter, Theresia? Is there something you want to tell your dear dad? Ah-no, I hear you! I love you too, my darling.”

“Father, will you just sit down already?”

“‘Just’ sit down? Now, that does hurt your father a little bit…”

“Sit, Dad!”

“Yes’m!”

Theresia pointed forcefully to the chair by the bedside, and only then did she finally get through to the man. Once he was safely seated, Theresia, in her pajamas, at last leaned back against the pillows propped up on her bed.

She’d been greeted here in the bedchamber of her family home by a man who had the same fiery red hair and blue eyes as she did-Veltol Astrea. Despite his distinctly tough and manly appearance, he could be strikingly petty, and his doting love for his daughter inspired a resolute hostility toward her husband.

Mostly, others just chuckled at the fidgety father, but he was more out of sorts than usual today. In a word, he seemed downright shady.

The cause of this behavior did not lie with Veltol himself, but with Theresia, who he had insisted should lie quietly in bed.

“Father…” Theresia said. “I’m very touched you’re so worried about me,

but having you pace around my bedroom doesn’t help anything. It tires me out just watching you. Please, give it a rest.”

“O-oh, yes, I see… In that case, well… Yes, if I must. Very well. Theresia, you just leave everything to your father. Giving it a rest is what I’m best at! Why, the residents of this mansion shush and shoo me away all the time!”

Veltol eagerly nodded his assent to his daughter’s eminently reasonable although strikingly urgent request. The way he pounded his chest, proud of this fact, was somewhat tragic, but Theresia had enough compassion for her father not to say so.

With Veltol, however, just keeping his mouth shut was not necessarily enough to make himquiet.

“Father… I can practically hear you looking around. Your wholeaurais noisy.”

“I think it’s beyond my ability to do anything about that! Don’t you think you’re going a little far?!”

“Dad, don’t yell.”

“Urk!”

When his daughter flatly rejected the emotion for her that radiated from every pore on his body, Veltol-despite his advanced age-well, he looked like he might cry.

His antics were forestalled when the door to the bedroom opened, and a woman with long, bright flaxen hair interrupted the conversation. It was Theresia’s mother, Tishua Astrea. “My dear,” she said, “Theresia ispregnant.

We need to see to her comfort.”

“Mother!” Theresia said and looked at the newcomer as if at a savior.

Veltol, however, stood up indignantly. “What a way to talk, Tishua. You’ll never find another person at this moment who’s attending to Theresia’s comfort as devoutly as I am. Search the whole world, there’s no one.”

“I realize you’re trying to be considerate by using your quietest voice, but I can’t hear a word you’re saying. You’ll have to speak up.”

“What?! You mean whispering is no good, either?!” he exclaimed in his quietest voice.

“I still can’t hear you.”

Veltol might have been exclaiming and complaining as softly as possible, but the obtrusiveness of his energy was unchanged.

Still, her mother’s arrival bolstered Theresia’s morale. When Tishua saw her daughter’s look of supreme trust, she pointed elegantly out the window. “Look, dear. Theresia simply can’t stop fretting about those flower beds in the garden. Surely her father could be kind enough to go and water them for her?

I can’t believe you’ve been so oblivious as to miss it.”

“Hrm! Well, of course I noticed that! I simply thought there must be something else I could do, something more… Hah, well, I’m going! You just watch how kind I can be!”

Veltol had practically raced out of the room before he finished speaking. Theresia watched him go, her eyes round with surprise, while Tishua smiled, very pleased. “He’s so simple to handle. He’s just so sweet, you see.”

“You’ve always been so good at knowing what to do with Father,” Theresia said.

“The real secret to successful married life is for the wife to always know how to steer her husband. Just you make sure you learn how to steer yours.” With that, Tishua sat down in the seat Veltol had just vacated.

“Yes, Mom,” Theresia said, obedient as a child and happy to learn from her mother, who had so much more experience than she did in being a wife.

“Heavens,” Tishua said and smiled, gently but wryly. “You sound just like a meek child. To think…my little girl is going to be a mother herself. I can hardly believe it.”

“Yes, uh…well, I feel the same. Love, marriage, children… All of it seemed like it would be so much further in the future. Or… No. It’s, like, for me…”

“You thought those things had nothing to do with you.”

“…Yeah.”

Theresia was overwhelmed with wonder at her mother, who could put into words these feelings she couldn’t articulate.

In every way, Tishua was Theresia’s idol, the person she sought to imitate. Theresia’s ideal of how to be a woman and how to be a wife came from observing her parents’ happy marriage. She wondered if she and Wilhelm would be able to go through life like Tishua and Veltol.

“You needn’t worry, Theresia. Unlikemyhusband, Wilhelm knows how to stay calm and has a bit of dignity.”

“Compared to Father, a newborn baby knows how to stay calm.”

“That’s…well, it’s not that much of an exaggeration. For him, the world is always fresh and exciting.”

At the sight of her mother putting a hand to her mouth and speaking fondly of Veltol, Theresia found herself smiling as well. Her hand wandered to the gentle swell in her belly.

A full three months had passed since her pregnancy had been discovered.

Truthfully, the revelation of her pregnancy had been…well, what it was. Under the circumstances, it had been hard to embrace the idea from the bottom of her heart, but with the passage of time, it had become more real to her.

For one thing, the child in Theresia’s belly was growing steadily, without regard for how long she might need to get mentally prepared. With her body changing faster than her mind and thoughts, all her heart could do was hurry to keep up.

Thanks to which, she was somehow managing to tackle all the various issues, problems, and questions that came along with pregnancy and childbirth.

Luckily, everyone around her proved deeply considerate. Her parents, for example, made frequent trips to the royal capital to see their beloved daughter and made every effort to prepare to welcome their first grandchild. Notably, His Majesty the King, upon hearing the news, had planned to throw a celebration that would have involved the entire country-which was nice of him-but naturally, Theresia declined.

Supposedly, Theresia heard, when the king had secretly attended her and Wilhelm’s wedding in disguise, he’d found it so moving that he’d wished he had made it a national affair. This was very much in keeping with the famously good-natured character of the reigning monarch, Jionis Lugunica.

Truly, Theresia was blessed by her family, her husband, and her neighbors.

Which was precisely why…

“You must stop blaming yourself for what happened to Carol, Theresia.”

“Mother…”

“Yes, Iamyour mother. Do you not think I can tell what’s on your mind? The guilt you’re feeling? I’m sure your father feels the same way I do.”

Tishua placed her hand gently over her daughter’s. Theresia looked down, unable to say anything.

Her mother was right. Veltol had spectacularly poor judgment in matters of all kinds, but on this most important subject, her father would surely not miss the mark. Especially not when it involved his own child, and Carol, whom he treated like a daughter.

“You and Wilhelm bear no fault for the misfortune that befell Carol. I know you’ll be upset about it no matter how many times I say that, but Iwillkeep saying it.”

“Oh, Mother… I know you will.”

“Sometimes I just don’t know what to do with you. If only you could see things half as simply ashedoes.”

“Do you really mean that?”

“No, I’m joking. I love him very much, but if you ended up like him, I don’t know how I could apologize to Wilhelm.”

Theresia found her lips curling upward at her mother’s attempt at a joke, but she didn’t have the strength for a real smile. Tishua noticed and sighed. “Theresia, I like to think that my husband and I do everything we can for you as parents. And for Carol, too. But in the end, it’s all down to your own efforts.”

“The end…”

“You need to ponder it, mull it over, ask me or anyone you want for advice, just find a way you can accept whatever it is-and then, make sure you talk to Wilhelm. Husband and wife, no matter what you’re facing.”

Theresia listened intently, her hand still clasping her mother’s. This, she sensed, was a deeply important lesson. Maybe even the secret to what made Tishua and Veltol seem the ideal couple to Theresia.

Even as she digested her mother’s words, Theresia reached out with her free hand and felt for the baby, making sure it was there.

She wanted to do everything she could-everything she could to bless this life in her belly. Even if it meant she could not yet relinquish the name of Sword Saint, which she had so reviled and distanced herself from…

As she gazed at her beloved daughter’s gossamer face, Tishua murmured, “Really… I just don’t know what to do with you,” so quietly that Theresia didn’t hear.

2

She was buttoning her shirt when, much in spite of herself, she let out a sigh.Shoot, she thought, but it was too late. The other person in the room had already heard her and was giving her a pained smile.

“Believe me, it huuurts me almost as much as you to know how little you can expect of me.”

“I’m sorry, Lady Mathers. And when I’ve been relying on your goodwill.”

“That’s certainly not what I meant for you to get from that. I suppose the waaaay I said it is the problem. Please, allow me to apologize.” Roswaal shrugged almost offhandedly, and Carol gave a thin smile. That apology was Roswaal’s way of being thoughtful. She was trying to cover for Carol’s rudeness by suggesting they both felt the same way, but it only made Carol feel guiltier than ever.

They were in a room in a stone tower on the grounds of the royal castle of Lugunica that had been allocated specifically for research into magic.

This tower was a twin of the one where prisoners were kept, but it had been shuttered for more than a decade. When, after the Demi-human War,

Roswaal had become a strong proponent of the importance of magic, King Jionis had relinquished it to her for use as a laboratory.

Since then, magical research had been going on day and night. Roswaal was only the most prominent of those who worked there. Carol had come more than once in hopes of finding a way to break the curse on her.

“Here and only here, we can haaaave our little rendezvous without any concern that anyone else will butt in,” Roswaal said.

“Why must you make it sound so scandalous? I know you just want everyone out of the way because we don’t know how dangerous this spell actually is.”

“Oh, you’re so cold. We’ve known each other for five years now. Surely you could relax a little.”

“Don’t ask me to do that. I’m afraid I might forget my place and make some sort of slip.”

“Oh, I wouldn’t miiiind in the slightest if you did.” Roswaal winked her blue eye playfully; Carol, unsure how to respond, settled on an ambiguous smile.

It remained, however, that Roswaal was not wrong. They had been acquainted with each other for five years now, and, somewhat in spite of herself, Carol had started to feel genuinely close to Roswaal somewhere along the line. Not just as comrades-in-arms who had fought in and survived the Demi-human War together, but genuine friendship.

Every time she noticed it, Carol would admonish herself for being so disrespectful.

“In any case, here are the results of the test from which we expect so little. Good news or bad-which do you want first?”

“Let me guess. The good news is that it hasn’t gotten any worse. And the bad news is that the only way to find out how to break the curse is to ask Stride himself. Am I right?”

“Goodness gracious, you’re going to talk me out of a job…is what I might say, but I suppose it’s my own failure for not bringing you a different outcome.”

“I didn’t mean to…”

“You’ve stopped getting mad, haven’t you, Carol?”

The unexpected observation brought Carol up short. “What?” she said. She thought maybe this was another of Roswaal’s ill-advised attempts at humor, but those two differently colored eyes were trained on her with utmost seriousness. This forced her to take seriously the suggestion that she wasn’t enraged anymore.

“I’m aware I often used to raise my voice. I didn’t have the emotional resources to do otherwise. I realize this is no excuse, but when I first met you, Lady Mathers, I was still young, and my manners were often lacking.”

“I’m not trying to make you disavow your younger self. I quite liked the way you would turn red in the face from yelling. You often butted heads with young Wilhelm, didn’t you?”

“I still do. Much as it embarrasses me…” Carol faced Roswaal, unsure what the heart of this conversation was supposed to be. Roswaal always did like roundabout paths and deliberately obscure ways of speaking, but Carol also trusted that the woman rarely if ever said anything truly meaningless. And she knew how much Roswaal hated the fact she had yet to find an effective countermeasure against the hex on Carol.

If, from that position, Roswaal was engaging her in a discussion, then all Carol could do was approach it just as seriously. “I’m no longer a girl of ten,” Carol said. “I can’t continue to make Lady Theresia worry by constantly getting upset about-”

“Even though ‘Lady Theresia’ is the cause of the mess you find yourself in?”

Carol’s breath caught. Roswaal’s voice was quiet, and there was even an element of cold anger in it. For a moment, Carol only looked at Roswaal, certain she had somehow misheard. Roswaal looked back at her and raised a single finger.

“Are you listening? Let’s be clear. The fault of the spell placed upon you rests with her. Needless to say, it’s the spellcaster himself, Stride, that we must call evil, but-being targeted by such a man and ultimately dragging you into the matter-the failure is hers.”

“H-how dare you…”

“You must be an idiot not to see it. Or are you deliberately averting your gaze? That isn’t kindness, let me tell you, just a limp refusal to think. It’s youuuu who is suffering and not your ‘Lady’ Theresia.”

“That’s enough! Stop this!” Carol leaped out of her chair, glaring at Roswaal and her merciless pronouncements. “How…how can you say such things?! Lady Theresia is at fault? She’s been in agony for so long-this whole time! I only hoped that just as much happiness would follow for the rest of her life. And yet-!”

“She is not you. Everyone ought to hope for their own greatest happiness. Once they’ve achieved it, then we can call it a virtue for them to hope for happiness in others. You’ve got it all wrong.”

“I…!”

Carol, pained by her frail protests, felt something hot at the edges of her eyes.

Why should she have to stand here and have this argument with this woman? Roswaal had just been with Theresia, had rekindled their friendship. What reason did she have for this anger?

Somewhere in the welter of emotions bubbling up within her, Carol felt something was wrong. Roswaal could go beyond the pale sometimes, but never this far…

“Lady Mathers… Are you testing me, perchance?”

“Oops, you noticed?”

Immediately, the severe look on Roswaal’s face vanished, and the chill atmosphere that had filled the room dispersed like mist. Suddenly, she seemed as if nothing had happened. Carol found herself blinking at the change.

“Sorry, sorry. I know I weeeent too far. I wasn’t testing your loyalty, but Iwasdeliberately trying to anger you. I’ll make it up to you, I promise.”

Carol took a short breath, then a deeper one, then one deeper still. “Why? Why would you…?”

“I see you just swallowed any number of things you might have liked to say.”

Carol appeared to have mastered herself with a few breaths. Impressed by the effort, Roswaal sat in a chair and crossed her long legs. She gestured for Carol to sit as well. Then she said, “Yes, I was trying to get you angry. And you did, just as I predicted. I feel tremendous satisfaction and relief in that.”

“Satisfaction I suppose I can understand, but…relief?”

“Yes, relief more than anything. I have some experience with this myself…but when your heart breaks, and you start to give up, the first thing people do is stop getting mad.”

Carol paused. “Oh. I…”

“It’s been three months, and I haven’t been able to help you at all. You simply assumed today’s test would be the same-no new developments. And

you showed neither disappointment nor despair at that fact.” This time, Carol was completely silent.

“Resignation mauls a human heart faster than anything else. In a way, that’s more terrible than the curse itself.”

Roswaal’s warning had a terrible persuasiveness to it, a great weight suggesting it was true.

Struck by the other woman’s grim words, Carol realized she had nothing to say back to her. Roswaal had been right; the test results neither excited nor upset her. She had been convinced that under the circumstances, nothing would happen-not a fortuitous stroke of good fortune, nor the inevitable bad luck. What was that but giving up? What was it but resignation?

“Is that why you wanted to make me angry?” Carol asked. “Is that why you painted yourself the villain?”

“It was a lot quicker than waiting for something or someone else to upset you. Thankfully, I know Lady Theresia is the button to press with you. You

really did get furious down in your guts, didn’t you?”

“Yes, viscerally incensed. But that’s not just because you spoke ill of Lady Theresia. It’s because, Lady Mathers, you tried to take advantage of my trust.”

“Your trust?” Roswaal blinked.

Carol glared at her. “Oh, don’t act like it’s the first time you’ve heard this. I’ll only get angry again.”

Roswaal always acted familiar with others, ready to play the dear friend- but at these crucial moments, she always seemed to have trouble grasping someone else’s emotions. That was why she found it so easy to make the choice to play the villain.

“You complained I don’t speak casually enough to you, didn’t you? Please don’t say such things lightly if you don’t really mean them. Because that’s why…”

“That’s why what?”

“Why I…hesitate to believe that I’ve really become your friend, Lady Mathers.”

It took courage to voice those words. Carol found herself unable to meet Roswaal’s eyes. She was afraid to look her in the face as she said so. It would have been so easy for Roswaal to tell her off for impertinence or thinking too much of herself. And yet, feeling it was cowardly to speak the words and then effectively run away, Carol slowly made herself look back at the other woman.

Roswaal J Mathers stood silent, looking deeply intrigued.

“L-Lady Mathers?” Carol ventured.

“Ahem, yes. Sorry. What you said was so unexpected that my mind went blank for a moment.”

“Was it that unimaginable?!”

“In a sense-because it’s so unusual for me to be evaluated without reference to my family, my looks, or my value to my interlocutor.”

Having recovered from her initial shock, Roswaal began to regain her customary volubility. If anything, it was now Carol who was trying to get her feet back under her, but quite regardless of how she was feeling, Roswaal looked down quietly and said, “I am sorry. I considered a variety of possibilities, but it seemed the only option for responding to your concern. Please know, however, that I did it because I believed it was necessary, and not out of any disrespect for you.”

“I-It’s all right, really. I can see you’re not accustomed to apologizing, Lady Mathers.”

“But…”

“You bowed your head to me once, and you truly meant it. That’s enough for me.”

Carol reached out and took Roswaal’s hand, which hung there limply. To her, it felt momentous, and she remembered that when Theresia had done the same thing for her, she had felt as if saved-had known she was trusted. She wanted to give that to Roswaal.

“Lady Mathers, I do get angry. I cry. And although I might not be very good at it, I also laugh. I promise you I won’t give those things up, now or in the future. Does that satisfy you?”

Roswaal was speechless for a second. “Yes,” she finally managed. “That’s good. I was going to say you could upbraid me to your heart’s content for failing to bring you any results, if you wished, but I suppose now I had better not.”

“That’s the wiser choice. Although, before you were wise, you weren’t nearly wise enough.” Carol was grateful for how Roswaal had wanted to get something out of her, even anger at Roswaal’s own cruelty. But if she had really wanted a display of emotion… “Then surely joy would have been as

good as rage? I can certainly say I would have preferred it.” “It’s faster to make someone yell than to make them laugh…” “Lady Mathers,” Carol said with studied severity.

“Yes?” Roswaal answered, shrinking slightly. Carol chuckled at the rare chance to see the woman squirm, as if to prove that it was not so hard to make someone laugh.

“If you would, tell me about your child… About Master Karl. What happened when he was born? I’m sure it will be of help to Lady Theresia.”

“Meeeeaning you’re going to put Lady Theresia ahead of me, your friend?” Roswaal said teasingly, her characteristic drawl returning to her voice.

“Don’t spoil the moment,” Carol said, but she smiled in spite of herself.

She could smile. She was smiling. Carol closed her eyes, pushing away the guilt she felt about that fact.

She’d convinced herself that laughing, raging, crying, were all inappropriate things to do when so many people were trying to help free her from her curse. But now, she had only a prayer on her lips-a prayer for the lover who must struggle with guilt even more seriously than she did. “Grimm… Please, be safe.”

3

“I can’t believe you went and did that. Even if you did get away with it because they were all just fodder.”

“I know what you thought. You figured you could handle it all on your own, somehow or other, right? And yeah, your intuition served you right, as usual, but… Shit! You actually did it. And I can hardly even freakin’ complain!”

Wilhelm clawed at his hair, clucking his tongue at the silent protest of Grimm, who walked beside him.

Behind the pair, members of Zergev Squadron streamed in and out of a tavern whose sign had collapsed onto the ground. They led a series of rather battered-looking men away to prison.

All of the men were roughnecks, people who had been uncooperative with Grimm’s attempts to get information out of them, causing him to become angry and beat them senseless. The number of the ruffians was not less than twenty, but even if there had been fifty of them, it would not have been enough to give Grimm pause. Indeed, there wasn’t a scratch on him.

Be that as it may, it was Wilhelm’s duty as captain to scold him for having gone too far.

At the moment, Grimm and Wilhelm were in the town of Abiet, in the south of Lugunica, following rumors that Stride and his minions had been spotted in the area.

Stride’s mischief in the kingdom hadn’t stopped with the attack on the Astrea manor. He’d continued to do evil within the kingdom’s borders.

They had consistent eyewitness reports of their quarry, but-whether because of the shinobis’ ability to move through the shadows as Wilhelm had seen or because of some other yet unexplained power of Stride’s collaborators -they couldn’t seem to pin him down or catch up.

Ultimately, even the ruffians they’d taken in at the tavern turned out to be only local toughs who’d been paid to provide safe haven. They didn’t know the first thing about the situation surrounding Stride, let alone what he was really after.

He was playing with them, pure and simple. Wilhelm understood well why Grimm might just be pissed off.

“It makes me think of the civil war, of the time when we were trying to chase down those three leaders of the Alliance. It was risky business, but we had a great battle with Valga Cromwell. You remember that?”

“How could I forget? Youdiedbecause of his little plot.”

“Yes, true. Sorry, I knew that question would touch a nerve.”

Wilhelm snorted. The lean man before him-the ghost of Pivot-closed

his eyes and shrugged.

For three full months now, ever since the struggle with Stride at the mansion, Pivot had remained ever on the edges of Wilhelm’s awareness. With how often he had been away from the capital pursuing his enemy, Wilhelm realized he had seen more of this ghost than he had of Theresia in that time.

“Thinking about it makes me want to cut you down just to let off some steam,” Wilhelm said.

“Feel free, if you can. Sadly, however, threats don’t work on me as I am now. If it were that simple to get rid of me, you wouldn’t have been dragging me around for three months.”

“Pipe down. I’ve got my hands full just trying to look after Grimm right now,” Wilhelm said, not bothering to hide his irritation. He then deliberately put Pivot’s amused smile out of his mind.

Wilhelm knew there was something abnormal about talking with Pivot’s spirit like this. It was not possible to speak with the dead, and he hardly believed in hollows, supposedly the lingering souls of dead people. But this incarnation of Pivot, Wilhelm did not strike with his sword. In fact, he had come to treasure the ghost’s ability to give him objective opinions. Normally, it was Grimm who would fulfill that role, but it would have been asking a great deal of him to do it now.

Grimm, looking somewhat haggard but with a bright fire burning in his eyes, was clearly not quite in his ordinary state of mind. He wasn’t sleeping well at night, and he was always on edge; there was no chance for him to rest his heart and mind. At the same time, he would always be the first to rush into danger, letting himself get hurt as if to punish himself.

This was getting out of hand. It was dangerous. No matter how badly Grimm might get injured, it wouldn’t redeem Carol’s situation-for there was no need for Grimm to redeem anything.

“Having said that, Wilhelm, you’re not really in any position to criticize him, are you? Think how long it’s been since you saw your wife. Your

pregnantwife. Aren’t you worried about her?”

Wilhelm took a moment before he answered, “For now, her father and mother are looking after her. And as long as Carol doesn’t touch Grimm, she can basically do what she’s always done.”

“Those are reasons why Lady Theresia will be all right, not reasons for you not to worry. Needless to say, perhaps, the two are similar, but not the same.”

Pivot’s touch of sarcasm annoyed Wilhelm, but there was nothing he could say. In the three months since they had discovered Theresia’s pregnancy, his resolve-left so pathetically unsure on that day-had grown no more certain. What right did a man like him have? What right did Wilhelm, who was no more than a sword, have to be someone’s father?

“Iielm?”

Grimm looked back, questioning, for Wilhelm had stopped in his tracks. If he was getting concern even from Grimm in his distracted state, then Wilhelm was far gone himself. The rest of Zergev Squadron must have been uneasy with their captain and vice-captain acting like this.

This would never do, Wilhelm decided. He was about to say “It’s nothing” when-

“Wilhelm! Grimm!”

“Conwood?”

They turned toward the sharp shout to see Conwood Melahau, one of Zergev Squadron’s old hands, rushing toward them. He was supposed to be on the other side of town, leading a separate unit on patrol. Wilhelm tensed. If Conwood was coming to them personally, then something must have happened to his unit.

When Conwood saw the look on Wilhelm’s and Grimm’s faces, however, he held up his hands. “Wait, wait. Don’t jump to any conclusions, please. I’m not here to warn you about an enemy or tell you about an emergency. But, uh,

I mean-itisurgent, all right?”

“If it’s none of those things, then don’t make itsoundlike it is. What’s the damn story?”

Conwood saluted. “We didn’t have you or the vice-captain, obviously, but we were able to minimize any damage the enemy could do. But anyway,” he said with some sarcasm, “here’s the point: Someone from Six-Tongue is here from the capital and wants to talk to you.”

“Orfeu, huh? He’s stuck to me like glue…”

Grimm looked awkwardly away from Conwood. The man had a gift for delivering warnings with a light touch.

Wilhelm was more interested in the name Six-Tongue-Orfeu’s organization, which had effectively become an arm of the kingdom’s intelligence network at this point. It was none other than Wilhelm himself who had recommended Orfeu to Bordeaux as a man who could get things done, but he had done more than even Wilhelm had imagined. It was SixTongue that had gotten wind of the eyewitness accounts of Stride and his people in Abiet, and it was thanks to their good work that the enemy hadn’t been able to disappear entirely, for all the trouble they gave pursuers.

However…

“Lemme guess… You want a more concrete lead, eh?”

These were the words with which Orfeu greeted Wilhelm and Grimm, along with a broad wink. He was sitting on a table that should have seated four people. Not particularly good manners.

They were in an assembly hall in the center of town. Zergev Squadron had commandeered it and were now using it as a base of operations-making reports, holding meetings, and so forth. The squadron had been told that as they didn’t intend to stay for long, there was no need to be particularly considerate of the townspeople. But Orfeu, sitting on the table as if he owned the thing, looked like he wouldn’t have been all that considerate anyway.

It was some time since Wilhelm had met Orfeu face-to-face. He’d supposedly been running hither and yon all over the kingdom on his spy missions, but his carefully curated appearance gave no sense he was under any strain.

“Truth is, nobody buys into this give-your-blood-sweat-and-tears-for-thekingdom business. Works better if you tell them you need their strength forpeace. See, anyone can be an agent of the state, but there are different ways to go about it.”

“And you dragged yourself all the way from the capital because I justhadto know this?” Wilhelm and Grimm seated themselves at the table, glowering at the flippant Orfeu.

“Hell no,” he said, seating himself in a chair as he spoke. “First up, you asked what I got out of this place. I came up empty again, and I’m sorry about that.”

“If you wanna apologize, don’t do it to me. Tell the man sitting next to me. He just finished beating twenty guys half to death.”

“Damn,” Orfeu whistled. “I mean, that’s the vice-captain of Zergev Squadron for you. And to think, he looks like the type who would be popular with the ladies-just goes to show, ya can’t trust appearances.”

Grimm nudged Wilhelm with his elbow. Wilhelm replied with a look as if to say he couldn’t be responsible for everything Orfeu said, up to and including his bad jokes. Then he turned back to Orfeu. “So? You’re the one who brought up definite leads the moment you saw us. Have you got a plan? Something that will let us catch them not by the tail, but by the collar?”

“Ifeverything comes off right, yeah. But if they don’t, things could turn ugly, so I figured I ought to run it by you. That’s why I’m here.”

“I see. Cooperation between the leaders of Zergev Squadron and SixTongue, is it?”

Wilhelm didn’t say anything, but when he glanced over, he saw the phantom Pivot had taken the last seat at the table. Grimm and Orfeu couldn’t see him, of course, so Wilhelm ignored him.

He thought, however, that Orfeu was getting at something slightly different from what Pivot perceived. Normally, Orfeu would never “run” an operation by Wilhelm. But here he was, looking Wilhelm in the eye and saying he was going to do just that.

“It involves Theresia, doesn’t it?” Wilhelm said.

“Let me be clear right at the outset, I’m not going to ask your bride to take up a sword or anything. I know she’s pregnant. Congrats, by the way.”

“Forget it. Just keep talking.”

“That’s what I do best. So like I said, she won’t be doing any sword fighting. But Idowant her to do some work as the Sword Saint. That’s why I thought I should talk to you.”

Orfeu rested his hands on the table, one atop the other, and looked seriously at Wilhelm. Grimm likewise glanced at Wilhelm to see what he was thinking.

If he was afraid Wilhelm would explode with fury, he needn’t have worried.

“I assume you’ve got a purpose and a reason. What do you want her to do?”

“Well, I’ve been chasing Stride and his friends, and I’ve discovered that everyone who’s harbored him has had something in common. There’s the punk-ass toughs he bought off, mercenaries with nowhere else to turn, and folks with a grudge against the kingdom.”

“All of them are hostile to the Kingdom of Lugunica, is that what he’s saying? Wait. Are they all…”

“Demi-humans?” Wilhelm asked, disturbed he had been so slow-witted as to miss it. He’d been so fixated on the antics of Stride and his people that he hadn’t even stopped to wonder what might motivate those who supported him. But he should have guessed from the beginning that Stride, whose plans revolved around Theresia, the Sword Saint, would seek help from people hostile to the kingdom or people hoping to foment a war.

In which case, there was every chance that the people Stride had turned to shared his objectives.

“The civil war may be over, but there are still angry demi-humans everywhere you go,” Orfeu said. “It’s possible they’re harboring Stride or working with him. I want to borrow the power of the Sword Saint to urge them to stop doing it.”

“You don’t think invoking Theresia’s name will just aggravate them? Much as I hate to say it, she killed more demi-humans than anyone in the Kingdom during the war.”

“Sure, I know that. But that’s exactly what my, uh,friendis asking for. To talk personally with the Sword Saint, the one who killed all those demihumans.”

Wilhelm was silent. Orfeu knew very well what a terrible psychological burden that desire would place on Theresia. That was exactly why he had come all this way to seek Wilhelm’s permission. To “run it by him,” just as he’d said.

And since he’d gone to all that trouble, Wilhelm couldn’t just dismiss the man out of hand. He had to give Orfeu’s idea a fair hearing. He had to carefully consider any plan that might help them break this stalemate.

“Say we let Theresia talk to this guy who wants to see the Sword Saint. What makes you think it will help anything? Who is this person who claims they can get the demi-humans to listen to them?”

The answer, Wilhelm presumed, would dictate whether he agreed to Orfeu’s plan.

Orfeu raised a single slim finger and said, “Cragrel Dawson. The last of the captured leaders of the Demi-human Alliance.”

4

The moment Theresia entered the room, she felt asensationin the air, one she recognized. She paused, trying to recall where she had experienced it before, but she soon realized-it was the air of a battlefield. Like the battlefields Theresia had gone to as the Sword Saint. It was the smell of blood and fire, which lingered in her nostrils even now that she had put down her sword. And it was coming from…

“Oh-ho. You’re actually here, mrrm, in the flesh. The Angel of Death, here before me in the guise of a mother now.”

The voice that greeted Theresia came from an old man. It sounded like a dry, rasping wind. Sitting on a chair with his hands on a cane was an elderly demi-human. He was covered in light green scales, and on his head there grew a yellow comb. She could see a long tongue curled behind his thick lips. He seemed to straddle the line between a lizardfolk and a frogfolk, which gave him a special status even among the demi-humans.

The old man’s name was Cragrel Dawson. During the Demi-human War, he had inherited the leadership of the Alliance after the death of its three masterminds. In short, he was the representative of the demi-humans of the Kingdom of Lugunica now, and the person who had signed the peace accord with His Majesty Jionis to end the war that had gone on for nearly ten years. Theresia, the Sword Saint, had of course been among those present at the ceremony. But at the time, Cragrel had seemed nothing like this.

Theresia began to feel a creeping dread at hearing herself addressed as the Sword Saint-but when she spoke, she betrayed no hint of it. She only plucked up her skirt in a curtsy and said, “I believe this is the first time we’ve exchanged words, is it not, Lord Cragrel?”

She addressed him as “Lord” because he, in fact, held a noble title. He had been awarded a peerage for his contributions to ending the war. This meeting, indeed, was taking place in the capital, within a room of the mansion that Cragrel had been given.

Considering Cragrel’s importance, the mansion was under careful guard, but at the moment, he and Theresia were alone. They had agreed that this exceedingly secret meeting should involve no outsiders at all.

On the one hand, Theresia trusted Cragrel wouldn’t try anything; on the other, the fearsome atmosphere that had enveloped her the moment she walked into the room caused her to straighten up a bit.

Cragrel scratched his head with a three-fingered hand at Theresia’s greeting. “‘Lord Cragrel.’ That’s too much of a, mrrm, title for me. If Libre and Valga were still alive, I wouldn’t even bear this burden… It seems ironic I should be the one to be old, enjoying a quiet life of retirement.”

“If you’ve been able to let your heart rest these past years, then I’m glad to hear it. You’ve certainly earned the right.”

“The right, the right! Mrrm, If I have earned it, then surely you have as well.”

Theresia felt Cragrel’s gaze dart to her stomach, and her breath caught. She was still a long way from her delivery date, but her belly was big enough that it was impossible to hide even at a distance. It was unmistakable that under her loose-fitting clothing, a new life was budding. To have this old man, a demi-human against whom she had stood against as the Sword Saint, comment on that felt…wrong, somehow.

However, Theresia only said, “Yes. Thanks to the help of so many people -my family and friends, and above all my husband-I’m able to live my life in bliss. I was able to stop killing myself, believing that such happiness was more than I should hope for.” Cragrel was silent.

“This child, and everyone alive today, has that right, too-the right to live in peace and good health tomorrow and all the days to come. All of us and all of you wanted the same thing in that war. We hurt and stole from each other until we finally saw that; I’m only glad we no longer have to.”

Resting her hand on her belly, Theresia let her mind wander back through all the days when blood had stained her, when she had wielded her sword, when she had stolen life after life. She traced the path that had led her to this moment.

She was not alone. Wilhelm, Carol and Grimm, and Bordeaux and his men had all taken and been taken from. So had Cragrel and the other demihumans. But most of them hadn’twantedto engage in such theft. Thus, peace had been able to prevail.

“Now, however, there are people who want to let all that be in vain.

You’ve heard, haven’t you, Cragrel? That’s why you called me here.”

“Given up calling me ‘Lord,’ mrrm?”

“You seemed not to like it. I’m sorry I don’t sound quite formal enough. Somewhere along the line, I grew too impassioned.”

She regretted letting her conscious and unconscious thoughts mingle together. Cragrel gazed straight at her, as her cheeks flushed slightly. Then, abruptly, he looked at the ground. His old, rounded shoulders began to shake.

“Hoh… Ho-ho-ho… Ho-ho-ho-ho-ho!” “C-Cragrel?” Theresia asked.

“Pardon me, pardon me. I couldn’t restrain the, mrrm, laughter. I never thought, mrrm, to hear such a thing from the mouth of the Angel of Death. If Valga could hear it, oh, he would be shocked!”

Cragrel shook his head slowly and almost mumbled to himself, as if he were looking into the distance. Theresia found she could say nothing.

Valga Cromwell had been considered the foremost strategist of the Demihuman Alliance. He was a Giant, and the one who had driven the kingdom further into crisis than anyone else. It was Theresia’s husband, Wilhelm, who had finally stopped him. From the way Cragrel spoke, he and Valga had been acquaintances, perhaps even friends.

Now, cruel as it was, Cragrel had to look not at individual demi-humans but at all of them collectively.

“Troublesome men, Valga and Libre-to die and leave such important work to an old man. Guru of those left behind I may be, but…well, so long as I have accepted the duty, I will see it through, Sword Saint.”

“I am-but please, call me Theresia. I think that’s more appropriate for you and I.”

“Mrrm. Then, Theresia, if I tell you what the kingdom wishes to know, then what will become of my fellows? The war that has only just ended threatens to flare up again…”

“I willnotlet it. That, if nothing else, I swear,” she said, allaying Cragrel’s concern in the most emphatic possible terms.

The resumption of hostilities the old demi-human feared was a future that must be avoided at any cost. No one in the kingdom today, neither human nor demi-human, wished to see that conflict reignited. Much less at the instigation of some outside meddler.

At Theresia’s assurance, Cragrel cast his frogfolk-like black eyes to the ground again in thought. The silence went on for several solid minutes, during which time Theresia spoke not a word, waiting for Cragrel to say something.

At last, the silence was broken by Cragrel’s long, deep sigh. “It was I who summoned you, Theresia. I wished to meet the Sword Saint, the person who has buried more of my companions than any other, and be sure of something.”

“What is it? What did you wish to discover?”

“Mrrm. I wished to see you when you saw my face and to see my own heart when I saw yours. If, as I feared, we each still harbored the old anger… But no.” Theresia saw herself reflected in Cragrel’s black eyes, which looked at her with a touch of fondness. “We have each of us a heart that repudiates war, you and I, mrrm. We do not lie to each other.”

“Cragrel…” Theresia’s eyes widened, for as Cragrel spoke, she felt a change in the room. The atmosphere of a battlefield had prevailed ever since she had entered, but now it was gone-and she knew why. It was not because Cragrel had been testing her by exuding such tension. This room had, truly,beenhis battlefield.

This had been the great fight of Cragrel Dawson’s life, facing the kingdom’s Sword Saint in his capacity as the demi-humans’ leader, and exchanging words with her to discover what the future of his people would

be. And what had finally relieved that air of battle was…

“I turned away from Valga and the others, for I could not fight. If there is something I can do, it is to lead as many of my people as possible, every single one I can, to the tomorrow you speak of.”

“So you’ll…”

“I will do as the kingdom asks. I shall summon my comrades, and I will tell you the whereabouts of those suspected of harboring the ones you seek. Only, insofar as it’s possible…”

“We’ll be gentle. I know.” Theresia paused. “Thank you, Cragrel. You have my gratitude.”

As leader of the demi-humans, Cragrel had agreed to everything the kingdom had wanted. Theresia could not have been more relieved by his response, but she was also concerned about his obvious fatigue. He might look outwardly like one who would not soon feel the effects of age or tiredness, but he seemed shrunken, noticeably smaller than he had been when she’d entered the room. If that was only from relief at no longer having to sustain the tension of meeting the Sword Saint, then so be it. But suppose it was not relief, but exhaustion?

“Cragrel… I know it may not be my business, but let me say one thing.” “Yes, mrrm?”

“You fought brilliantly onyourbattlefield. Speaking as the one who stood against you, I can say this: You have nothing to be ashamed of before your friends.”

Theresia did not know if her words would be any comfort to Cragrel, let alone salvation. Perhaps they might not resonate with him at all, in which case, there was nothing she could do. She’d had countless such encounters during the civil war.

Nonetheless, Theresia said the words to Cragrel, who silently covered his face.

That was enough to bring at least one conclusion to the Demi-human War.

5

“Please? What I can do is limited right now, so I want to do everything I’m able.”

He’d known that would be how Theresia would respond when he told her about Orfeu’s suggestion. He knew Theresia’s personality, knew how she hated being stuck in one place on account of her pregnancy. Having been summoned under the name of the Sword Saint, there was no way Theresia would sit silently by. And yet…

“Ahh! Theresia! My Theresia! No, no, no, no, no. A one-on-one meeting with the leader of the Demi-human Alliance-and while she’s pregnant?

Maybe we should have said no?!”

“Calm down, ‘Father.’ That’s whatwe’rehere for.”

“Calm down? How am I supposed to calm down?! If anything,youshould be getting upset! You’re her husband, aren’t you?!” Veltol’s face was pale.

Wilhelm put a hand to his forehead and sighed. “If you and I both lose our heads, then this world is over…”

Veltol, who was currently pacing the parlor, had accompanied Theresia to Cragrel’s estate and was now on tenterhooks wondering how the discussion was going. In addition to the guards who were permanently posted at the mansion, Wilhelm and a detachment from his squadron were there to watch over the proceedings.

Cragrel had specifically requested to meet only with the Sword Saint, no outsiders, so as the talk went on, only he and Theresia were in the room.

“Master Veltol’s fears seem uncharacteristically…founded.”

“Even if Cragrel does have it out for the Sword Saint, anything he did would just start the next war. But it wouldn’t matter, because nothing he could do would work on Theresia.”

“You’re saying nobody can defeat the Sword Saint? When you are living proof of the contrary?”

Wilhelm said nothing.

“Sincere apologies. I’m just imagining the worst-case scenario.”

Wilhelm leaned against the wall with his arms crossed. Beside him, Pivot gazed at the mansion’s garden and idly needled Wilhelm. He didn’t need Pivot’s help to think of all the things that could go wrong. Wilhelm hated the look in Pivot’s eyes, who seemed to see right through to his dark imaginings. He clicked his tongue.

“Argh, I can’t take it, Wilhelm! Let’s talk about something distracting. At this rate, I’m afraid I’ll get so worried about Theresia that I’ll charge into that room before I can stop myself and- D-did you just click your tongue at me?!”

“No, not at you, Father. A distraction, you said?”

“Yes. I know it may not be your forte. But never fear! I know a subject that will get even you smiling. Behold: talk of children!” Wilhelm remained studiously silent.

“In terms of the future of the family, one of course wishes for a boy. But as you know very well from observing Theresia, daughters are also profoundly precious. Why, it’s hard to decide between the two. What about you, what do you think? Hm?”
