Veltol was talking a little too fast, perhaps out of his desire to distract himself. It was a bit overwhelming. He was, however, clearly intent on genuinely propounding the virtues of children. Yet Wilhelm hesitated to answer, so much so that even the typically dull Veltol picked up on it.

“What’s the matter, Wilhelm?” he asked. “Could it be? Are you anxious?” He narrowed his blue eyes, and Wilhelm, to his surprise, found himself looking at the ground in spite of himself. It was an unconscious expression of anxiety, the ultimate evidence that Veltol had hit the nail on the head. It was a terribly deep-seated worry, difficult or impossible to let go of, fostered over three months of pursuing Stride with nothing to show for it.

After a long moment, Wilhelm mumbled, “Do you really think I can be a dad?”

A change came over Veltol’s voice then. “Mm. Tell me, my boy.”

Wilhelm was grateful for this heartfelt solicitousness on the part of his father-in-law, but he still couldn’t look up as he said, “The Sword Saint is Stride’s target. And she’s who Cragrel wants to talk to… I made Theresia put the sword aside, and I don’t want her to use one again. I mean to keep on protecting her with all my strength so that she never has to.”

“As her father, I find that heartening, and I’m grateful. What troubles you about such a choice?”

“Because I realized…when that baby is born, Theresia won’t be the only one I have to protect anymore. And that’s going to be an issue I’ll always have going forward.”

It was simple. When the child was born, there would be two people Wilhelm needed to keep safe. The more children he had, the more his family grew, the more family members he would have to protect-and between them, the Sword Saint and the Sword Devil had simply incurred too many grudges in this world.

“And hell, Stride doesn’t even really have a grudge against us. We’re just the perfect way for him to get what he wants. With people like that on our tails…”

“You wonder whether you can keep your family completely safe. That’s why you’re not sure if you can be a father.”

“Damn straight. I mean…uh, yes, sir.”

Having bared his heart to the utmost, Wilhelm could only hide his face for shame. He’d suppressed this anxiety for so long that now that he had let it loose, it overflowed, paralyzed him and brought him to his knees with mewling he could hardly stand to hear from himself.

And at last, he understood, truly, deeply: The fear he carried, and that of every citizen of the Kingdom, had been borne by the Sword Saint, Theresia van Astrea. She had carried that weight, and he had made her put aside her sword, even though he should have understood the burden she would take up in its place.

“Wilhelm. My Wilhelm… Grit your teeth!”

Wilhelm was busy clenching his fist at his belated realization; he never expected the howl from Veltol. His eyes widened as he saw Veltol raise his fist, and he dodged to the side. In the same motion, he grabbed Veltol’s arm and reflexively slammed him into the wall.

“Ngghaaa! Y-you dodged me?! You’re not supposed to dodge! This is your father-in-law’s knuckle!”

“Sorry. It was so sudden, I moved on instinct.”

“I’m s-so glad to have such a manly son-in-law. But please let me go, it hurts!” Tears brimmed in Veltol’s eyes.

Wilhelm released him, apologizing again-although he wasn’t sure his father-in-law should be looking at himquiteso sourly. In any case…

“I misjudged you, Wilhelm-to hear such feeble whining from you!”

“Says my teary-eyed father-I mean, no, you’re right.”

“No, I’m not! Don’t mistake me, Wilhelm!”

“Are you right or not?! Which is it?!”

No sooner had Wilhelm thought he was being exhorted to reform himself than he was told he had it all wrong. His head was spinning.

Veltol flicked him in the nose with a finger-although he had to lift his entire arm, cradling his right hand, to do it. It was the arm he had lost the use of during the struggle with Stride, and other than the healers who cared for him, the only people who knew about it were Wilhelm and Veltol himself.

“Even with my arm in this state, I’m still a father,” he said. “The blessing of the Sword Saint went to my younger brother, and I’ve never been able to do anything much worthy of the Astrea name. You can imagine how good I am with a blade.”

“I…uh, well… Yes, sir.”

“It’s not quite the same situation you and Theresia find yourselves in, but the Astrea house is nobility, and I won’t say I’ve never felt the touch of danger. Of course, I’ve asked myself whether I have the strength to protect the wife and child I love. Yet all the same, I became a father. Ask me why.”

Slowly, Wilhelm said, “Why?”

“Because my fear and anxiety were outstripped by my wish for happiness!” Veltol pulled back his hand and pounded his chest with it. The words hit Wilhelm like a gust of wind, and he swallowed heavily. He felt the same sense of shock he had when Veltol had revealed his useless right arm. Veltol had neither strength nor technique, yet without either force of arm or skill of sword, he displayed a different kind of power entirely.

“When you think of your family, you’ll find strength and courage beyond anything you normally possess-I guarantee it. And if I may say further,

Wilhelm, there is no need for you to do all of it alone.”

“No need…to do everything alone?”

“Very much so. How could two inexperienced amateurs hope to succeed as a mother and father by themselves? I urge you, rely upon those with more experience than yourself. You should know better than anyone how skilled Tishua and I have proven at raising children.” Veltol winked and grinned so broadly that Wilhelm could see every one of his teeth.

He was completely at a loss, as always. It was always like this. Wilhelm simply couldn’t win against Veltol. He was just one man, yet as a husband, as a father, Wilhelm could come nowhere near his strength.

Once again, he found himself defeated-and then, he noticed. He discovered a shaft of light piercing the gloom.

“When things get busy,” Veltol said, “you may find yourself unable to be with Theresia or your precious child. However, at those times, you must turn to me and Tishua, or to Carol. In fact, it will be a great help to me: an excuse to make the trip to the capital!”

“As long as you promise not to annoy Theresia too much, I’d welcome you,” Wilhelm replied, joking in the same vein as Veltol. He felt he had to joke, in fact, lest Veltol’s sheer wondrousness inundate him completely. Wilhelm respected Veltol as a man, but he wished he would think twice about making them see him every single day.

Only moments after Wilhelm had found himself taking fresh stock of Veltol as a man, the door of the meeting room opened, and Theresia peeked out into the lobby.

“Wilhelm? Father?” she said. “I’m sorry to keep you waiting.”

Wilhelm went to her and took her hand. “Theresia… Are you all right? How did the discussion go?”

“Yes, I’m fine. I was able to have a proper conversation with Cragrel. I think we should discuss the rest of the major details with Orfeu…” Then she blinked. “Wilhelm?” She peered at him with clear blue eyes. “What about

you? Areyouall right? Your face, it looks a little…”

“I was talking with your father. He really gave me the what-for.”

“My father did?”

“Wait, now, just wait! That’s simply not true! I didn’t givehimthe whatfor! He nearly twisted my arm off!” Veltol said, his arm hanging at his side, as Theresia frowned. She looked doubtful just the same, but Wilhelm had meant he’d been beaten spiritually rather than physically. And Veltol had enough pity in him not to mention what they had talked about.

“In any case, if Cragrel is open to talking, that’s what matters most,” Wilhelm said.

“Yes, but…Wilhelm? ‘Gave you the what-for’?” Theresia still wanted a better picture of what had happened.

“Forget about the what-for! That’s between me and your dad. I’ll pay him back in good time. What’s more important is-” Wilhelm drew her in and embraced her. Theresia’s eyes widened against his chest, and for some reason, Veltol exclaimed, “Oh, my!”

“Wh…what brought this on? I said I was all right.”

“I know. I’m the one who’s not doing so well. So lend me your strength.”

“My strength?”

“I just need to know you’re by my side, healthy and smiling.”

Theresia’s eyes wavered for a second. She didn’t understand, but after just a moment she said, “I hear you,” and hugged Wilhelm back, pressing her cheek hard to his chest.

Wilhelm could feel her belly, protruding ever so slightly, between them.

Veltol was right. It made him want to find some way to protect this new life.

As for Veltol, who had taught Wilhelm to see things that way, he was biting on his handkerchief, barely able to hold back his emotions.

6

Miklotov MacMahon was one of the kingdom’s most important bureaucrats.

Somewhere in his thirties, he’d shown a gift for administration, and ever since the Demi-human War, he’d made himself indispensable in the machinations of Lugunican politics. He’d even helped Wilhelm once, using every trick he knew to reinstate the young man with the squadron he had effectively deserted, and the two still saw a fair amount of each other now that Wilhelm had risen to lead that same unit.

Miklotov’s summons to Wilhelm was sudden and very secret. Bordeaux was still Zergev Squadron’s direct superior, so Wilhelm had never been personally summoned by Miklotov before. And this was right when Theresia had spoken with Cragrel Dawson, and when Orfeu and his “Six-Tongue” organization were busily infiltrating Demi-human Alliance remnants all over the kingdom.

Receiving this call as the knights were trying to corner Stride and his people, Wilhelm found it difficult not to feel like the wind was being taken from his sails.

Thus, Wilhelm went to Miklotov with a few choice words ready if the matter at hand wasn’t worth all this disruption, but as it turned out…

“One certainly never expected to come face-to-face with this particular person,” remarked Pivot, who was kneeling beside Wilhelm.

“Shut up,” Wilhelm snapped-but only in his head. Outwardly, he, too, knelt and maintained his silence.

“Hm,” murmured the one Wilhelm was treating with absolute respect. “Don’t stand on ceremony. Raise your head; you’re in the royal presence.”

“Thank you, sire, but it’s precisely because I am in your presence that I’m kneeling.”

“Hm, yes, I suppose you’re right about that. Pardon me. I was thinking of you like one of my bosom buddies and let myself get a touch too familiar, I suppose. You have, after all, been in my bedchamber before.”

Wilhelm almost choked, but managed, “That was an act of profound disrespect, sire. I have no words for your immense mercy.”

“Oh, please. True, I was awfully shocked, but it’s a good story these days. Trias… Or, no-it’s Astrea now, isn’t it! But, ahem, so as not to confuse you with your wife, I’ll simply call you Wilhelm.”

Before Wilhelm was a middle-aged man with distinctive crimson eyes, a composed demeanor, and a rich beard. He wore a friendly smile and nodded eagerly, clearly in high spirits. He was genuinely personable, but his rank meant Wilhelm could not simply banter with him, certainly not at this distance. He was kneeling before the current King of Lugunica, His Majesty Jionis Lugunica.

Reminded-and forgiven-afresh for his indiscretion, Wilhelm shrank inside. But he gave a good glare at the slim, long-haired man standing next to Jionis-Miklotov.

He was a delicate-looking man with intelligent green eyes and pale skin that had never known hard fighting. Yet he had more nerve than any halfcocked soldier and didn’t so much as flinch under Wilhelm’s gaze.

“Mmm… I do truly apologize for all but ambushing you here. Simply know there is a good reason for it.”

“Your Majesty has come all the way here. I know you’re not the kind to do such a thing just to pull a prank.”

Wilhelm saw now why he’d been so strictly enjoined to come to this meeting alone. His question was:Why?Jionis didn’t have to resort to sneaking to get to Wilhelm. The man could never have refused a direct summons from the king.

Moreover, Wilhelm had been called to Miklotov’s office. Why was Jionis here, and why had Wilhelm been called in Miklotov’s name?

“If I had summoned you in my own name, it would have been a whole affair, and I wished to avoid that. The matter at hand, after all, is one of great import to the kingdom, something that must be handled with immense delicacy.”

“Your Majesty…”

The king’s words were quiet but full of his authority; they had the power to make Wilhelm straighten his back even more. At last, still kneeling, he looked up and met Jionis’s gaze. In those lovely crimson eyes staring at the Sword Devil, so crucial to this kingdom, he found sorrow and a deep sense of responsibility.

The kingdom’s gossips said the current king was too idealistic and too good-hearted, that he was a man suited not for the rigors of politics but only to keep the throne warm. In this instance, however, Wilhelm knew beyond a doubt he was kneeling before a true king.

“I gather it was your wife, Theresia, who spoke to the demi-humans’ leader, Cragrel Dawson, and drew out his inmost thoughts. Though we have relieved her of her position as Sword Saint, it seems she’s been pressed into the kingdom’s service once again. I can only offer my regret at my own lack of strength.”

“I thank you, Your Majesty, but add that she wanted to do it-and insofar as it’s brought results, I can only be proud of her.”

“Mm, I am truly blessed in my subjects. You and your wife, of course, but also Miklotov here. Without him, I might never have noticed the possibility.”

Wilhelm frowned at Jionis’s stern demeanor and looked at Miklotov. The administrator gave the slightest nod and said, “Here.” He put something on the table: a single scroll. There seemed to be something written on it.

“This is a copy of the Dragon Tablet,” Miklotov said.

Wilhelm’s eyes widened. “What?!”

The Dragon Tablet was one of the great treasures of the Dragonfriend Kingdom of Lugunica, an artifact left by the Holy Dragon to warn the kingdom of disasters yet to come. It predicted events that would shake the nation, and its location was a closely guarded secret. Only the king’s personal guard, who swore fealty and whose actions and bloodlines were free of any blemish, were permitted to know where it was, let alone see it.

Copying the contents of the stone was, needless to say, not permitted.

“What is such a thing doing here?” Wilhelm said.

“That, my good Wilhelm, is precisely the issue. Normally, the copying of the text of the Dragon Tablet would be unthinkable. That stele is held more valuable than almost anything else in the kingdom. Only those chosen for the king’s guard should be handling it, and then only with the greatest care.

However…”

“However…what?”

“Immediately after Lord Cragrel agreed to aid us, one of the guards went missing. This copy was found in his chambers.”

When Wilhelm looked closer, he saw that the copy was incomplete. The guard had probably failed to dispose of a version that lacked the entire text. This naturally invited the suspicion there was a complete copy somewhere.

“Why would anyone copy the Tablet?” Wilhelm asked. “No, that’s obvious-to show it to someone. But I thought the king’s guard were chosen according to the strictest conditions. You’re saying one of them betrayed the kingdom?”

“You’re right,” Miklotov replied, “no one of unknown background is invited to serve in the guard. But we’ve had word, you see, that the ones currently sowing discord in our kingdom can neutralize even the greatest reserves of loyalty and devotion.” Miklotov looked dour.

“One of Stride’s rings, huh?” Wilhelm hit on the same possibility as Miklotov and nodded.

With Stride’s “Ten Proud Commandments,” it was a simple matter to make even a member of the royal guard dance in his palm. It seemed now he had refrained from using the power of his rings within the kingdom, other than at first to establish his safe houses, so that he could save them for this bold stroke.

“So he used a member of the royal guard to get a look at the stele? But why would he want-”

“To abolish the pact,” Jionis said.

For a moment, Wilhelm’s mind went blank. He couldn’t process the meaning of the words.

At last, slowly, he began to digest what the king had said.

To abolish the pact. In other words, to break the vow that had been sworn between the Kingdom and the Holy Dragon.

“My advisor has informed me they suspect the enemy’s aim is to strip the nation of the Dragon’s protection and thereby leave it totally defenseless.”

“Your advisor, sire?” Wilhelm, who hadn’t heard that expression before, looked from the king to Miklotov and back. Miklotov would seem to be the natural choice for such a position, but from the way the king spoke, it seemed it was not Miklotov he had referred to.

“You must pardon me,” Jionis said, “but there are reasons I cannot reveal this person’s identity. But they come nothing short of Miklotov for sheer quick-wittedness. Sounds wonderful, doesn’t it?”

“You give me too much credit, Your Majesty,” Miklotov replied. “Might I ask you to tell us further what this advisor has said?”

“But of course. They inform me that in order to counter the actual measures required to abolish the pact, we must find a certain place.”

“A certain place, sire? But where?”

“Shamrock Valley.”

At that, Wilhelm’s breath caught-how many times this was, he’d lost count. He felt like the king had done nothing but surprise him since that initial “ambush,” but this place-name was the biggest shock of all.

Shamrock Valley: the place believed to be the very headquarters of the

Demi-human Alliance. It was the location its three leaders, back before Cragrel, had called home. For there…

“In that valley where the miasma hangs thick, the Witch who was the kingdom’s great enemy is said to have left her legacy-and now, the text of the Tablet has been stolen. Wilhelm, this is a task I can ask only of you and your troops: Go to Shamrock Valley and bring back the Witch’s legacy.”

7

“If those are King Jionis’s orders, you’ll have to follow them… But… Shamrock Valley?”

“First Cragrel Dawson, now the Witch’s legacy. Even with the civil war over, one thing after another keeps coming back to remind us of it. This may all be part of one great cycle of woe.”

“Please don’t talk about Cragrel that way. I think it’s good I spoke to him.” Theresia helped Wilhelm into a white overshirt.

He put his arms through the sleeves, grateful for his wife’s assistance, and said, “Sorry.”

It was the day after Miklotov had summoned him for the surprise audience with Jionis. Wilhelm had promptly reorganized his unit and was preparing to head to Shamrock Valley. He couldn’t tell anyone the details of what Jionis had said, not even Theresia.

“All I can say is the current situation might be about to change in a big way. Unless things really go south, I think the castle should be safe, but be extra careful. I asked Miklotov to bulk up your personal guard.”

“Thank you… I’m afraid that if His Majesty gets wind of this, my own guards might start butting in, telling me I should focus on my pregnancy!”

Theresia stuck out her tongue (she was joking, sort of), but Wilhelm met her with silence. When he had agreed to the Shamrock Valley plan, he had asked the castle to protect Theresia and her parents. For Jionis had said the same thing then.

“Very perceptive, indeed. In fact, I should say that in this case, our King’s good reputation has paid off,” Pivot murmured from the edge of Wilhelm’s vision. Wilhelm pointedly ignored the phantom and sighed.

Whatever the case, with Jionis’s blessing, Theresia and her family would stay at Lugunica Castle while Wilhelm and his unit were away. With nothing to keep him looking back over his shoulder, all that remained was to return with success.

“That Roswaal, she’s been obsessed with this ‘Witch.’ She’s never been able to investigate inside the mist before, so I’m sure she’s working her buns off now that we can go in there.”

“Roswaal will be there? I’m sure she’s trustworthy, then.”

“Trustworthy? Not sure I like putting it that way.”

Nonetheless, what Wilhelm felt for Roswaal was not different from the faith he placed in Grimm, Bordeaux, or his other comrades-in-arms. If anything, given she was the first person he had turned to in his effort to discover what Stride and his minions were up to, one could say he trusted Roswaal more than anyone else-though he never imagined when they first met, they would have such a relationship.

“’Course, it’s not just Roswaal. It was the same with your buddy Carol… Heck, Grimm and Bordeaux and Conwood and the rest, too.”

“And me, right?”

Wilhelm paused. “Yeah. I thought I’d gotten involved with some weirdo flower girl.”

“Well, I never knew my husband was so rude!” With laughter in her voice, Theresia hugged Wilhelm from behind. She wrinkled the uniform he had just put on-not that he was boorish enough to point it out. At the same time, he felt Theresia’s warmth and the life within her pressed up against him.

Only a short while before, that warmth had not inspired anticipation and joy so much as anxiety and dread. But now…

“Go do your job, Dad. That’s what the baby in my belly is saying.”

“I sure will. And what areyousaying to me?”

“That it’s probably pointless to tell you not to push yourself…so just come home safe.” “Huh?”

He grinned in spite of himself, a smile of admiration and appreciation. Truly, Wilhelm realized, his wife knew him better than he knew himself.

When Wilhelm and Theresia came downstairs into the front hall of the mansion, they found Grimm, who had come to fetch Wilhelm, in the middle of a painful goodbye with Carol.

“Grimm, please be safe,” Carol said sweetly, giving him a hug. Naturally, the curse activated, and the dark bruises appeared on her neck, but she held the embrace for as long as she could, praying for her loved one’s safety and fighting the curse. Surely the man who had placed the spell, Stride himself, would have scowled to see it.

“My, but you doooo push yourself too hard. I’m blushing just watching you…perhapsI’munder a spell?”

“I’m sorry you had to see it, then,” Carol replied. “Lady Mathers… Please, look after Grimm and the others.”

“You may reeeest assured.”

For some reason, it was Roswaal who received Carol’s somewhat breathless request. When Roswaal noticed Wilhelm giving her a dubious look, she simply shrugged.

Wilhelm chose to ignore her and Carol, instead walking up beside Grimm.

“You ready?”

“Aamn right!” came the raspy and brief, but enthusiastic reply.

Wilhelm nodded. He cast a glance at everyone who had come to see them off: Theresia and Carol, Veltol and Tishua. He looked around at his family and sighed.

Family… Yes, they were family. Something important that Wilhelm must and would protect with his sword.

“Father, look after Theresia and the others,” he said.

“Hrm? Oh! Yes, of course-you need not even ask. I’ll do my duty and do it with pride!” Veltol said. Wilhelm had deliberately made the request of him among all those staying behind-it was his reply to their exchange of the other day, and Veltol understood him very well.

The family witnessed this vow between a seasoned father and a father in training, and then-

“All right. We’ll be back,” Wilhelm said.

“Yes, and we’ll be waiting. Go do what you have to do, for your family’s sake,” Theresia said.

Those words of parting proved to Wilhelm that Theresia felt just as he did, and he was flooded once more with the sense that he was impossibly lucky to have chosen her-or, no…that she had found him. With that feeling filling his heart, he set off.

They had no way of knowing that it would be the last time this family was all together.

CHAPTER 4: ACT IV

Colors of Mist

1

The valley lay cloaked in mist all year round, a miasma made of fouled mana. A great many demon beasts made their home there, drawn by the miasma- this was Shamrock Valley.

At the height of the Demi-human War, the kingdom’s army, having suffered a series of defeats at the hands of the Alliance, heard-mainly by rumor-that Shamrock Valley was the base of the Alliance’s three principal leaders: Valga Cromwell, the master strategist; Libre Fermi, the Viper; and Sphinx, the Witch.

However, the vile environment provided the valley with a natural barrier, leaving the army no way to verify the rumors. Consequently, after the end of the war, there was little need to explore the place and it was quickly forgotten. Stories were all the kingdom knew of the place.

Now, Zergev Squadron was to go charging into this deadly vale on the king’s own orders…

“I’ve got to say…it’s a bit of a letdown,” murmured Pivot, clasping his hands behind his back and looking around.

There was indeed a thick mist hovering everywhere, but it was nowhere near as bad as the tales had said. They had claimed the fog was so thick that you couldn’t see your outstretched hand in front of your face. In fact, the mist was thin enough that they could actually make out the terrain.

It would be hasty, however, to suggest that this meant Shamrock Valley had not deserved its reputation. Otherwise, how could one explain how often the valley had rebuffed the attempts of survey teams to explore it?

Zergev Squadron was quickly proving what it had over and above any survey team.

“To think, they were fooled by suuuuch a simple trick,” said Roswaal, narrowing her multicolored eyes. In her hand she held a magic crystal that glowed with mysterious light. The stone had been placed discreetly in the valley’s walls and was what had caused this place to be feared as terra incognita.

“That thing’s practically a pebble. That’s what keeps the mist or miasma or whatever trapped here?” Conwood asked.

“I should say so,” Roswaal replied. “And the really nasty thing about a ploy like this is that it escapes even someone with your perception, my dear Conwood. Ugh…I do curse my own shortcomings.”

“You have shortcomings, Lady Mathers? Surely, you jest.” Conwood grinned and shrugged, then ordered his men to keep searching. Roswaal watched him go.

“Hey,” Wilhelm called to her. “You don’t usually go getting all depressed. Now’s not the time to start. We’re counting on you-do your damn job.”

“Ah, you say I look to you like a woman wounded? I wiiiish you’d pick kinder words, then, and soothe me with your compassion.”

“You beat the Witch. That’s all you need to remember,” Wilhelm said brusquely.

Roswaal’s eyes widened, but then she smiled. Wilhelm was not aware of all the nuances of fate that had brought Roswaal and the witch Sphinx together. He knew only that Roswaal had despised Sphinx and sworn to destroy her-and had succeeded.

It was fruitless, then, to feel defeated by the Witch’s parting gift.

“She planted magic crystals around the pass, which produce a current of magic that fills the valley with mist and miasma. To enter or leave, one must move the stones in a prescribed order,” Roswaal said.

“What happens if you get it wrong?”

“Presumably, it rapidly causes the miasma to thicken. It may make you sick or, in a worst-case scenario, lose consciousness. The miasma would

attract many beasts and, well, there’s the end of your story.”

“Okay. You’re right, that is a nasty trick.” Wilhelm frowned and looked around the valley. The miasma might not be as dense as he’d feared, but the danger of it still hung thick in the air. He narrowed his blue eyes.

In his opinion, Roswaal was wrong to think of herself as defeated. It was like saying she’d “lost” because she’d failed to spot stones buried in the ground that no one had known about, in a place she wasn’t familiar with. That wasn’t even a fair fight. He’d only learned of them because-

“I must say, Idowonder who this counselor of King Jionis’s is,” Roswaal mused.

“He said there’s a reason he can’t tell us. Don’t try to sniff them out.”

“I’m not sniffing. But a person’s free to wonder, aren’t they? One might think, for example, that the Kingdom of Lugunica is secretly communicating with the Sage.”

“The Sage? You mean the Sage Shaula of the Three Heroes?” Wilhelm’s eyes went wide. Of everything he had thought Roswaal might say, he hadn’t expected that.

The Three Heroes were people of legend who had sealed away the Witch of Jealousy when the world had stood on the brink of destruction. The Sage was one of them, along with the Sword Saint and the Holy Dragon.

The power and influence of House Astrea, the line of the Sword Saints, and the Holy Dragon Volcanica went without saying. The Sage Shaula, the

third of the Heroes, was of course deeply revered as well…

“But word is the Sage stays shut up in a tower in the east of the kingdom and never lets anyone get close,” Wilhelm said. “So why would you bring them up?”

“Did I not say? The communication is seeeecret. It’s true that supposedly no one has been into the tower in hundreds of years, but say the royal family and the Sage each possessed a conversation mirror? They might juuuust be able to get some advice from the Sage, who can allegedly see everything in the world…”

“So they’d have the Dragon Tablet to grant the protection of the Dragon, the Sage’s advice, and the Sword Saint’s power in case anything happened?

That would cover all the bases.”

“And that is, I dare say, exaaaactly how Lugunica looks to other nations.” That brought Wilhelm up short; he had glibly said that to be sarcastic.

The Holy Dragon, the Sword Saint, and the Sage-the glory of those longago saviors continued to protect the kingdom, overshadowing the deeds of those who lived and fought now, like him and his comrades. That state of affairs did not amuse him.

But amusing or not, it was the truth of the Kingdom of Lugunica at this moment.

“Without the pact with the Dragon, it’s unimaginable our neighbors would have stood quietly by as we exhausted ourselves with the Demi-human War,” said Pivot, voicing Wilhelm’s thoughts.

“Yeah. The Empire, at least, would have tried to take a swing at us,” he said with a nod and a scowl.

Ironically, it was because no other state dared intervene that the Demihuman War had dragged on for almost ten years. Might the conflict have ended sooner if another nation had made a move on Lugunica? It was impossible to say.

“It’s onlyspeculationon my part,” Roswaal said. “A baseless assumption. But I can think of no one else who could see through the valley’s trap. Or… well. Not quitenoone else, but…”

“But what?”

Roswaal put a thoughtful finger to her lips. “They couldn’t possibly see more than the Sage. Forget I said anything.” Granted they couldn’t hope for a certain answer to the question, it still frustrated Wilhelm to simply have the subject dropped like that.

Before he could pursue the point, however, someone shouted from deep within the mist. One of Wilhelm’s soldiers.

“A hut! It’s the Alliance’s…the Witch’s safe house! We found it!” Wilhelm and Roswaal looked at each other.

“Iielm!” Grimm called.

“I know! I’m on my way!”

Wilhelm and Roswaal headed toward the voices on the far side of the mist, walking faster and faster as they came ever closer to discovering the demihumans’ base that had eluded them for so many years.

And then…

“So at last we come face-to-face,” Roswaal said, standing with Wilhelm and gazing at the building. The touch of emotion in her voice proved she felt the same way he did about it. All that time ago they had been locked in mortal combat-and now, they sought their enemies’ place of hiding, so as to find an escape from the encroaching danger those same foes had left behind.

If this was not fortune most foul, what was it?

Grimm stood before the hut, nodding as Wilhelm and Roswaal came up. Conwood and the rest of the squadron had taken up positions around the building, ready for a fight.

“It might be a trap,” Wilhelm said. “What’s the plan? I can take point if you want.”

“Sadly, I can’t let the man with the pregnant wife expose himself to such danger,” Roswaal said lightly, going ahead of him. Wilhelm drew his sword and followed.

The hut had harbored the Witch herself. Anything might be waiting for them. Roswaal reached out slowly and let her fingers brush the door. “No sign of magical traps,” she said. “I’m going to open it now.”

This she did, pushing the door ajar. Wilhelm slipped past her and into the building. He scanned the place closely but concluded that, just as Roswaal said, there didn’t appear to be any booby traps inside.

“I see. It might not look like much, but I believe this really was the Alliance’s brain trust,” said Roswaal, studying the walls by the shining light of a portable candlestick she carried. Wilhelm followed her gaze to see a large map posted on the wall-an enlarged depiction of the lands of Lugunica.

Several places bore red marks.

He remembered them all. They were all sites of major battles from the Demi-human War.

“They even have Aihiya Swamp,where I died. And Castour Field and Redonas Plateau, too.”

Wilhelm hesitated only a moment, ignoring Pivot’s grim remark. “If we’re sure, that’s what counts. The only question is whether we’ll find what we’re looking for.”

“The Witch’s legacy, as His Majesty would put it.Sphinx’slegacy,” said Roswaal. From the way she pointedly rephrased it, Wilhelm took it that she didn’t like referring to Sphinx as a Witch, but he chose not to point that out. Instead, he looked around.

The hut contained more than just the map. There were also bookshelves with a handful of books on them, as well as other shelves containing tools whose use and niceties he didn’t know. It was eerie, the way they were just left sitting there.

“Think we should tear down the walls or pull up the floor?” Wilhelm asked. “Conwood could do it with no trouble.”

“No, Sphinx was a rationalist. She’d already set up a way of preventing people from entering the valley. I don’t think she would hide the results of her own explorations here in the hut. If we can just find any materials we could take with us…”

Roswaal, who had reached out to examine one of the tools on the shelf, abruptly stopped talking.

Wilhelm was not off his guard enough to let that pass. He noticed immediately, turning to her and demanding, “What is it?”

Roswaal was staring fixedly, not at the implement in her hand, but at the shelf itself. “No one is supposed to have been here since Sphinx was

defeated…so why isn’t there more dust? It’s almost like-”

“Like someone’s been here recently. Like they left a trace.” Wilhelm, immediately reaching the same conclusion as Roswaal, frowned hard.

At that exact moment, there was a raucous commotion outside. He heard someone curse.

With a gasp, Wilhelm shot back to the entrance to find out what was going on. He saw what was wrong immediately. At some point, a thick mist had enshrouded the area around the hut.

No question about it-this was the Witch’s parting gift they had so feared. Or no, rather, a trap laid by the walking disasterusingthe Witch’s parting gift to take care of anyone who might come creeping after him.

Noticing the change in the flow of the wind as well as a sense of despondency in his own heart at the thickening miasma, Wilhelm took the handkerchief that had been tied around his arm and wrapped it around his mouth instead.

“Grimm! Conwood! Form up the unit! This will attract the beasts!”

“!”

“Roger that!” came Grimm and Conwood’s answers.

“Roswaal! Grab whatever you need! We’re out of time. Only bring what you can carry into the mist. It’s your call what we should take!”

Without waiting for an answer, Wilhelm dived outside. He rejoined Zergev Squadron, which was quickly forming itself up in the dense fog, and, with a silver flash of his sword, immediately cut down an approaching shape.

The shape gave a high-pitched cry and bounced along the ground. It turned out to be a massive bat with a horn growing out of its head-a black-winged mouse. It was only the first of many of the valley’s demon beasts that Wilhelm could feel bearing down on them, making his hair stand on end.

He would have to take them out one after another, trying to protect Roswaal as they attempted to bring home the Witch’s legacy.

What was more…

“If you’re behind this, Stride, I know this won’t be the end of it!”

Wilhelm gritted his teeth, stood with his sword at the ready, and flung himself at the oncoming beasts. Grimm stood beside him with his giant shield, using it to smash in the creatures’ faces.

Wilhelm didn’t have to look over at his old friend as they rushed into battle, didn’t have to see the tension written on his face to know. Grimm and Wilhelm fought in the shadow of the same fear: that a new disaster waited for whoever survived.

2

Wilhelm and Grimm’s fears came true, and in the worst way possible.

It was all very sudden.

With Wilhelm and his companions gone, Theresia and her family accepted His Majesty Jionis’s hospitality and took up residence at Lugunica Castle. There were no words to express the gratitude they felt at the warm welcome that Theresia with her child and Carol with her curse received.

While Wilhelm was away, Veltol and Tishua would stay in the royal capital the entire time, adding themselves to the guards already supplied at the castle. Veltol, having been personally entrusted with Theresia’s safety by Wilhelm, was particularly on tenterhooks and seemed to be spinning his wheels even more energetically than usual.

Even so, Theresia was grateful to have him there. It kept her from getting lost in anxiety and tension, and above all, it put a real smile on the face of Carol, who was so eager not to look depressed.

The girls could chuckle over Veltol’s silly antics, or make merry watching how Tishua got her husband to dance in the palm of her hand, or enjoy chatting together about the future of the child who would soon be born.

They were passing the time in just such an idle and pleasant way when a man appeared cloaked in a terrible aura.

“I can’t say it measures up to the Empire’s Crystal Palace, but this castle is admittedly rather nicer than I had expected. Even if one does hear that it was nearly destroyed by the savagery of the demi-humans. You did well to save it. I give you my praise.”

It was with this condescending announcement that the man, uninvited, strode proudly down the castle’s crimson carpet.

The air in the parlor froze, filled with the tension of battle. Lugunica Castle was naturally the most secure place in the capital-indeed, in the nation. How had this interloper-the man everyone in the kingdom was more on alert for than anyone else-simply waltzed in?

“Stride…,” Theresia said in a strained voice when she saw him.

At the sound of his name, the man, Stride, narrowed his black eyes, and a cruel smile crossed his lips. “It’s been far too long, Sword Saint. Have you been in good health?”

“What did you do to the guards outside?” Theresia asked.

“Hmph. You stand inmypresence, yet the first thing you think about is the safety of the common mob? Such disrespect would normally merit the most severe punishment…but at the moment, I’m in a good mood. Thus, I shall be magnanimous enough to overlook this infraction.”

Theresia shut her eyes tight against his cruel, arrogant response. It did not answer her question, but Stride’s indifferent attitude said everything she needed to know about the fate of her guards.

The civil war that had nearly cost them everything had finally ended, and the kingdom had begun to rebuild its peace ever so slowly-so why…?

“How can you be so cruel to others?”

“A striking question from the Sword Saint who has claimed thousands, if not tens of thousands, of victims of her own. My deeds are like a child’s trifles compared with the number of livesyouhave taken. If body count is the way we determine guilt, there can be no one in this world to surpass you.” Theresia gasped.

Once again, Stride’s answer was tangent to her question, but his words cut deep. It was the razor-sharp anguish that had continued to torment her ever since the war had ended, since she had been permitted to put down her sword, since she had conceived a child with Wilhelm.

Stride had a natural gift for driving cutting words into his opponent’s weakest points, then pouring poison in the wound.

Theresia may have lost her voice, but there was one who hadn’t, and she burst out in a rage. “You cur! That’s the last time you humiliate Lady Theresia!” Carol thundered, drawing the knight’s sword she had earlier set aside. She stretched out an arm, ushering Theresia back toward Veltol and Tishua, and stepped out in front of them. Theresia’s parents huddled around her protectively, facing Stride head-on.

“Hmph,” Stride sniffed, sounding bored. What was the basis for his attitude? He stood alone against them.

“Carol, watch out for the shadows,” Theresia said. “Wilhelm says he has shinobi…”

“I’m aware, milady. I know this man has not the strength to break through the castle guard on his own. He’s a coward who’s forever controlling others, mocking them, trampling them underfoot.”

Vigilant of the shadows, Carol edged forward, pressing Stride into a space she could control. Her sword had excellent reach, and her technique bore the approval of the Sword Saint herself. And just like before, she sensed from Stride the life force of a man who was wracked with illness.

Naturally, if Eight-Arms were to appear, the situation would turn around in an instant…

“Sadly, however, he is too large to travel in the shadows. There’s no need for such caution. You won’t see him here. I have prepared other amusements.”

“Amusements! We won’t play your little games! But wewillmake you spit out everything you know! What are you planning? How did you get into the castle?!” Carol demanded.

“Listen to you squawk, girl. Have these long days without the intimacy of the man you love weighed on you so?”

“You son of a-!”

Stride simply crossed his arms as his venom brought Carol to the height of fury.

It was then that she received a further bolster to her strength: reinforcements.

“Lady Astrea! Are you all right?!”

Two members of the castle guard, sensing something amiss, burst into the room and immediately grasped what was happening. They leveled their weapons at the intruder, Stride, in tandem with Carol, who stood at the ready. “Prepare yourself!” Carol cried, and armed antagonists launched themselves at Stride from three directions at once.

Carol could boast excellent swordsmanship, as could the two castle guards. And in all the time Theresia had known her, this stroke, she thought, was the most perfect Carol had ever delivered. Her sword came up in a glorious half-circle to strike Stride down-and it would have.

“…Wha?”

A small gasp escaped Carol the moment she unleashed this flawless strike.

She would have known, of course, at least intellectually; she would have understood that in order to get Stride to tell them his plans, they would have to render him powerless, bind him, and leave him alive.

Yet her sword was slick with blood. It had plunged without mercy into its target, taking the life of its victim.

Except…its victim was not the kingdom’s great enemy, the walking disaster Stride.

Lying there in a pool of blood, brought down by Carol’s sword stroke, were the two guards.

“C…Carol?” Theresia said, stunned.

“I… Wh…what? What have I-why-?” Carol lowered her sword, as amazed as Theresia. Her hand was stained with the blood that ran down her blade, and crimson flecks stippled her pale face.

She had cut down her own reinforcements with superbly honed skill. It could not possibly have been by her own volition. And if it wasn’t, then…

“I believe you asked me what happened to the guards outside,” said Stride, who had not so much as flinched while this tragedy unfolded. As perhaps the one person in the room who understood why this chaos was transpiring, he didn’t spare a glance for the dead soldiers or for Carol, but looked steadily at Theresia as he spoke.

It took her an instant to understand what he was saying-that he was referring to the first question she had asked.

Stride saw by the flicker in her eyes that she had made the connection, whereupon he jerked his chin and said, “This woman.”

“You…”

“You wished to know how I entered the castle. It was this woman.” “N-no… I would never…,” stammered Carol.

“I told you I had prepared other amusements. None other than this woman.”

As he offered these revelations, Stride unfolded his arms and showed them his left hand. The burgundy ring on his thumb glowed with an eerie light.

It was one of the Ten Proud Commandments, the spells Stride possessed-

the Burgundy Thumb.

“This business of you choking whenever you touched your beloved was merely a distraction. This goes for many, not just you lot-but people are far too certain of how deep they think another’s malice runs.”

In Stride’s murmur, Theresia almost thought she could catch a note of incomprehensible sadness. It was an emotion she had never expected of him, so much so that she almost hoped she had misheard.

She was never given the opportunity to find out the truth-for with a rasping gasp, Carol turned.

She wore an expression of shock, at odds with the way she moved. It was as if she had lost all control over everything below her neck. She raised her sword in a fighting stance of genuine beauty.

“The time is now ripe,” Stride said. “Be proud: I did not hold as much of the advantage as you lot seemed to think.”

“Please, stop her!” Theresia begged, her hand on her swelling belly as she looked at Carol.

The walking disaster, however, merely laughed. It was a cruel sound, his lips opening in a jagged smile as he said, “Do it.”

The ring on Stride’s thumb glowed even brighter than before.

Carol, who knew more acutely than anyone what that order meant, bellowed, “No… No! No, no, no, noooooo!”

Her eyes were open wide, and tears streamed from them, yet her body acted against her will, wielding without mercy the swordsmanship she had spent her life perfecting.

She turned her blade upon her family, the ones she had vowed to protect from the moment she had first taken up the sword.

“Ruuuuuun!” she shouted, and in the next instant, she lunged at them.

Theresia began to move on instinct. At almost the same time, she felt the child shifting in her womb, and it caused her to hesitate for a beat. She stopped, but Tishua shoved her aside.

Theresia fell to the ground, her mother protecting her. She saw Veltol stand in front of his wife, his arms spread wide.

Her mother offered Theresia her embrace, her father his back as they sought to protect their daughter, standing before the stroke of the sword.

“Noooooooo!”

With that cry and a silver flash, a deep red flower of blood bloomed in Lugunica Castle.

3

Zergev Squadron returned to the castle only after it was all over.

Wilhelm and the others had banded together to survive Sphinx the Witch’s trap, which Stride and his people had exploited to attack them. He and Grimm had led the way as they sliced through the horde of demon beasts, but it remained a miracle that everyone had come back alive.

“There are casualties, but I think only Zergev Squadron could have produced this result. Even though it’s now out of the young man’s hands…

Very impressive work.”

Was Pivot trying to comfort or encourage him? Either way, Willhelm twisted his lips. “They pulled the rug completely out from under us; we just happened to jump. If Roswaal doesn’t find something worthwhile in those materials we brought back, it’ll be as good as if we never went.”

Stride and his people always seem to have the initiative. If Shamrock Valley turned out to be a wild goose chase, the kingdom seemed likely to burn, unable to strike back against the malice of one man, this walking disaster. But…

“It won’t turn out like that. I promise you, I will figure out what he’s planning,” Roswaal said firmly.

“Thanks. I’ll need you to.”

They were in a dragon carriage, where Roswaal was studying the Witch’s legacy-or more precisely, the papers that might or might not have anything to do with the legacy.

In Roswaal’s focus and determination, Wilhelm thought he could sense something more than just the fact this situation had brought them up against the Witch, something more than anger at the way they had walked right into the trap in the valley. Something sterner and more stubborn hidden behind all that.

Whatever it was, he would have to rely on Roswaal to find the truth behind the plans of this supra-rational strategist.

“Don’t lose your cool…,” Wilhelm muttered.

He gritted his teeth, feeling frustrated and powerless in the face of any problem he couldn’t solve with his sword. Sitting beside him in the carriage was Grimm, who looked at the floor, appearing to feel just as pathetic.

He’d fought side by side with Wilhelm against the demon beasts, doing his share of the smashing, yet there wasn’t a scratch on him. Somewhere along the line, he’d become a man to rely on. These days, he might even be able to give their old captain, “Mad Dog” Bordeaux, a run for his money in a fight.

In short, here sat two men, the Sword Devil, who had relieved the Sword Saint of her title, and a man who could go toe to toe with the Mad Dog. “And yet look at us. I always hate this.”

“…Wanna see…Arrol,” Grimm whispered despairingly.

“Yeah, I hear you,” Wilhelm said softly.

Each of them wanted to be reunited with their beloved: Grimm with Carol, Wilhelm with Theresia. It only added an extra sting to their sense of helplessness. The wish might even have sharpened their feelings of shame, but still they refused to give it up.

They held that wish close as Zergev Squadron returned to the capital of Lugunica.

“I’m so sorry. I stayed here in the capital, and they still stole a march on me.” Bordeaux’s voice was racked with sorrow, and his huge shoulders heaved as he bowed his head as deeply as he could.

A thought passed unbidden through Wilhelm’s mind, that recently it seemed all he had seen of Bordeaux was the huge man apologizing for things he had been unable to do. No sooner had the thought occurred to him, however, than he cut down that part of himself that was trying to escape reality with this distraction and took Bordeaux by the shoulders.

Amid the uproar that had consumed Lugunica Castle, he interrogated Bordeaux Zergev.

“What happened?!” he demanded, shaking the other man vigorously.

“There was an intruder in the castle,” Bordeaux said. “I have to assume he had someone on the inside. He killed three guards and then escaped…and he took Theresia with him.”

“T-Theresia’s been kidnapped?!” Wilhelm was all but speechless as Bordeaux could only look at him in agony.

This was the worst of the worst of the worst of his imaginings. When he thought of Theresia’s face at the moment they’d parted, of the life growing in her belly, Wilhelm broke out in a cold sweat. For an instant, he felt blind fury swell within him, but he managed to push it back down just before he exploded.

“Wha… A…arroll?” Grimm asked in his rasping voice, his face at least as pale as Wilhelm’s. Only when he saw it did Wilhelm finally stop to consider how his companion must be feeling.

If the castle had been attacked and Theresia had been kidnapped, then Carol would certainly have been involved as well.

However, the facts, as it turned out, were beyond Wilhelm’s darkest expectations.

“She was taken with Theresia,” Bordeaux said, but then corrected himself. “No, I should be more precise. Carol Remendes killed the guards and abducted Theresia in tandem with the enemy leader.”

“Wha…what?”

“It was the spell from that ring that we’ve been worried about for so long. The surviving guards all testified that she shouted for everyone to run with tears streaming down her face. She didn’t have control over her own body. It was… It was a tragedy.”

At that, Wilhelm simply had no words.

He had misjudged the true strength of Stride’s Ten Proud Commandments.

They had threatened to consume Veltol’s life, placed terrible shackles upon Carol, and presumably put a similar spell upon someone in the royal guard. He’d suspected that had the power to take a life or force people to do what

Stride commanded them. It would certainly explain how someone had been induced to make a copy of the Dragon Tablet. But he had been wrong.

He knew Carol-knew she would never offer up Theresia to save her own life. If that was a choice, she would sooner cut off her own head. If even Carol, then, had been forced to obey, it meant Stride’s rings simply made puppets of others.

Carol had no choice but to do Stride’s bidding, helping him enter the castle against her will, helping him achieve his goals, killing the guards, assisting him in Theresia’s abduction…

“Wait,” Wilhelm said. “I understand Theresia’s been kidnapped, and that Stride took Carol with him so that he could use her. But… But Theresia’s parents were in the castle, too.”

While Wilhelm was away, Veltol and Tishua were supposed to be with Theresia and Carol. They had promised to do whatever they could to be of help to their beloved daughter and the young woman who was like a second daughter to them. They must have been here.

Veltol… Wilhelm had entrusted Theresia to Veltol.

“I couldn’t be here, so I asked them,” he said. “I asked them to stay by

Theresia’s side. And her mother and father promised that they would…”

He knew beyond a shadow of a doubt that they would have kept their promise at any cost.

Wilhelm felt his face tense as he faltered through the words. His heart pounded in his ears, and the sweat that poured down his back made the way he had perspired earlier seem mild.

The same panic and tension that consumed other members of House Trias, the blood that flowed in Wilhelm’s veins, when they sensed danger now swallowed him as well.

Bordeaux looked at Wilhelm, then closed his eyes, and finally said without preamble, “Wilhelm, listen to me. Lord Veltol Astrea is…” Wilhelm couldn’t speak.

“He was a credit to his kingdom, one of its proudest knights…and a great father.”

4

On the bed sat a woman who looked far removed from how she usually did.

She had flaxen hair with gentle ripples, an amply curved body clad in a dress the color of new grass. She had an ageless beauty that made her look younger than her years, and on her face had always hovered an inscrutable smile.

Now, though, her lips were drawn taut, her cheeks were pale, and her hair had lost its luster. It was a sad sight. She sat clad in nightclothes, looking listless and limp. In her eyes was the expression of one lost in a dream.

Perhaps she really was dreaming-dreaming of bliss with her husband and daughter.

“Mother? It’s Wilhelm. I just got back. I can’t apologize enough that I wasn’t here sooner.”

Tishua made no reply.

“I heard about what happened while I was away. Father… Your husband… I just don’t know what to say. It’s horrible.”

Wilhelm knelt before his silent mother-in-law, ruing the vanity of his own words.

All he could come up with were hackneyed phrases and worn-out expressions. If he really wanted to express his gratitude, if he really wanted to draw near to Tishua’s heart, why couldn’t he find something better to say?

A shame. A tragedy.The words seemed hopelessly inadequate when he thought of Veltol Astrea.

As Wilhelm bowed his head, Tishua only gazed into the distance, her eyes empty.

Her husband had been cut down and her daughter had been abducted before her eyes. According to Bordeaux, Tishua had no wounds as such, but no matter what was said to her, she wouldn’t react. She was like an empty shell.

Wounds of the heart were not like those of the body. Could it be the Tishua’s heart and mind would never again- “Wha? Mother?”

Wilhelm abruptly looked up, sensing something above him. Gently, ever so gently, fingers found their way into his hair, the color of burnt tea, and stroked his head, as if comforting a child. The hand belonged to none other than Tishua, lost in her personal abyss, and as Wilhelm watched in amazement, the light returned to her eyes.

“Wilhelm…,” she said slowly.

“Mother! I mean…Lady Tishua, do you recognize me?”

“Yes, of course I do. I just… I’m sorry you had to see me like that.” Tishua blinked several times, then looked down as if embarrassed. Wilhelm shook his head, then turned and nodded to the guards, indicating they should summon a healer.

“Someone will come to help you right away, Mother. I know you don’t have any physical injuries, but-”

“No. Wilhelm, no. Don’t go to that kind of trouble. I’m all right. There’s something more important. Something I must tell you.”

Wilhelm gasped, caught off guard by the force of her words. She put a hand on his shoulder, and while the strength of it startled him, what held his attention more than anything was Tishua’s eyes.

Over the distinctive hazel color floated a faint patina of crimson light. It was the same kind of terrible spell that had consumed Veltol’s life. Tishua hadn’t lost herself from shock. She’d been left to serve as a messenger to Wilhelm.

But Tishua realized it, and from within the compulsion of the spell, she began to assert her own will, telling him, “I’m sure I don’t have to tell you exactly how I feel about that man. He forced Carol into murders she never wanted to commit and made off with Theresia. He is a menace, a disaster, and

someone that can’t be allowed to remain in this world.” Wilhelm was absolutely silent.

“You must destroy him, I tell you. This is his message to you: ‘I await at the place of the Silver Flower Dance.’”

It almost sounded like a line of poetry. The Silver Flower Dance- Wilhelm figured it out almost immediately. He’d heard that his first encounter with Stride, when he had crossed swords with Stride’s guardian Eight-Arms, had come to be known by that name.

If Stride said he would be waiting at that place…

“Pictat. That’s the city where I fought that battle. He’ll be there, with Theresia and Carol.”

Of course, knowing Stride’s cruel mind, even this could be some sort of trap. However, he had entered the castle in person to kidnap the Sword Saint Theresia. It suggested this was the culmination of a plan he had been advancing in secret, working somewhere in the kingdom for months.

Stride and the rest of them would be waiting in Pictat, and a vicious battle would no doubt ensue.

“Wilhelm, I’m under a spell. Please, bind me hand and foot, and put a gag in my mouth so I can’t bite off my own tongue. I think those are the minimum measures necessary.”

Having delivered her message and fulfilled her role, Tishua now suggested for herself treatment that should have been unthinkable for nobility. Yet if she was under a curse, it was the right thing to do.

“Leave the rest to me, Mother. I swear I will bring back Carol and Theresia, and the child.”

“I pray for your success,” she said, clasping his hands. Her words were brief but heavy. They were the words of a woman who had married into a warrior house. Yet at the same time, Wilhelm felt her nails dig into the palms of his hands. She didn’t seem to know she was doing it, but there was a cry of a woman who had just lost her husband. This, too, she entrusted to him. “You are a man of the Astreas,” Tishua said. “You must never forget it.” “Never,” Wilhelm said.

Tishua’s voice was shaking, and tears streamed down her cheeks. Her hands had become strikingly cold. Nonetheless, her words carried an emotion that had to be respected, and Wilhelm could only nod.

He was the Sword Devil who now carried the name of Astrea, and he would obliterate the Death Wish.

5

“Even now, she sheds not a tear. A strong woman.” Stride took Theresia’s chin in his slim fingers and pulled it up. She glowered at him, at his contemptible mocking smile, emotion roiling in her sky-blue eyes.

“I have a husband, you shameless lout,” she said. “I’ll ask that you not touch me so thoughtlessly.”

“Hah. You think I would lust after a woman whose belly is already swelling? Then again, perhaps youwouldbe more exciting that way. Before motherhood, you were too despicable for me to even cast my eye upon.”

Dripping contempt, Stride released Theresia’s chin, and pain flooded her eyes.

It hurt to be denounced for having been the Sword Saint, to be asked if she could atone for all the lives she had taken. That same pain, however, was the wellspring of her personal determination. It was necessary for a proper reckoning.

Stride’s mockery, though, was another matter. He ridiculed her not for her deeds, but for who she was. The fate that attended her identity brought her unwanted trials-and not her alone, but also those around her. And that was when she felt hot tears begin to well up.

“Father…”

She could still see him, his back to her, protecting her from the silver flash of that sword. It had slashed him from shoulder to hip, and the sight of her father collapsed in a pool of blood was burned into her retinas. The sound of her always calm mother screaming echoed in her ears. It wouldn’t go away.

There was Carol, too, the one who had been forced to do this. Her weeping face and crying voice were likewise lodged in Theresia’s memory.

As mingled tears of grief and pain began to brim in Theresia’s eyes, someone gently offered her a white handkerchief.

“Um… Here,” said its owner.

Theresia, who was shackled but whose hands were still free, took it. It was frustrating, the implicit suggestion they knew she couldn’t or wouldn’t fight back, but the handkerchief was not to blame.

“I’m not going to say thank you,” Theresia informed the handkerchief’s owner.

“You, uh… Of course, you don’t have to. I didn’t, um, really do it for your thanks, anyway.”

The voice was as feeble as the buzzing of a mosquito. It came from a diminutive, barefoot girl, with long gray hair that fell in waves. It was impossible to miss how the sleeves of the ashen robe that covered her body dragged on the ground when she walked, but neither her fine appearance nor her ill-fitting outfit were what stood out most about her.

No, the most noticeable thing was that even as she spoke with Theresia, she kept both her eyes tightly shut.

The fact she was here with them showed that she was one of Stride’s faction. But it seemed there was more than one way to be involved with him. For she was Stride’s…

“Melinda, you cur. How dare you give that woman anything by your own will, without first asking my leave.”

“Oh! I-I-I’m so very sorry!”

“You are the key to all my plans. Do nothing careless but keep to one side.

That is, if you value yourself as my wife.”

Under the pressure of his words, the young lady called Melinda shrank into herself, shoulders shivering, and she nodded vigorously. The reaction didn’t appear to be one of fear or duress, however, as there was a touch of color in her cheeks. It was hard to believe, but Melinda appeared to be overjoyed to be ordered around.

This was a way of being husband and wife completely different from the one Theresia knew. To her, it looked twisted.

Theresia pulled her lips taught and forced herself to remain detached so that she wouldn’t become emotional. She decided, for this moment, to put off anger and sadness. The ability to change her focus, which she had cultivated during her time as the Sword Saint, activated, and she began to consider the situation coldly.

First, Theresia had been brought to Pictat, a city that had thrived as a center of business. Stride and his people had made no effort to hide their destination when they’d taken her from the castle. They didn’t even put a blindfold on her as they went, so Theresia knew she was on the highest floor of a spire at the very center of Pictat, which was divided into five areas.

She also knew Stride and the others were using the spire as their base of operations to enact their fell plans in the city.

And she knew of the enemy’s most crucial fighting strength…

“My lord, Raizo reports his return.”

“My lord, Shasuke reports his return.”

They emerged from Stride’s shadow, their voices indistinguishable from each other. Two shinobi, clad in black, of identical appearance, flanked Stride on either side.

“Master Raizo, Master Shasuke, fine work,” said Melinda.

“Your words do us too much honor, Mistress. However, it is I who am

Raizo.”

“And I, your humble servant Shasuke.”

“Oh! Oh! Ohhhh… I’m sorry, I’m so terribly sorry!”

As each of the shinobi raised his hand in turn and gave his name, Melinda put her head in her hands in a tizzy. Her abject apologies actually elicited an embarrassed scratch of the cheek from the shinobi. Meanwhile, without so much as turning toward them, Stride asked, “The outcome?”

“Just as you desired, my lord, we have lit the sparks all around the city.”

“We made certain to show ourselves and draw attention. People shall soon gather.”

“If all went as planned, then very good. If even you, my generals, were only useful to me in emergencies as Melinda is, it would not be enough

simply to curse my fate. Continue to do your utmost.”

“As you wish, Sire!” Raizo and Shasuke answered in unison, jerking their chins.

Judging by their ability to slip in and out of the shadows, these twins had to be the shinobi that Wilhelm had rated so highly. They and Melinda were among Stride’s faction, but there was one more…

“Raizo and Shasuke are back?” came a voice. It rolled heavy and deep, as if a mountain had spoken, and carried such menace that, at first, it was hard to believe it belonged to a person.

Theresia soon saw that the owner of the voice was every bit as menacing as the sound he made. He had blue skin, was built like a boulder, and had eight terrible arms. It was Kurgan.

Kurgan bowed his head-he was so tall that Theresia had to crane her neck to look up at him-and entered the spire’s topmost floor. He glanced at the two shinobi and then laid eyes on Theresia.

“So you are with us, Sword Saint. I’m glad to see you in good health.”

“Hesaid the same thing,” Theresia said after a moment. “And I was doing very well until I met you two.”

“Superb. A mother-to-be and full of spirit. That makes me very glad indeed.”

Feeling the warrior’s eyes on her belly, Theresia unconsciously crossed her arms in front of her stomach as if to protect her child. When Wilhelm and Kurgan had crossed swords, with Wilhelm fighting on Theresia’s behalf, Kurgan had claimed he wanted Theresia as his battle prize, although she didn’t believe he meant it. He’d claimed he wanted to produce a strong child with her, but she couldn’t predict how he might treat her now that she was actually pregnant.

But then, if it was a matter of prediction, there was a deeper question to answer.

“I don’t have the slightest idea what you people are planning,” Theresia said. “But do you really think you can do anything to our kingdom with just this handful of people?”

“Hoh? Just this handful, she says. And what makes you think this accounts for all my pawns at my disposal?”

“Even if they don’t, there can’t be many. You only have ten rings, so it can’t be more than that. In fact, it can’t be more than nine, since we broke the ring that placed the spell on my father!”

“You mean the father who lost his life sniveling before the sword of the girl he’d treated as his own daughter, who had served him for so many years?”

Theresia didn’t immediately answer, but at those words, she could no longer hold back her warrior’s aura, and the spire filled with tension. The shinobi immediately adopted fighting postures, and Melinda clutched her head and exclaimed, “Eek!”

Stride, for his part, did not so much as flinch. Kurgan sighed and glowered at him. “Stride, do not needlessly antagonize her. If the Sword Saint’s ill humor should by any chance lead to misfortune, then everything we have sought to do here would be in vain.”

“Spare me your oh-so-loyal remonstrations. The relationship between us is not one of master and servant. You would do well to remember your place.”

It showed how much confidence Stride had in the binding power of his rings that his condescension did not waver in the slightest, even in the face of Kurgan. However, confronted with Theresia’s burgeoning aura of battle, it seemed the warrior was not mistaken.

“Your assessment is not bad,” Stride began. “As you perceive, I do not have an unlimited supply of pawns. It’s not that I am without the means to gain more of them, but I am not fond of the Witch’s methods. The dead

should remain dead. That is the way of the world.”

“You’re the last person I would expect to be worried about the way of the world.”

“That ‘way’ is among the enemies that I must kill.” Stride paused. “I believe I’ve said a bit too much. It seems even I may become overexcited when my plans are so close to fruition.”

Despite his description of himself as excited, the tone of his voice never altered. Theresia’s cheeks tightened.

Stride had said in no uncertain terms that his plans were nearly ready. He had broken into Lugunica Castle and abducted Theresia, the Sword Saint, and clearly, he had no hesitation about bringing chaos to Pictat. These were bold strokes, nothing like his earlier sneaking around in the dark-which proved that his true plan was now in motion.

That was why he had put a spell on Tishua to leave a message for Wilhelm.

“But that was your biggest mistake,” Theresia said.

“Hoh. Me, mistaken? Why so?”

“Wilhelm will win. Which means you will lose.”

Theresia put this forward proudly, as the greatest miscalculation that Stride and his forces had made. Wilhelm would win. He would achieve victory, beyond any doubt. Even Theresia could see that Eight-Arms was more powerful than any ordinary warrior. His skill with the sword might even be in the same league as Wilhelm’s, but Wilhelm would win. He would be victorious.

Theresia never doubted Wilhelm’s victory, for he was her beloved Sword Devil.

“Heh…”

Theresia’s declaration provoked a laugh that was little more than an exhalation of breath. She looked over and discovered that it had come from Kurgan, who stood with four of his arms crossed in front of him, and the other four-the Devil Cleavers-stroking his swords.

Eight-Arms Kurgan himself was laughing at Theresia’s declaration of the Sword Devil’s victory.

“I find your words most amusing. The Sword Devil’s work on that day was indeed profoundly impressive. I see why you would think there was reason to have faith in him.”

“Don’t humor her, Eight-Arms. Or are you trying to make excuses knowing you may lose your rematch?” Stride said.

“By no means. No means at all,” Kurgan replied, his face and voice stern, and Stride gave an unimpressed snort. It was obvious, however, that he treated Kurgan alone differently from Melinda and the shinobi.

Without question, Eight-Arms Kurgan was Stride’s most powerful

“piece.” And he meant to play him against the kingdom’s own greatest trump card, the Sword Devil Wilhelm.

But…

“I must wonder, Sword Saint, what does account for the poor circulation of your blood,” Stride said.

“What do you mean?” Theresia asked.

“Have you given all your wits to the child in your belly? Your proclamation is that of a girl who has taken leave of her sense. For you would see at least the surface of our plans if you would only follow the thread of your own logic.”

Stride poured contempt upon Theresia’s incomprehension. She knitted her brow, trying to measure out the evil meaning of this walking disaster-and then, she realized.

This was his response to her earlier declaration. Her question of why he had started a war he could not win, a battle in which he was destined to be defeated.

It meant…

“You think you have-”

“A hope of victory? Oh, I do,” Stride said forcefully, cutting Theresia off even as the realization dawned on her. Deep in those almond-shaped eyes, she could see a dark, murky emotion, and it sent a chill down her spine. It was hardly the first time she had felt the menace and the unseemly threat from Stride’s words and plans, but this chill, the sheer dread of it, was new.

She could feel that this was not mere talk or a bluff, that Stride really meant what he said.

“My lord,” came a voice, too refined to call simply quiet.

Stride finally took his eyes off of Theresia and turned to Melinda, who had spoken his name. Her slim shoulders shrank even further, and she gestured outside the spire, toward the city. “People have begun to gather. Um, what would you like to do?”

“There is nowhatabout it. You know your job. So do it.”

Melinda’s frail body shivered at Stride’s curt order, and she nodded vigorously. At the same moment, there was a change in the aura surrounding Kurgan and the shinobi, and things began to happen.

Before things could go further, Theresia felt the handkerchief she still held in her hand. It might be mere cloth, but as the Sword Saint, she could swing it with enough force that it would become a weapon that could break bones. If she broke the right ones, it would be a simple matter of taking an opponent’s life. Even to kill Stride.

Of course, if she did that, the rest of the people in the spire would presumably tear her apart. But even still…

“I do not advise resistance,” Stride said. “The lives of your servant and your mother are in my hands.”

Theresia tensed. “Don’t mock me. Carol and my mother are women of a warrior family. As am I, of course.”

Stride held up his hand, on which two of the rings were glowing eerily, in a bid to stop Theresia. Theresia had to push aside her weak, weeping, sniveling self to answer him.

Her reply was what was truly in her heart, but it was also the worst possible outcome, one she hoped would never, ever come to pass. But she was of the family of the Sword Saints, House Astrea. She had a duty to sacrifice her own life in order to save the Dragonfriend Kingdom of Lugunica from destruction. And not only her, but Tishua, and Carol, whose House Remendes served the Astreas. So even if it meant throwing away her life, and her promise to her husband, she would take up the sword and- “…Oh.”

No sooner had she reached this decision than a tiny, strained sound escaped Theresia’s throat, and she dropped the handkerchief. The white cloth fluttered to the ground, and the walking disaster stepped on it mercilessly.

Stepped on it and ground it underfoot.

“Theresia van Astrea, Sword Saint,” said Stride, addressing her by her full name and title. “You said yourself that you would never take up the sword again. You told me that was your vow. But I do not believe what you claim with your words alone. When your life, and your husband’s life, and the future of your kingdom are in danger, you will abdicate any vow and take up the blade once again. Beloved of the Dragon, you will trust to your blessing to wield your blade with superhuman strength.” Theresia could say nothing.

“And so…”

Stride crushed the handkerchief, venomous evil entering his voice, and malice darker than the deepest darkness filling his pitch-black eyes. He raised his other hand, and one finger on it glowed. The thumb on his right hand had a gem that gave off a fell light. A matching light shone in another place- Theresia’s swelling belly.

“And so, I waited. I waited for your belly to swell, for you to become a mother, for you to breathe life into a point of weakness that you would prize over your own existence, or that of your husband, or even the future of your nation.”

“No… Stop…”

“Do not resist me. If you do, the Burgundy Thumb will steal the life from your child. This is not an empty threat.”

“Stop! Please!”

Theresia clutched her belly with her now empty hands, dropping to her knees.

She did not need any further demonstration of the power of the Ten Proud Commandments. She had seen what they could do to Carol. How easily could they pluck the life from a yet-undeveloped fetus that had not even given its first cry?

The resolution that had been so firm in her heart until a moment before, the choice she was so sure she had made, her assurances to herself that her mother and Carol would feel the same way-all of it shattered into dust.

Stride’s plan succeeded: The Sword Saint laid low her own sword.

Theresia knelt there, collapsed and unable to get up. Stride turned his back on her, going to the double window and opening it so that he could look down upon the city below.

Through the open window came a breeze carrying a faint smell of burning -and the sound of shouting voices, voices full of rage and anger. Melinda had mentioned people gathering. And the shinobis had said something about spreading sparks and making sure people saw them.

The people assembling, then, must have been Pictat’s security force. Pictat ranked among the five largest cities in the kingdom. If it had some two or three thousand guards, then no fewer than several hundred could be here around the spire. Stride had gone out of his way to make sure they were there.

He must have some reason for doing so. Some evil goal the Death Wish possessed.

“Melinda, how many can you take?” Stride asked.

“S-sire… I think my vision can encompass all of them at once,” she said meekly.

Stride gave a jerk of his chin and brusquely issued an order to his wife. “Good. Then I permit you to open your eyes.”

Melinda took a step forward and Stride gave her his place at the window. Now, it was she who looked down upon the city guards.

She could not, of course, look on them with her eyes shut-so she opened them.

Theresia’s breath caught. She recognized the spiderweb pattern in Melinda’s eyes. The Evil Eye gazed down upon the world. It began to interfere with the world, to twist the laws that should have held sway.

The walking disaster cackled at the sight and said: “Now behold, O observers who fix the paths of fate. See what the world will choose.”

CHAPTER 5: ACT V

Thousands of Fires

1

“I’ve figured out what the enemy is really after with the breaking of the pact,” Roswaal said, her face grim, as they rode in the dragon carriage toward the place of combat.

Between Stride’s personal incursion into the castle and the message from Tishua, it was easy to guess that Pictat, with all the karma it carried, would be the site of the final battle with Stride.

Knowing that the prestige of the kingdom itself would be riding on this fight, many soldiers had volunteered for the job. So it was that a unit was assembled with unusual speed, and Zergev Squadron was chosen to be the tip of the spear.

It was the most potent fighting force Lugunica could muster at that moment, eventually including not only the squadron’s Captain Wilhelm, but his vice-captain Grimm, as well as Conwood, elite troops all. In addition to Roswaal, Zergev Squadron brought with it one other person from outside the unit. Specifically…

“Listen to me, Lady Mathers. To be frank, I think heading into a battle with hardly any idea what the enemy is planning is not a wise choice.”

This opinion, delivered in heavy tones, came from Bordeaux, who stood with his thick arms crossed, looking like a log.

He had brought his halberd, almost as tall as he was, and he was clad in armor so heavy that an average person would hardly have been able to stand in it. Both were evidence he was there with Zergev Squadron as a fellow warrior.

He had spent all this time chasing Stride, always a step behind, until finally he had permitted them to get inside the royal castle itself. The Mad Dog’s patience was finally at an end. Proudly proclaiming that he had not slacked in his training simply because he had given up command of the unit, he rejoined them on a temporary basis, swearing that he would not slow them down.

Roswaal responded by raising a single finger. “To be precise, the enemy’s strategy is the abolition of the pact.
