The immense power of the Holy Dragon, abiding by the covenant, had swept away the violence that had threatened Pictat.

Stride Volakia was dead, those who had joined with him defeated, and now by the Holy Dragon’s power, even the dragon Stride had summoned had been dispatched.

“…We won,” someone said, their voice dry.

The words seemed to break the spell. People looked at each other, as if to make sure they were really alive, that the people beside them were really alive. Voices, shouts of joy, began to fill the city.

Still silent, Wilhelm, too, stood up and looked at Bordeaux, who nodded back at him. Grimm, meanwhile, jumped down off the building, demonstrating how his shattered knees had been healed.

Someone else came rushing up to them as well.

“Wilhelm!”

He turned at the shout. Coming down the road toward him at a run was a woman with her long red hair flapping behind her, a look of determination on her face. A woman he loved.

She was desperate to know that her husband was safe, as soon as she could. He could see it in her eyes. Theresia made a beeline for Wilhelm-but her foot caught on something, and she looked like she was about to fall. Wilhelm rushed forward and caught her up, holding her tight.

“Wil-” she began, but he didn’t speak. There was no need for words. Instead, he pressed his lips to hers.

She was here; she was real. He could feel her warmth against him. They were both alive, and the battle was over.

At that moment, that was all he needed.

Here ends the Vanquishing of the Fell Dragon, and the nightmare of Stride Volakia.

All that remains is the final act of the Battle Hymn of the Sword Devil.

CHAPTER 7: FINAL ACT

Hymn of Battle

1

The day of the funeral, it rained.

“I guess she would have said this was appropriate weather for a funeral,” said Conwood, looking up at the sky as he stood dressed in a formal knight’s outfit that didn’t really look right on him.

Conwood was sort of joking, but Wilhelm nodded and said, “I can practically hear it.” All the other members of the squadron within earshot made noises of agreement.

She had been like that, given to pronouncements that might rub people the wrong way. The group gazed at Conwood’s offering, a bouquet of white flowers that also looked out of place. It was a chance for them to share their memories of the deceased.

It had been ten days and change since the Vanquishing of the Fell Dragon in Pictat.

The entire kingdom had been distressed to discover a new battle beginning barely years after the end of the Demi-human War, and the involvement of both an evil and a good dragon brought back to mind the pact the kingdom had made with the Holy Dragon.

Ironically, Stride’s actions had breathed new life into the Dragonfriend Kingdom of Lugunica.

Plans were already well underway to restore the ravaged city, and people were gradually awakening to their pain and beginning to walk toward tomorrow bearing their sorrow. This state funeral was among the rituals necessary for that process.

And then…

“You must be Master Wilhelm. Mother told me so much about you.”

The words came from someone who looked like a child, barely ten, who bowed deeply to Wilhelm. He had indigo hair that went down to his shoulders and a face that promised great beauty in the years to come. His body was delicate and not yet developed, but the young man who stood there in formal clothing had multicolored eyes, one blue and one gold.

Even without that distinguishing trait, Wilhelm would have had no trouble finding other traces of Roswaal J Mathers, his comrade-in-arms who fell in that battle.

“I’m Wilhelm van Astrea, knight of the kingdom,” he said. “And I owe your mother an awful lot. More than I’ll ever be able to repay. Me and my wife both owe her everything.”

The young man didn’t answer.

Wilhelm went on, “I never got the chance to tell your mother, but I’m really grateful to her. She took on so much. I want to repay that debt. If you’re ever in trouble, if you ever need anything, just tell me.”

Wilhelm stopped there and knelt. He removed his beloved sword from his hip, scabbard and all, and laid it at his feet. Then he looked the boy square in the face and said, “Iwilllend you my strength.”

Thus he swore to Karl Mathers, the living memento of his lost friend, with the most elaborate courtesy a knight could offer.

A knight’s word was his bond. No matter what difficulty or danger, this vow would not be broken.

“I have to imagine my mother is stamping her feet somewhere for missing the sight of the Sword Devil pledging himself to her son,” Karl said with a bit of a smile, and Wilhelm was taken aback by the expression. The lonely softening of the lips, the sense that the boy was looking at something precious, looked so much like his mother.

“Lord Karl, you… No, wait. When you take over the position, you take over the name, too, right?”

“Yes, eventually. But not immediately. It will be some time before I go by the name Roswaal. Until then, please, call me Karl.”

Wilhelm hesitated. “I hear your mother’s original name was Julia. I only found out when they inscribed the tombstone.”

“It’s customary for holders of this position to keep their original names secret once they inherit it. They might divulge it to their spouse, or perhaps to a very close friend.” Karl winked, leaving only his blue eye open.

“I hear you.” Wilhelm sighed and nodded. He had known Roswaal for five years, and she had never once raised the subject of her true name with him.

She had probably never planned to tell him.

It was not, he assumed, because their relationship had been superficial.

“For me, your mother will always and forever be Roswaal.”

“I’m sure she agreed with you. No doubt that’s why she didn’t tell you.”

At that, Wilhelm had to stop and ponder an unexpected sense of fulfillment. The boy was, at most, speaking on behalf of his mother. Yet, for some reason, it felt to Wilhelm almost as if Roswaal herself were telling him.

But that would be too easy.

“I will keep your words close to my heart,” Karl said. “Somewhere, someday, I may need to borrow your strength, Master Wilhelm. When I do…”

“I will overcome any obstacle to reach you. In the vow of a knight, there is no falsehood.”

There was a beat, then Karl smiled a little. “I can feel my servant’s eyes on me, so I suppose I must excuse myself.”

Wilhelm could see Clind, the butler, standing a short distance behind Karl. He wondered how the stylish young man was taking his master’s death.

One thing had not changed: He still served the Mathers household and would do his duty. Wilhelm could see that from his bearing.

“I must say farewell for today, then. We’ll meet again, somewhere, someday.”

As the boy turned, about to walk away, Wilhelm said, “Lord Karl Mathers.”

“Yes?”

“I give you my gratitude. You and your mother. Thank you. I owe you a lot.”

They were simple words, with none of the formality or ostentation of a knight’s most sacred vow-it was the same way he’d spoken to the boy’s mother.

Karl listened, and for a second, he stood there with his eyes wide. Then he said, “Be careful not to injure yourself, and live long.”

With those oddly adult words, he walked away, and this time Wilhelm let him go.

2

The chamber was somewhat too solidly built to be called a guestroom as such.

It had a thick stone floor and walls and was protected by the effect of a Meteor. Even a dragon’s breath would need a few tries to smash it. It was guarded day and night by elite troops.

The matter of the “Death Wish” might have been resolved, but Stride had left serious questions behind. One couldn’t be certain some copycat might not appear in the future, and the room showed how seriously the possibility was being taken.

“If I didn’t know better, youngster, I’d say this room looks less like it’s meant to keep intruders out, and more like it’s meant to keep someone in.” “Mmm. I’m rather surprised to hear you say that, honored advisor.”

Miklotov was in the ironclad room, giving a tour to a very important visitor of the King’s.

Even in his youth, Miklotov had been esteemed a sage, and His Majesty Jionis thought highly of him. Others claimed he had cunning beyond his years or that he was a man whose wits were not to be taken lightly.

That reputation, however, was ultimately nothing more than flattery. Miklotov could not help thinking this when he looked upon the person who had crammed their massive frame into the confines of an all-too-small chair, looking quite bored. This was a member of the Giant race, whose existence could not be revealed, and who showed open hostility for Miklotov-no, for all the humans of the kingdom. He was also the person who King Jionis had welcomed as an advisor. It was this decision that confirmed for Miklotov that he paled in comparison to his king.

“‘Advisor,’ heh!” the giant said. “Enough nonsense. You must be out of your mind to be alone in a room with me.”

“You could harm me, but then what? You’re the one who can’t afford to be reckless with me.”

“Is that sarcasm? Being reckless when Isupposedlycouldn’t be is what brought me here. Am I wrong?”

“If we define recklessness as the product of being cornered, then perhaps you are. You had other plans, other options. That’s what saved you this time.” Miklotov dropped his eyes and let a note of gratitude enter his voice. That earned a noisy, none-too-pleased click of the tongue from the giant. “I must ask why,” Miklotov went on. “I thought you hated the kingdom, hated humans. Why are you helping us?”

“Not hated.Hate.I hate all of you even now. A fire burns in my heart to pull every last one of you up by the roots, and it hasn’t been extinguished once in all my life.”

There was nothing Miklotov could say to that.

“However, I won’t stoop toyourmethods. If I did, my kin would truly have died in vain. That is not what I wish. That’s all there is to it.”

This giant was willing to suppress the undying hatred that scorched him from within.

It occurred to Miklotov that this might make the giant even more terrible than when he had been openly hostile, but he was careful not to let the thought show on his face as he stood up from his chair. He didn’t want to let this go on too long and incur even more displeasure. He had conveyed his thanks and inquired about the reason for the giant’s cooperation. All that was left was…

“Not that I’m trying to butter you up, but is there anything you need? Without your advice, things would have gotten even worse than they did. I should like to accommodate you, so far as I’m able.”

“AnythingIneed,” the giant murmured, and the hatred in the words was unmistakable. It sent a shiver down Miklotov’s spine. As he looked on, wideeyed, the giant shrugged and began to smile. “You ask if there is anything Ineed, youngster! If there is anything, then I have needed it from the day of my birth. For my people, the demi-humans, have not been free even once!” Miklotov had no answer to this.

“Look now at your Kingdom of Lugunica. I have thrown nothing at you yet. But one day, the chains that bind me shall be broken, and then you shall

know-know which of us truly deserves to be bound.”

Miklotov was ashamed to have carelessly burned himself on a wound unhealed, on a flame undying. But despite the shame, he asked the giant who lived with that wound and that fire, “May I convey your words verbatim to His Majesty Jionis?”

The giant offered no answer-but, to Miklotov, his silence sounded like a wish.

Would he one day get an answer, even as he was hated, even as he was mocked, even as he was scorched by the flame of rage?

“Hmm… I know I asked for this job, but I think I still got the short end of the stick,” Miklotov muttered, quietly enough that no one would hear, just before he left the room-and then, he gave a brief sigh.

3

“To think, I and I alone live to endure the shame of survival.”

Eight-Arms Kurgan stood at the place that had become his refuge, the fortress marking the border skirmishes between the Volakian Empire and the kingdom, and let out a long sigh as he contemplated his regret and humiliation.

It was perhaps understandable, insofar as his survival was totally unexpected.

Devastated by his battle with Wilhelm, the defeated Kurgan should have lost his life then and there. Except that before he expired, the healing power of the Holy Dragon’s blessing had intervened.

Hence, out of all the members of Stride’s faction, only he-the warrior- had survived. It was deeply ironic.

And yet, there was no sign in his voice that he cursed his destiny. If anything, his eyes looked like he had gone from a man possessed to a man at peace. He seemed to calmly accept the outcome.

Almost as if defeat had beentheirtruest wish.

“You don’t look too broken up about it. Don’t tell me you’re going to say you decided to quit while you were ahead,” said a voice.

“Stride was never very interested in winning or losing,” Kurgan replied. “In that sense, indeed, he was always losing. But at the bitter end, my son accomplished his fondest wish. That is enough.”

“His fondest wish…?”

“He should have died as nothing and nobody, but he shifted the very thinking of the world. And he did not die alone.”

Even at that, Wilhelm couldn’t grasp even a shred of what Stride really thought or felt. Maybe Kurgan didn’t even know, in reality. Perhaps no one could see into that man’s heart.

He had run roughshod over the world from sheer selfishness, and at the end, he had invited his own destruction and then died.

He had lived, then died. It was simply that there were those who sought to love him, though they could not understand him.

“Be well, Sword Devil.”

It was the last time the Sword Devil and Eight-Arms would speak together.

“What’s gonna happen to him when he gets back to the Empire?” Wilhelm asked Bordeaux, who stood beside him on the roof of the fortress, watching the dragon carriage with Kurgan aboard vanish into the distance. The handoff had gone without a hitch.

Bordeaux, who had been responsible for transporting Eight-Arms, glared out over the Empire’s territory with his craggy face. “The Empire’s going to owe the kingdom alotafter this. A member of their imperial family fled his own country, attempted to overthrow another one, and caused a lot of damage and injuries.Frictiondoesn’t begin to cover what there’ll be between us.”

“That’sifthe Empire even acknowledges Stride’s existence.”

“Yeah, exactly. And I guarantee you they won’t. It’ll probably come down to looking for a compromise where the kingdom doesn’t, either.” “Freakin’ sucks. Is that politics?” Wilhelm spat.

“You haven’t seen the half of it. Believe me, I’m no better than you are at problems we can’t solve by beating them up,” Bordeaux said with a grim smile.

He was right: It was a world that neither he nor Wilhelm found very palatable. But unlike Wilhelm, Bordeaux had no choice but to try himself on that less than ideal battlefield.

“This battle… It was the last one for me,” Bordeaux said.

Wilhelm didn’t respond.

“In my last foray onto the field, I got to fight alongside you and Grimm, the guys in the squadron… Even Pivot. I got away with my life, and I even witnessed the Holy Dragon with my own eyes. I don’t think there could be a better conclusion to a soldier’s career.”

Seeing Bordeaux now, no one could claim that he’d retired out of fear or an excess of philosophy. Wilhelm wondered what he could say instead, but unable to find the words, he looked to the sky.

The clouds scudded by, urged on by the wind, slowly changing their shapes. Wilhelm’s heart was rent by the knowledge that time would march on, unceasing, and there was nothing that would not change.

“Wilhelm,” said Bordeaux. Wilhelm didn’t answer. “I hope you-and your heart-won’t ever change.”

It was uncharacteristically sentimental for Bordeaux, but Wilhelm couldn’t just laugh it off. Instead, the Sword Devil gazed far into the blue sky and silently vowed to hold those words close forever.

4

“Carol and I are going to be family.”

Grimm wrote the admission shyly, after the alcohol had been flowing for a while.

They were in their usual tavern, just at the moment when day was giving way to twilight. The establishment was suffused with the odor of alcohol and the jabbering of voices, the perfect chance to share some joyous news.

“I’m not sure what to do here. Do I congratulate you, or ask what the hell took you so long?”

“Knowing you? Both, I assume.”

“Yeah? Yeah… Congrats. So, what the hell took you so long?”

The two of them raised their mugs and clinked them together in celebration.

“Going to be a family” was very Grimm-like language, but what it meant was that he had finally proposed.

Many of those who had watched his and Carol’s love story develop had been getting impatient for exactly this moment, but only with recent events had the hurdle that had been holding them back-the fact that Carol was a noble and Grimm a commoner-been removed.

“You did more than anyone else in that battle, after all!”

Indeed, Grimm had not only defeated the enemy leader, Stride Volakia,

but had rescued the captured Sword Saint and even done battle with a rampaging dragon. It sufficed to say, he had contributed quite a lot. In recognition of his actions, he had been officially awarded the rank of knight, and-although the title would not be hereditary-the status of nobility. His class difference with Carol vanished.

“So when’s the wedding?”

“Not too far off. I’m going to talk to Carol about it.”

“You’ll be inviting me, right?”

“I’m amazed you even think you have to ask!”

As they traded banter, one aloud and one by writing, Wilhelm and Grimm continued with their drinking. This was not generally a place to drink the most exquisite alcohol-but tonight was different, in no small part because of the very special news Grimm had brought. But his marriage wasn’t the only unique thing he had to report.

Wilhelm knew Grimm had something else important to say. So he asked:

“You’re quitting?”

“Yeah. I’m leaving the knights,” Grimm wrote on his notepad. It wasn’t the most natural way to hold a conversation, but the two of them had gotten used to this modest impediment. For Wilhelm, Grimm writing on the paper with his stylus was a familiar sight. Now the young man continued: “I’ve made up my mind. Once Carol and I are married, I’m retiring from the unit.” “Why’s that?” Wilhelm asked.

“I want to be close to Carol. Being with her is what gives my life meaning.”

There was passion in the movements of his pen, passion in the shapes of his letters. Grimm was racing forward, and all Wilhelm could do was give him a friendly shrug.

When he thought back, he saw that Grimm had been defying his expectations ever since the first time they’d met. Grimm had joined the army with nothing but an ill-defined hope of becoming a hero; the connection between himself and Wilhelm had been nothing more than that they had both survived their first battle. After that, he was snapped up by Bordeaux’s Zergev Squadron, took the field as a member of one of the most elite units in the royal army, and somewhere along the line, he’d become Wilhelm’s closest friend.

He was a man who was never taken in by appearances. He couldn’t be fooled that way. So it was very Grimm-like to earn a claim as one of the kingdom’s most distinguished knights, take his bride for his prize, and then retire.

And to do it so he could continue to be with the woman he loved-that was a master stroke.

“You’re the biggest idiot I’ve ever met,” Wilhelm said.

“You’re the last person I want to hear that from.”

“Oh, you’ll hear it! You big coward, can’t even draw your sword.”

Grimm’s eyes went wide at this unexpected provocation. Wilhelm found the look of shock immensely gratifying.

Did Grimm remember? That when he and Wilhelm had first met…

“I’ll screw up my courage and plant my shield right here,” Grimm replied.

Back then, it had been a challenge that Grimm, unable to find the meaning of battle, couldn’t answer. Wilhelm, disgusted by his failure to say anything, had ridiculed him as a coward.

Now, more than seven years later, Grimm had a response.

It was the culmination of his and Wilhelm’s relationship.

“So you quit. Then what? Gonna take over the family business?”

“My little brother already did that. Besides, don’t act like you don’t have a stake in this, ‘Master.’”

“Hey, now,” Wilhelm said, frowning as he followed the last of Grimm’s words and his flowing, swift writing.

Even Wilhelm wasn’t dense enough to miss the point of that nickname. “You can’t mean…”

“Lady Tishua has already agreed.”

“WhatIheard from ‘Mother’ was that I’m the next head of the house,” Wilhelm said.

“And Carol and I will be trusting you to take good care of us, Master.”

Wilhelm’s lips twisted to find himself so easily outmaneuvered, and he took a long swig of alcohol. It was distressingly delicious. Never mind that he was essentially already ignoring the post he had supposedly inherited from Veltol as head of the house.

He wiped away a dribble of wine from his lips, and with it the odd feeling in his heart, part regret and part joy.

“And here I thought I’d finally seen the last of you!” he said.

At Wilhelm’s evident look of defeat, Grimm laughed, as it were, without a sound.

5

“Grimm and I are…going to be together.”

At this news, almost too wonderful, Theresia laughed in spite of herself as she leaned against the cradle.

She adored Carol, who had prefaced her announcement by saying with a very serious look that she had something extremely important to tell Theresia, then had taken forever to get the words out, and who was still blushing furiously.

“L-Lady Theresia?” Carol said. “How can you laugh?”

“How can I not, Carol? You came in here looking like you were going to challenge me to a duel. I had no idea what you were about to say… You’re just too adorable.”

“D-don’t tease me, milady…” She slumped slightly, picking at her sleeve. Theresia felt another smile come over her face. Carol could no longer remotely hide the fact of her love, and Theresia was sure that Grimm’s citation as a knight was behind it. Now that the difference in status between them was gone, their marriage had to be close behind.

“So you’ll be Carol Fauzen? Or will he be Grimm Remendes?”

Carol hesitated. “I… I mean to take his name, Fauzen. My younger brother will inherent the Remendes name. My name… My name deserves to be struck from the Remendes line, anyway.”

“Carol, don’t-”

“It’s necessary, Lady Theresia. No matter how forbearing you and Lady

Tishua may be toward me. I can still feel that day in my own hands.”

Here Carol demonstrated her long-standing stubbornness. She did not intend to yield on this point.

Carol looked so serious and appeared to have made up her mind, so Theresia had neither the right nor the courage to belittle that resolution. One thing, however, she wished to know.

“If you depart the family of the ‘scabbard’…will you leave my side, as well?”

“You may be Lady Theresia, Lady Theresia, but even you can’t say such a thing without making me mad!”

Deeply relieved to hear that, Theresia stuck out her tongue apologetically, making no effort to conceal her silliness. She wanted to respect Carol’s decision, and would. But they had always been together, and Theresia wanted them to stay together always. That was her true wish.

And to help it come true…

“Are you sure about Grimm? Is that your happiness, Carol?”

“Lady Theresia…”

“I want you to find all the joy you possibly can in this world, Carol-so I want you to be with the person who makes you happiest. Are you sure it’s Grimm? Is he your destined one? Is he your Wilhelm?” Tears beaded in her eyes as she spoke.

Carol embraced her gently and stroked her back to comfort her. “I won’t go so far as to say you’re embarrassing yourself, Lady Theresia, but I think you’re confused.” She was like an older sister comforting her younger sibling, or a mother her child, and indeed Theresia was taken by a sense of security.

At the same time, she felt slightly pathetic. Carol must be far more nervous than she was.

Even so, Carol smiled as she embraced Theresia. “It’s all right, milady. Your Carol is as happy as she can be. Grimm is my destined one… Or, no, he isn’t. Destiny didn’t choose him. I did.”

“Yes… I trust your choice much more than destiny’s.” Theresia wiped her tears away-but then, she pursed her lips. “But that’s not fair, Carol. When

you put it that way, there’s nothing I can say to you.”

“No, I guess there’s not. Now you can countmeamong those who have defeated the Sword Saint.”

“I think I will. You’re right… I must be the weakest and most pathetic of my line. A failure of a Sword Saint who just keeps losing battles. I hope you’ll keep defeating me in the future.”

“Just you wait and see. Your Carol will be by your side always, Lady Theresia.”

Carol released Theresia and placed her hand gently over the other woman’s, then the two of them gazed into each other’s eyes.

Theresia’s servant was so sure and lovely as to make her proud, and she had grown strong. In her eyes, in her face, Theresia could see of the reasons for her transformation.

It wasn’t that they had been able to regain everything. There were many things that would never come back. But even so, Theresia was grateful that she and Carol could smile at each other, and spend their time together.

She offered up a prayer of thanks to all those who had given everything to make it possible.

6

So even the long-winding Battle Hymn of the Sword Devil finds its way to a conclusion.

“Carol and Grimm looked so happy, didn’t they?”

“Yeah, true.”

“When I see Carol like that, I can’t even be sad that she’s been taken from me.”

“Yeah, true.”

“Wilhelm, are you sad that Carol took Grimm from you?”

“Yeah, true.”

“Argh! You’re not even listening to me!” Theresia burst out. She and Wilhelm were in the bedchamber of the Astrea mansion, where Wilhelm was giving noncommittal answers to her remarks.

Theresia puffed out her cheeks in annoyance, but Wilhelm looked like he hardly noticed. He was completely taken with something else-pressing his ear to Theresia’s belly and listening to the child within.

“Quiet, Theresia,” he said. “I can’t hear the baby crying.”

“It’s not crying! And anyway, you should be listening to what your dear, cherished, beloved wife is saying!”

“I’ll listen later.”

“How can you say that? I can’t believe you!”

Theresia might have complained about her husband’s attitude, but at the same time, she was thrilled to see Wilhelm, who had been afraid even to touch her, actively engaging with the baby.

Of course, only insofar as he was careful to giveherher fair share of love and attention as well!

“And?” Theresia asked. “Did the baby say anything to you while you were so busy neglecting your wife?”

“Nah, not a word. I can’t hear anything. I’m starting to wonder if maybe it’s dead.”

“Don’t say such awful things! It’s alive! There-I just felt it move!”

“You didn’t-whoa! It really did!”

Wilhelm was about to argue with the child’s mother, but the baby began kicking in the womb. Theresia put Wilhelm’s hand on her belly right where the child was, so he could feel it-and he looked happy, indeed, blissful.

How long had it been since they had spent such an innocuous, quiet, irreplaceable moment together? Theresia almost felt that it was a tremendous luxury, that she asked for far too much indulgence.

“Then again, we’re newlyweds and apprentice parents. We deserve a little luxury now and then.”

“Luxury? It’s just you and me together. It’s everything before this that was out of whack.”

“Th-there you go again, saying stuff like that so readily. It’s not fair! You’ll be able to be with me today, won’t you?” Theresia asked, sitting squarely on the bed and looking up at him.

Wilhelm raised an eyebrow and nodded gently. “Yeah. Everything’s finally mopped up… Although, I’m sure it’ll be busy again before long.”

“The military is reorganizing, isn’t it? Zergev Squadron is going to be merged with another unit and get bigger… And I even heard they were going to make it the royal guard unit.”

The source of this little tidbit was Tishua, who ran in the social circles of high nobility.

Some said that Tishua’s socializing was just a way of forgetting her pain, but Tishua herself saw things differently. She was accumulating stories to tell. Someday, when she was reunited with her beloved husband Veltol, she would have plenty of tales to surprise him with.

“It’s solikeher,” Wilhelm said. “But the whole royal guard thing, I’m not so sure about it.”

“Why not? I think it’s wonderful that they’re recognizing all that you’ve done.”

“Yeah, but the royal guards don’t get to leave the capital very often. And they probably don’t get as many chances to actually fight. On the other hand, the trade-off is that I get that much more time to be with you.” Theresia was speechless for a moment.

“That’s why I’m conflicted. I guess the gratitude is stronger, though.”

Theresia could see on Wilhelm’s face that he was genuinely worried about this. To her, he was as precious as he was exasperating.

There were not many things in Wilhelm’s heart that could compare with the sword. She could argue with him, sure, but she was also pondering: As the wife of the Sword Devil, what was the right answer?

“Well, the decision is made, so you’ll just have to lean toward the ‘me’ side of those feelings. Even if it takes some time.”

“Anyway, we’ve got a few days together. Just leave everything to me.

Food, bath, everything.”

“I’ll let you take care of the food, but you arenothelping me bathe.”

“Hey, I’m trying to be considerate, here. Partly, at least.”

“C-considerateness shouldn’t bepartof it! I’m… I’m pregnant, you know!”

“Yeah, but you’re still Theres-”

“Okay! Nope! That’s enough! No more! Discussion over! Come here, give me a hug-nice and tight-and that’s it for now!”

With her face beet red, Theresia reached out and pulled Wilhelm down next to her, into a hug. Her frantic tone made it all too obvious what she really wanted, but Wilhelm gave a wry smile and indulged her.

Oh, this is something about him I just can’t stand!She was always finding new ways to fall in love with him and always would.

Once again Theresia found herself wondering whether it was acceptable for her to live her life feeling like she was on cloud nine like this.

Surely you needn’t worry about that.

Every time the question arose, she thought she could hear Veltol’s voice. If he were there at that moment, it was exactly what he would’ve said, his beloved daughter was certain of it.

So she found her feelings slipping out before she could stop them: “You know… I really wanted Dad to give our child its name.”

That had been Theresia’s final wish there in the dream evoked by the Evil Eye, when she had met the family she had never expected to see again.

Veltol had told her with a smile that he wanted to ponder it-but then what?

“Theresia…” Wilhelm, still embracing his wife, whispered when he heard that. “There’s something I haven’t told you.”

“Wha?”

He let go of her and took out something he had tucked into his shirt. “Your mother gave me this. She said to give it to you when the time was right.”

He handed her a wrinkled note. Theresia took it hesitantly, puzzled by the fact that it was slightly torn, folded in every direction, and the edges dark. It almost looked like a bloodstain to her.

With trembling fingers, she unfolded the piece of paper. “Oh,” she said in a very small voice.

“I guess your dad put a lot of thought into it.” Theresia took a small, trembling breath.

The note was a list-a list of innumerable names. Some of them had approving circles next to them, or had been crossed out. There was evidence of much contemplation, inspiration, rejection-the signs of a brutal battle.

There was one name, just one, circled at the bottom of the list.

“Heinkel,” she said, letting the word take shape, feeling it form on her tongue.

Heinkel: The sound of it was so intensely sweet that it tore at Theresia’s heartstrings.

“Looks like he only thought about boys’ names,” Wilhelm said. “But I guess that’s just like him. Once he had a thought in his head, he sort of got tunnel vision.”

“Yes, he really-yes. You’re right, it’s all…every bit of it, is just like

Dad…”

She’d never asked him directly to do this. She was sure that he’d gotten the bit between his teeth on his own, puzzling and puzzling until finally, after endless pondering, he had come up with this name.

“He kept his promise…” Theresia murmured.

The promise he should never have been able to keep, the one she had made with him after his death and had never believed would come true. Somehow, Veltol had made good on it. He had kept his last promise to his precious daughter.

He was a man who could do that. A man whowoulddo that. From the bottom of her heart, Theresia was proud of him.

“Heinkel, Heinkel, Heinkel,” Wilhelm said, trying out his child’s name. He’d laughed about how Veltol hadn’t considered the possibility that it wouldn’t be a boy, but nonetheless, he spoke the name.

Heinkel… That’s your name.

The name Heinkel’s grandfather, kinder and braver than any man, had given him.

The name Wilhelm would speak so many times in the future, once the child was born, filling it with love every time.

“You haven’t had it easy even before you were born, and I’m sure things won’t be any easier after you arrive, but Wilhelm and I will be here with you. Your father and your mother love you very much,” Theresia said.

“Plus, it looks like we’ll be stuck with Grimm and Carol for a long time yet. One thing I can promise you, once you get here, you won’t be bored,” Wilhelm added.

Theresia gazed at Wilhelm, her beloved husband reflected in her blue eyes, and smiled, as delicate and as lovely as a blooming flower.

“I love you very much, Wilhelm… What about you?”

Wilhelm gazed back at the wife he adored and said, “Come on. You know.”

Some things never changed-and that was exactly what she loved about him.

So this story comes to an end.

A story of a man and woman subjected to the malicious plans of the Death Wish, of love tested and love rising to the test.

There were many partings, and what was gained and what was lost did not weigh equally in the scales, yet the balance of bliss and misfortune are not for onlookers to judge.

For now, you need only listen and be enthralled by this song of the battlefield.

It is the song of a devil of the sword, scorched by love. A devil too inept to express himself except on the field of battle. And when all was said and done, what did he wish for?

May the future he dreamed of be there, at the close of this Battle Hymn of the Sword Devil.

<END>

AFTERWORD

TheLove Ballad of the Sword Devilseries is finished! Hey, it’s your author, Tappei Nagatsuki, here! A gray-colored cat!

As the “Ex” in the title implies, this series is a spinoff ofRe:ZERO Starting Life in Another World-, and it’s also the much-belated final installment in theLove Ballad of the Sword Devilseries that we first saw inEx2 and 3.

This tale began with a love song, proceeded through a love ballad, and finally arrived at a battle hymn. It’s been years since I wrote those earlier novels, and since then I’ve really done a deep dive into the Volakian Empire, which is so intimately related to so many of these characters, so there was a lot of adding and revising to be done before this book could be published!

My earlier writing represented the best I could do at the time, and pounding it into better narrative and structural shape was a difficult battle, but one worth fighting. Once again in this case, I hope I was able to convey the characters’ ways of living, fighting, and being without changing the overall nuances of the story.

As you may have guessed from the timing of this release, this book is bursting with characters who are very important to the currently airing third season of the anime, and for that matter with very important information for the history of the world ofRe:ZEROin general. If you’re just starting out to enjoy this world, this book is packed with information I would love for you to know! Thanks so much for being here!

All right, since evenExbooks have their page limits, it’s time to move on to the special thanks!

To my editor, I, I know you agonized for years about when to turnThe Battle Hymn of the Sword Devilinto a book, but I think you chose the best possible moment. Thank you for once again giving everything to this book!

To my illustrator, Otsuka, thank you again for your always wonderful work! I wrote this story a long time ago, and I’ve been really looking forward to seeing Melinda and the shinobi twins appear in the illustrations. The design

of Melinda’s eyes is genius!

To my designer, Kusano, my utmost gratitude for another phenomenal design on a cover bursting with characters! I think it’s the perfect way to conclude Wilhelm and Theresia’s story.

To Haruno Atori and Yu Aizawa, who are handling the manga version of Arc Four, thank you so much for your endless help expanding the world of this story!

My thanks also go out to the editorial division at MF Bunko J, the proofreaders and booksellers, the salespeople, and everyone else who has helped bring this book to life!

Above all, I have my greatest gratitude for the readers who have stuck with theLove Song of the Sword Devilseries all the way to the end, cheering me on the whole time! It’s thanks to you that I’ve been able to have so much fun writing this tale!

All right, see you next time! May we meet again amid the turbulent developments of the main series!

November 2024

(Taking plenty of energy from the new season of the anime.)

“Bah. Don’t you forget this.”

“Woohooo! Clap, clap, clap!”

“First things first. The next expected publication is volume 40 of the main series, planned for next March.”

“Forty! G-gosh, this story is really elaborate, isn’t it? It sounds like the Holy Dragon, who just helped us, will be a part of it. I wonder what’s going to happen!”

“Hee-hee, I thought so. That’s my Wilhelm. This child’s…I mean, Heinkel’s father!”
